24d5c4737a2e69cd	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What is Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)?	Coronary Artery Disease, also known as ischemic heart disease, is a condition where plaque builds up in the coronary arteries, restricting blood flow to the heart. It is the leading cause of death globally, accounting for 16% of total deaths.	CAD definition epidemiology subject:cardiology
24f0666135d1785c	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What are the global mortality statistics for cardiovascular disease?	According to WHO 2019 statistics, cardiovascular disease is the commonest cause of mortality accounting for 18.5 million deaths annually, far exceeding cancer (10 million) and respiratory diseases (under 4 million).	epidemiology mortality global-health subject:cardiology
6dd3e8f3d2499c0c	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What is the double burden of disease in South Africa and low/middle-income countries?	The double burden refers to the simultaneous high prevalence of both infectious diseases (tuberculosis, AIDS) and non-communicable diseases (lifestyle-related conditions like CAD, hypertension, diabetes).	epidemiology public-health disease-burden subject:cardiology
df45cad8b30a1d61	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What is the most lethal manifestation of Coronary Artery Disease?	Myocardial infarction (heart attack) is the most lethal and dangerous manifestation of CAD. It is a medical emergency caused by complete or near-complete blockage of a coronary artery.	CAD complications myocardial-infarction subject:cardiology
afb3d400e878cbdd	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What is the key message about CAD prevention and modification?	CAD is a disease of lifestyle that can be modified through choices about diet and physical activity. It can also be managed with pharmacological therapies and mechanical interventions (angioplasty, stenting, bypass surgery). Leading a judicious lifestyle can delay or prevent CAD onset.	CAD prevention management subject:cardiology
21a30b799e85d200	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What are the main clinical manifestations of CAD?	The main manifestations are: (1) Stable/Chronic coronary syndrome (stable angina), (2) Acute coronary syndrome including ST-elevation MI and non-ST elevation MI, (3) Unstable angina, (4) Silent ischemia, and (5) Sudden cardiac death.	CAD clinical-manifestations presentation subject:cardiology
9f7169b16f6612c1	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	Why is CAD now called Chronic Coronary Syndrome?	CAD is called chronic coronary syndrome because the disease starts early (in teen years or even earlier) but remains clinically silent for many years. Evidence of disease appears as fatty streaks in arteries from childhood, becoming manifest typically after age 35, most commonly between ages 50-80.	CAD pathophysiology disease-progression subject:cardiology
f077bfe46b10983b	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What is the difference between ST-elevation MI (STEMI) and non-ST elevation MI?	In STEMI, the thrombus causes total occlusion of the coronary vessel, making it a medical emergency with rapid myocardial death. In non-STEMI, the thrombus is not completely occlusive, allowing some blood flow; it is less time-dependent but still requires urgent treatment.	acute-coronary-syndrome myocardial-infarction STEMI subject:cardiology
891dac470a8f61d4	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What are non-modifiable risk factors for CAD?	Non-modifiable risk factors are: (1) Age - prevalence increases after age 35; (2) Male gender - higher lifetime risk; (3) Family history of early CAD (father/brother <55 years or mother/sister <65 years); (4) Certain ethnicities including Indian, Afrikaans, Ashkenazi Jewish, and Scottish populations.	risk-factors non-modifiable epidemiology subject:cardiology
e3b20df1a09b27a1	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What are the lifetime risks for CAD development by gender after age 40?	Men have a 49% lifetime risk of developing CAD after age 40 years, while women have a 32% lifetime risk. After menopause, the prevalence becomes equivalent between genders.	risk-factors gender epidemiology subject:cardiology
3198b300b9501713	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What is the relationship between family history and CAD risk?	Family history of CAD below age 50 years indicates increased risk. Specifically: if father or brother diagnosed before 55 years, or mother or sister diagnosed before 65 years of age, these individuals are at additional risk.	risk-factors family-history genetics subject:cardiology
df10e1d8fda1607a	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What is the most prevalent modifiable risk factor for CAD?	Hypertension is the most prevalent modifiable risk factor, with approximately one out of every three individuals globally having hypertension.	risk-factors modifiable hypertension subject:cardiology
eec8f074bcb7d602	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	How does diabetes affect CAD risk and outcomes?	Diabetes mellitus (both Type 1 and Type 2) increases the rate of CAD by more than 2.5 times compared to non-diabetics. Although hypertension and hyperlipidemia are more common, diabetes is a more severe condition, leading to greater disease burden and poorer outcomes in CAD patients.	risk-factors diabetes CAD-outcomes subject:cardiology
8646ded1b911752f	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What is the CAD risk increase from secondhand smoke exposure?	Non-smokers exposed to secondhand smoking have a 25-30% increased risk of CAD compared to those never exposed to cigarette smoke. This is why smoking is banned in public areas - not to protect smokers, but to protect non-smokers.	risk-factors smoking secondhand-smoke subject:cardiology
6b1e6a9d0cc514ae	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What dietary factors increase CAD risk?	Dietary risk factors include: (1) Added sugars in soft drinks and sweetened beverages - associated with >20% higher MI risk; (2) Saturated and trans fats; (3) Excess red meat and processed meats (sausages, cured meats); (4) Overall poor diet quality.	risk-factors diet nutrition subject:cardiology
9e31ffd6af394aac	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What is the relationship between obesity and CAD?	Obesity is an independent risk factor for CAD and also increases the risk of developing other risk factors like hypertension, hyperlipidemia, and Type 2 diabetes. This effect is particularly pronounced with abdominal obesity.	risk-factors obesity metabolic subject:cardiology
013ee4525d42addc	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What are less recognized risk factors for CAD?	Less recognized risk factors include: (1) Non-alcoholic fatty liver disease (NAFLD) - now the commonest cause of cirrhosis; (2) Chronic kidney disease - increases CAD incidence and mortality; (3) Inflammatory conditions (lupus, rheumatoid arthritis, inflammatory bowel disease); (4) HIV infection.	risk-factors comorbidities inflammatory subject:cardiology
fb6fc0f5e4e93028	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What is the Framingham Risk Score and what are its components?	The Framingham Risk Score is one of the oldest risk assessment tools. It considers: age, total cholesterol, HDL cholesterol, systolic blood pressure (treated or untreated), and smoking status. It provides a 10-year likelihood of developing cardiovascular disease including CAD, stroke, and peripheral artery disease.	risk-assessment Framingham tools subject:cardiology
6a5f5991e678e96e	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	Why is the Framingham Risk Score not useful in certain populations?	The Framingham Risk Score is not useful in diabetics or those with familial hyperlipidemia because these individuals are already at higher risk simply by virtue of having these conditions. Risk scoring is unnecessary when baseline risk is already elevated.	risk-assessment limitations diabetes subject:cardiology
15d62fc01f190b9f	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What are the Framingham Risk Score categories and their implications?	Risk score categories: (1) Score <3 = Low risk; (2) Score 3-15 = Moderate risk; (3) Score >15, especially >30 = High risk requiring pharmacotherapy in addition to lifestyle measures. Scores >30 require aggressive risk factor control to minimize MI and death risk.	risk-assessment stratification management subject:cardiology
c53b8f56342b6939	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	Who are considered very high-risk for cardiovascular events without needing risk scoring?	Individuals at very high risk who do NOT require risk scoring include: (1) Those with established atherosclerotic disease in any vascular territory; (2) Type 2 diabetes with other risk factors; (3) Type 1 diabetes with proteinuria; (4) Familial/genetic hyperlipidemia; (5) Severe chronic kidney disease (stages 4-5); (6) Asymptomatic individuals with evidence of atherosclerotic plaques on imaging.	risk-assessment high-risk very-high-risk subject:cardiology
256d61c513b5ff94	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What is the significance of atherosclerotic disease in multiple vascular territories?	If atherosclerotic disease is established in one vascular territory (coronary, cerebrovascular, peripheral arterial), the likelihood of having it in other vascular beds is high. This is why individuals with established atherosclerosis in any location automatically have very high overall cardiovascular risk.	atherosclerosis disease-progression risk-stratification subject:cardiology
2f2d486831358803	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What defines familial/genetic hyperlipidemia risk?	Severe familial hyperlipidemia automatically places individuals at high CAD risk. It is defined by: total cholesterol >7.5 mmol/L or LDL cholesterol >5 mmol/L (LDL is 'bad cholesterol'). This population is particularly common among people of Dutch descent in South Africa, with approximately 300,000 severely affected individuals.	hyperlipidemia genetics familial subject:cardiology
ede7fa5e22fe5eb7	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What is the pathophysiology of chronic coronary syndrome?	In chronic coronary syndrome, a plaque composed of lipid, inflammatory cells, and smooth muscle cells causes >50-70% lumen stenosis in a coronary artery. This narrowing limits blood flow downstream, manifesting as ischemic chest pain (angina) only when the vessel is narrowed >70%.	pathophysiology chronic-syndrome stenosis subject:cardiology
b8628a713ce65a8a	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What is the pathophysiology of acute coronary syndrome in STEMI?	In STEMI, a plaque ruptures and a large thrombus forms on the rupture site, causing complete occlusion of the coronary vessel. This is a true emergency because there is no blood flow downstream and myocytes rapidly begin to die (myocardial infarction).	pathophysiology acute-syndrome STEMI thrombus subject:cardiology
baf20a09394cfbc2	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What is the pathophysiology of acute coronary syndrome in non-STEMI?	In non-STEMI, a long-standing plaque fissures or ruptures and a thrombus forms, but the thrombus is not completely occlusive. Stenosis increases from 50-70% to ~90%, but some blood flow remains. Infarction occurs when bits of the clot break off and block smaller downstream vessels.	pathophysiology acute-syndrome non-STEMI subject:cardiology
7b6b569c12ae220e	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What is stable angina and how is it defined?	Stable angina is now called Chronic Coronary Syndrome. It is defined as angina where the symptom characteristics have remained unchanged for the preceding 60 days. This distinction is important to differentiate it from unstable angina, where symptoms are changing or new.	stable-angina chronic-syndrome definition subject:cardiology
cf97b28686f0f99f	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	How is angina diagnosed?	Angina is diagnosed by history and history alone. The diagnosis is made based on the patient's description of symptoms, not on any investigation or test. ECG, stress testing, or imaging can support the diagnosis but are not required for diagnosis.	diagnosis angina clinical subject:cardiology
15a4a656ff373d2d	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What are the three criteria for typical angina?	Typical angina must satisfy all three criteria: (1) Chest discomfort described as constriction in the front of chest, radiating to neck, jaw, shoulder, or arm(s); (2) Provoked by physical exertion or emotional stress; (3) Rapidly relieved by rest or nitrates within 5 minutes.	angina typical criteria subject:cardiology
e5a079adfba9a172	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What is atypical angina?	Atypical angina (probable angina) occurs when a patient meets only two of the three typical angina criteria.	angina atypical classification subject:cardiology
12361e64b3a34e56	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What is non-anginal chest pain?	Non-anginal chest pain is when only one of the three typical angina criteria is satisfied.	chest-pain non-anginal classification subject:cardiology
5e4d1eb62eace0b4	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What is the Canadian Cardiovascular Society Classification of Angina?	The CCS Classification grades angina severity: (1) Class I - Angina only with very strenuous exertion; (2) Class II - Angina with moderate exertion; (3) Class III - Angina with mild exertion; (4) Class IV - Angina at rest. This is used to convey angina severity universally.	angina classification severity subject:cardiology
67db3acf9c6cdefe	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What is the significance of the age 35 in CAD?	Age 35 is significant because CAD prevalence increases after this age. While the disease begins in early teen years with fatty streaks, clinical manifestations typically appear after age 35, with peak incidence between ages 50-80.	epidemiology age disease-progression subject:cardiology
5f6c189f9d4c2c8e	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What evidence shows that CAD begins in childhood?	Post-mortem examinations of young individuals (aged 19-20) who died during wartime already showed early markings of atherosclerotic disease in the form of fatty streaks in coronary arteries, proving the disease starts very early in life.	pathophysiology disease-progression early-onset subject:cardiology
1202852d4aafd535	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	Why is treatment of CAD lifelong?	Treatment is lifelong because CAD is a chronic disease process that continues throughout life. Even if a plaque rupture is managed and the vessel is reopened (by treatment or healing), the vessel remains abnormal. The underlying atherosclerotic disease persists and requires ongoing management.	management treatment chronic-disease subject:cardiology
cd40741379f237a9	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What percentage of patients with CAD present with angina?	Approximately 50% of patients with CAD present with angina (stable or unstable), making it the most common initial manifestation of the disease.	presentation epidemiology angina subject:cardiology
658c6eb64da8ab1d	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	At what percentage stenosis does a coronary artery begin to restrict blood flow?	A coronary artery must be narrowed (stenosed) by more than 50-70% to meaningfully restrict blood flow downstream and cause clinical manifestations like angina.	pathophysiology stenosis hemodynamics subject:cardiology
fa9d96672f216941	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What percentage stenosis is clinically symptomatic in typical CAD?	Clinical manifestation of ischemic heart disease typically occurs once a coronary vessel is 70% or more narrowed. At this level of narrowing, blood flow is sufficiently restricted to cause ischemia and symptoms.	pathophysiology stenosis symptoms subject:cardiology
1ebb44465f2e06fc	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What is the role of inflammation in CAD pathophysiology?	CAD is fundamentally an inflammatory process. The atherosclerotic plaque contains inflammatory cells along with lipids and smooth muscle cells. This inflammatory nature explains why inflammatory conditions (lupus, rheumatoid arthritis, IBD) increase CAD risk.	pathophysiology inflammation atherosclerosis subject:cardiology
1b20086f594f6a64	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What diagnostic modalities are used for CAD diagnosis?	Diagnostic modalities include: (1) ECG - may show previous MI or changes with stress; (2) Stress testing - treadmill, pharmacological (dobutamine), or nuclear stress tests; (3) CT coronary angiography - visualizes plaque burden, quantifies stenosis percentage, and demonstrates coronary calcification.	diagnosis testing investigation subject:cardiology
c0292119e429558d	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What is the relationship between radiation pattern and angina severity?	The radiation pattern of chest pain (to neck, jaw, shoulder, or arm) is part of typical angina description. This characteristic radiation helps distinguish angina from other causes of chest pain and is one of the three diagnostic criteria.	angina clinical-features diagnosis subject:cardiology
f91f01fe940b70c5	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What does ST segment depression of more than 1mm indicate, especially if horizontal?	Highly suggestive of underlying coronary obstruction.	ECG ST-depression CAD-diagnosis subject:cardiology
b94daa8c7d7df559	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What is the significance of ST elevation myocardial infarction (STEMI)?	A medical emergency where myocytes are under threat and begin to die after 5 minutes without blood supply and oxygen delivery.	STEMI myocardial-infarction emergency subject:cardiology
1e41f7811f9f5e8f	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What are the diagnostic criteria for ST elevation in limb leads vs chest leads for STEMI?	Limb leads (I, II, III, aVR, aVL, aVF): require at least 1mm elevation. Chest leads (precordial): require at least 2mm elevation.	STEMI ECG-criteria diagnostic subject:cardiology
f4ff2541f6cb5171	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What does 'tombstone' pattern represent on ECG?	A pattern of convex ST elevation (often 5-15mm) seen in anterior STEMI, named for its appearance.	ECG STEMI anterior-infarction subject:cardiology
bcb421a820439fb4	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What is reciprocal ST depression in the context of myocardial infarction?	ST depression in leads opposite to those showing ST elevation; occurs when infarction affects one region and helps confirm the diagnosis.	ECG myocardial-infarction reciprocal-changes subject:cardiology
075e4cd23a42afd9	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	When is exercise stress testing contraindicated?	In very high-risk patients (with typical anginal symptoms and multiple risk factors) and very low-risk patients (young individuals with no risk factors), as it may provoke ischemic events or is unhelpful.	exercise-stress-test contraindications risk-assessment subject:cardiology
7affcb1d2ef08f96	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What is the Brusport protocol for exercise stress testing?	Standard protocol where the treadmill increases speed and incline every 3 minutes; has 7 stages, with most individuals expected to complete at least stages 1 and 2 (6 minutes of exercise).	exercise-stress-test protocol methodology subject:cardiology
4dad4adb8b02b138	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What ECG changes during exercise stress testing warrant immediate cessation?	ST elevation (ominous), ST depression greater than 1.5-2mm, or inability to increase heart rate adequately.	exercise-stress-test ECG-changes stopping-criteria subject:cardiology
1aad2c4136812137	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What are the two main classes of antiplatelet drugs used in coronary artery disease?	Aspirin and ADP receptor antagonists (P2Y12 inhibitors) such as clopidogrel.	pharmacotherapy antiplatelet-drugs coronary-artery-disease subject:cardiology
c6f5f0c56e5321ed	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	Name the main classes of lipid-lowering agents used in CAD management, in order of escalation.	Statins (first line), azetamide (if statin targets not reached), and PCSK9 inhibitors (if further reduction needed).	pharmacotherapy lipid-lowering statins subject:cardiology
612d0bf693c703a1	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	When are beta-blockers particularly beneficial in coronary artery disease?	In patients with prior myocardial infarction and LV dysfunction (ejection fraction <40%), and in those with high heart rates or angina, as they reduce myocardial oxygen demand.	pharmacotherapy beta-blockers mechanism subject:cardiology
14e3c16e283abb7a	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What are contemporary disease-modifying therapies in coronary artery disease beyond statins?	PCSK9 inhibitors, bempedoic acid, inclisiran (SGLT2 inhibitors and GLP-1 agonists for diabetic patients), inflammatory modulators (e.g., Ziltivekumab, Canakinumab), and renin-angiotensin-aldosterone inhibitors.	pharmacotherapy novel-agents disease-modification subject:cardiology
22776be5fcd736b7	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What is the target resting heart rate for patients on beta-blockers with stable angina?	Below 60 bpm, ideally in the 50s.	beta-blockers therapeutic-target stable-angina subject:cardiology
5943410300bf1576	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What non-dihydropyridine calcium antagonists are used when beta-blockers are contraindicated?	Diltiazem and verapamil, as they also reduce heart rate, blood pressure, and force of LV contractility.	pharmacotherapy calcium-antagonists alternative-therapy subject:cardiology
c8e5e5aeda8ea418	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What hemoglobin level should be maintained to ensure adequate myocardial oxygen delivery?	Hemoglobin should be kept above 8 g/dL; anemia compromises oxygen delivery to cardiac myocytes.	management anemia oxygen-delivery subject:cardiology
fbaefbae35a4321f	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What oxygen saturation target is recommended for patients with coronary artery disease?	Above 95%; supplemental oxygen should be given if saturation is lower.	oxygen-therapy saturation-target management subject:cardiology
d35a097e4f1b4b8c	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What are the key lifestyle modifications recommended for stable angina?	Stop smoking, blood pressure <130/80 (ideally), LDL <1 mmol/L, HbA1c <7%, BMI <25, endurance training 30 min 5x/week, and resistance training for muscle maintenance.	lifestyle-modification risk-factor-management stable-angina subject:cardiology
56bfd5246abeb19a	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	When should coronary angiography be performed in stable angina despite medical treatment?	When patients remain severely symptomatic (class III-IV angina) despite medical therapy, or when stress testing shows high-risk features (early ST changes before stage 2, drop in BP, marked ST depression), or if there is LV dysfunction.	coronary-angiography indications stable-angina subject:cardiology
6dcfa6ab9484d026	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What is the definition of LV dysfunction in the context of angina management?	Ejection fraction below 40% is clearly LV dysfunction; EF >50-60% is considered normal.	LV-function ejection-fraction dysfunction subject:cardiology
ef643b8ac0fb1c15	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What are the two main revascularization strategies for CAD?	Percutaneous coronary intervention (angioplasty with or without stenting) or coronary artery bypass surgery in those unsuitable for PCI.	revascularization PCI CABG subject:cardiology
798b8478f07457f3	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What is the difference between STEMI and non-STEMI acute coronary syndrome?	STEMI involves total vessel occlusion by thrombus (medical emergency), while non-STEMI has partial patency with blood still able to flow (urgent but not emergent).	acute-coronary-syndrome STEMI non-STEMI subject:cardiology
328db3cf59dc3666	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What is the critical 'total ischemic time' target for STEMI management?	Less than 3 hours from symptom onset to vessel opening; this is when maximum myocyte salvage occurs and lives are saved.	STEMI reperfusion time-critical subject:cardiology
565532e25bb7feea	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	How is the onset time for STEMI determined when pain is intermittent?	STEMI timing begins from the onset of the pain that is most severe and ongoing, persisting beyond 30 minutes; stuttering pain is timed from the continuous phase.	STEMI diagnosis symptom-timing subject:cardiology
4ca028ce0588a76a	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What are the two essential diagnostic criteria for STEMI in the acute setting?	Typical ischemic chest pain ongoing for more than 30 minutes AND ST elevation on ECG (≥1mm in limb leads or ≥2mm in chest leads).	STEMI diagnosis diagnostic-criteria subject:cardiology
f3cfffef33846a67	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	When should troponin be awaited before diagnosing STEMI?	Never; do not wait for troponin results in STEMI as it may take hours or days. Diagnosis is based solely on symptoms and ECG changes to enable rapid reperfusion.	STEMI troponin diagnostic-approach subject:cardiology
bb85d12b68c5061f	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What should be done if ECG shows ST elevation is not clear when a patient presents early with chest pain?	Repeat the ECG every 10 minutes until clear ST elevation is demonstrated, then act immediately; this maximizes diagnostic accuracy while maintaining urgency.	STEMI ECG-interpretation early-presentation subject:cardiology
426c23fa232e9a4b	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What is the gold standard for reperfusion in STEMI?	Primary percutaneous coronary intervention (angioplasty with stenting), which achieves 95% success rate in skilled hands.	STEMI reperfusion angioplasty subject:cardiology
d9bbe7a369e29ad3	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What is fibrinolysis and what is its time window in STEMI?	Fibrinolysis is thrombolytic therapy given when PCI is not timeously available; it can be given up to 12 hours from symptom onset, ideally within 3 hours.	STEMI fibrinolysis thrombolytic-therapy subject:cardiology
c94ade90054f1af5	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	Name the three types of fibrinolytic agents used in STEMI.	Streptokinase (cheapest, infused over 1 hour), alteplase (more potent than streptokinase), and tenectoplase (best fibrinolytic agent with superior properties).	STEMI fibrinolysis thrombolytic-agents subject:cardiology
86ea7adf74ce02ea	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What is the preferred fibrinolytic agent for STEMI, and why?	Tenectoplase is preferred (most effective), followed by alteplase, then streptokinase. However, any fibrinolytic agent given timely is better than nothing. Tenectoplase is most expensive and not always available.	STEMI fibrinolytic_agents treatment subject:cardiology
2b2e315ee0506726	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What dual anti-platelet therapy is used in STEMI?	Aspirin (chewed tablet for rapid absorption, then 100mg indefinitely) and clopidogrel loading dose 300mg followed by 75mg daily. Dual therapy continues for 1 year; then clopidogrel is stopped but aspirin continues for life.	STEMI antiplatelet_therapy medication subject:cardiology
331395c1751a00b9	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What anti-thrombin agent is preferred in STEMI and why?	Enoxaparin is preferred over heparin because it is given subcutaneously (easier administration). Heparin requires continuous IV infusion and needs monitoring, making it more cumbersome.	STEMI anticoagulation enoxaparin subject:cardiology
ec4d031b5e67c85b	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What is the minimum number of antithrombotic agents given in STEMI?	A minimum of 4 antithrombotic agents: fibrinolytic agent + 2 antiplatelet agents (aspirin + clopidogrel) + 1 anticoagulant (enoxaparin or heparin).	STEMI antithrombotic_therapy subject:cardiology
1c693f5594c177e1	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What is the success rate of primary angioplasty vs fibrinolytic therapy in STEMI?	Primary angioplasty leads to 95% vessel opening in skilled hands. Fibrinolytic therapy achieves approximately 64% vessel patency.	STEMI angioplasty fibrinolysis subject:cardiology
f4d1cf357f1d8432	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What is the timeline for endothelial incorporation of a metal stent?	A metal stent is typically incorporated into the vessel wall by the endothelium over 1-3 months.	stent vascular_biology subject:cardiology
d2604958a104dada	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What are the main adjunctive therapies in acute STEMI management?	Pain relief (morphine or nitrates), high-dose statins (80mg atorvastatin vs 20mg chronically), ACE inhibitors (if EF <40%), beta blockers (if no heart block/bradycardia), oxygen (if saturation <90%), and treatment of comorbidities.	STEMI adjunctive_therapy subject:cardiology
1e4e99f2cf6ebf9c	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What is a contraindication to fibrinolytic therapy in STEMI?	Absolute contraindications include: hemorrhagic or recent stroke (<6 months), brain tumor, recent severe head trauma, major trauma/surgery within 2 weeks, active internal bleeding (peptic ulcer), aortic dissection, pregnancy, or prolonged CPR.	fibrinolysis contraindications STEMI subject:cardiology
c37e1a59e4d09a77	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What is the impact of uncontrolled hypertension on fibrinolytic eligibility?	Severe uncontrolled hypertension is technically a contraindication, but it can be managed by starting IV nitrate infusion to lower BP below 150 systolic, after which fibrinolytic therapy can be safely given.	hypertension fibrinolysis subject:cardiology
58fd8b2bcdc9cf82	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What are the early complications of STEMI related to arrhythmias?	Ventricular tachycardia (VT) or ventricular fibrillation (VF) can occur immediately when the vessel occludes and myocytes begin to die. If not treated immediately, this leads to sudden cardiac death.	STEMI arrhythmias complications subject:cardiology
a86667ed6fa34586	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What heart block complications can occur with STEMI?	Right coronary or multiple vessel occlusion can cause high-degree heart block (Type 2) or complete heart block, requiring emergency temporary pacemaker insertion.	STEMI heart_block complications subject:cardiology
47760eec1aa18f39	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What percentage of left ventricular involvement leads to cardiogenic shock in STEMI?	If >40% of the left ventricle is infarcted, left ventricular failure, pulmonary edema, or cardiogenic shock can occur.	STEMI cardiogenic_shock complications subject:cardiology
04f0363c0dd4c126	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What mechanical complications can occur after STEMI?	Acute mitral regurgitation (due to papillary muscle infarction), ventricular septal defect (from full-thickness septal rupture), and cardiac rupture (full-thickness wall rupture).	STEMI mechanical_complications subject:cardiology
b55b6688e117a7b1	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What is the 30-day mortality in untreated STEMI?	Untreated STEMI has 30-day mortality of approximately 40%. Fibrinolytic therapy reduces this to ~9%. Early intervention within 1 hour of occlusion reduces mortality to ~1%.	STEMI mortality prognosis subject:cardiology
01b08e9e70e05e11	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What is the key difference between non-STEMI and unstable angina regarding myocardial necrosis?	In non-STEMI, ischemia is severe enough to cause myocardial necrosis (elevated troponin or creatinine kinase). In unstable angina, there is no biochemical evidence of myocyte necrosis (troponins and CK are negative).	non-STEMI unstable_angina troponin subject:cardiology
409a392bba17a862	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What biomarker has most improved the management of coronary artery disease?	Troponin is the go-to biomarker that has really altered CAD management. It has replaced creatinine kinase and is more sensitive for detecting myocardial necrosis.	biomarker troponin diagnosis subject:cardiology
6668e03a92e580cc	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	How is unstable angina defined in terms of symptom pattern change?	Unstable angina means the pattern of angina has changed in the last 60 days: angina becoming more frequent, occurring with less effort (e.g., walking 50m on flat instead of climbing 2 flights of stairs), OR new-onset angina developing for the first time.	unstable_angina definition subject:cardiology
fc6f09f8ea9d3123	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What is the pathophysiology of unstable angina and non-STEMI?	Yellow plaque with non-occlusive thrombus; bits of thrombus break off and occlude downstream vessels. The goal is to prevent the non-occlusive thrombus from becoming occlusive and causing infarction.	pathophysiology unstable_angina non-STEMI subject:cardiology
398f2ee6bc8e569a	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What are the three risk categories for non-STEMI?	Non-STEMI patients are risk-stratified into high, intermediate, and low risk based on symptoms, physical signs, ECG, and biomarkers. Higher risk correlates with poorer prognosis.	non-STEMI risk_stratification subject:cardiology
dcf2412b23b5e74f	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What defines high-risk non-STEMI?	High-risk non-STEMI includes: angina at rest in preceding 48 hours, hemodynamic instability (tachycardia >100, bradycardia <40, systolic BP <90), evidence of LV dysfunction (third heart sound, crackles, EF <40%), advanced elderly (>75 years), previous MI/angioplasty/bypass, other vascular disease (stroke, peripheral vascular disease), or positive troponins.	non-STEMI high-risk subject:cardiology
f5ec92d754f49b16	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What ECG findings are typically seen in non-STEMI and unstable angina?	ECG may be completely normal in both conditions (>30% of patients). When abnormal: ST segment depression, transient ST elevation, bundle branch block, or T-wave inversion may be seen. ST segment depression strengthens diagnostic confidence for non-STEMI. It indicates ischemia and suggests higher risk, especially when more pronounced.	ECG ST_depression non-STEMI subject:cardiology
8e68191e9b5dc726	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	How is low-risk unstable angina managed?	Low-risk unstable angina (nausea pain, normal exam, normal ECG, normal troponins) is not admitted. Patient is treated with aspirin, statin, blood pressure/diabetes management. Risk is reassessed with symptoms and stress testing; additional imaging (angiogram) is decided based on results.	unstable_angina low-risk management subject:cardiology
047b43206eade54b	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What anti-ischemic therapy is used in high-risk non-STEMI?	Nitrates (5-200 mcg depending on pain severity, if systolic BP >100, increased every 3-5 min until symptom relief or BP <100), morphine (1-5mg IV titration), and beta blockers to maintain HR <60 (if no heart block).	non-STEMI anti-ischemic_therapy subject:cardiology
cdf3ec3f873fa180	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)	What dual anti-platelet therapy is used in non-STEMI?	Aspirin (whole tablet chewed, then 100mg daily indefinitely) and clopidogrel for minimum 12 months. At 12 months, clopidogrel is stopped but aspirin continues for life.	non-STEMI antiplatelet_therapy subject:cardiology

Glycoprotein 2B3A receptor antagonist is a potent intravenous antiplatelet drug used selectively in the CCU or as bailout during angioplasty/stenting in non-STEMI patients.	non-STEMI antiplatelet_therapy GP_IIb/IIIa subject:cardiology

Troponin levels are checked at admission (may be normal early), then repeated at 3 hours and 6 hours after first patient contact. If non-STEMI, troponin levels rise over time.	non-STEMI troponin diagnosis subject:cardiology

Positive troponin elevation is a marker of high risk in non-STEMI. These patients proceed to angiography for revascularization.	non-STEMI troponin risk subject:cardiology

Serial troponins (3-4 measurements) should all be normal with no further pain or ECG changes to confirm low-risk status. 0995eab2fe674287	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	What is the ECG paper speed and time calibration?	Paper speed: 25 mm/second. X-axis (time): each 1 mm = 0.04 seconds (40 milliseconds). Y-axis (amplitude): each 10 mm = 1 millivolt.	ecg-calibration measurement subject:cardiology
f2a3db98d39e2ed3	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	How many electrodes and leads are used in a standard ECG?	10 electrodes that produce 12 leads: 3 standard limb leads (I, II, III), 3 augmented limb leads (aVR, aVL, aVF), and 6 precordial leads (V1-V6).	ecg-leads electrode-placement subject:cardiology

V2: 4th intercostal space, left sternal border. V3: Between V2 and V4. V4: 5th intercostal space, midclavicular line. V5: Between V4 and V6. V6: 5th intercostal space, midaxillary line.	ecg-leads precordial-leads subject:cardiology
9bdb1d424990557d	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	Which ECG leads view which cardiac walls?	Inferior leads (II, III, aVF): inferior wall. Lateral leads (I, aVL, V5, V6): lateral wall. Anterior leads (V1-V4): anterior wall and septum. V1-V2: septal region.	ecg-leads cardiac-territories subject:cardiology
698c7c571cff4299	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	How is heart rate calculated from an ECG?	Method 1: Count QRS complexes in 10 seconds and multiply by 6. Method 2 (regular rhythm): Count large blocks between two R waves and divide into 300.	heart-rate ecg-analysis subject:cardiology
66c48bdf4db1544c	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	What defines normal sinus rhythm?	P wave before every QRS complex and QRS after every P wave. Rate 60-100 bpm. P wave is negative in aVR and positive in lead II (normal pattern).	sinus-rhythm normal-ecg subject:cardiology
19778269f77e4057	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	What is the impulse conduction pathway in the heart?	SA node → intra-atrial pathways → AV node (with delay) → bundle of His → right and left bundle branches → Purkinje system → ventricular depolarization.	conduction-system cardiac-physiology subject:cardiology
6249c00ff528bef9	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	What causes sinus bradycardia and what is the heart rate?	Heart rate <60 bpm. Causes: beta-blockers, calcium channel blockers, athletes, right coronary artery ischemia/MI (which supplies SA and AV nodes), hypothyroidism, hypothermia, increased intracranial pressure.	sinus-bradycardia arrhythmias subject:cardiology
1062149fa4baae64	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	What is sinus tachycardia and its causes?	Heart rate 100-150 bpm in sinus rhythm. Physiological causes: anxiety, pain, fever, exercise. Drug-related: adrenaline, atropine, caffeine, alcohol, salbutamol. Pathological: anemia, hypovolemia, hyperthyroidism.	sinus-tachycardia arrhythmias subject:cardiology
6c632420bc26f964	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	What is the normal cardiac axis and its deviations?	Normal axis: -30 to +90 degrees. Left axis deviation (LAD): -30 to -90 degrees. Right axis deviation (RAD): +90 to +180 degrees.	cardiac-axis axis-deviation subject:cardiology
cfbdd4382d964618	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	What are the ECG findings in left axis deviation?	Positive R-wave in lead I, negative R-wave (or small R) in aVF and lead II. Causes: Wolff-Parkinson-White (WPW) syndrome, inferior MI, left ventricular hypertrophy.	left-axis-deviation axis-deviation subject:cardiology
f072f75417b26d26	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	What are the ECG findings in right axis deviation?	Negative R-wave in lead I, positive R-wave in aVF. Causes: right ventricular hypertrophy, certain types of WPW syndrome, atrial fibrillation, hyperkalemia, atrial septal defect.	right-axis-deviation axis-deviation subject:cardiology
49046469efd67934	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	What is P-pulmonale and what does it indicate?	P-pulmonale: P-waves >2.5 mm tall (peaked). Indicates right atrial enlargement. Associated with pulmonary hypertension, pulmonary stenosis, tricuspid stenosis.	p-wave-abnormalities atrial-enlargement subject:cardiology
1ce2fdf6037e958a	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	What is P-mitrale and what does it indicate?	P-mitrale: P-waves >120 milliseconds wide with bifid (two-peaked) appearance. Indicates left atrial enlargement. Associated with mitral valve disease.	p-wave-abnormalities atrial-enlargement subject:cardiology
4b5d0e547fc6a598	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	What is the PR interval and what is the normal duration?	PR interval: measured from start of P-wave to start of R-wave. Represents time of atrial depolarization plus AV node conduction delay. Normal: 120-200 milliseconds. Should be consistent beat-to-beat.	pr-interval conduction subject:cardiology
f03eb50860864c63	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	How are Q-waves classified as pathological?	Q-waves are pathological if: (1) >25% height of following R-wave, OR (2) >1 small block (40 ms) in width. Pathological Q-waves usually indicate previous MI but can also occur with LVH and bundle branch blocks.	q-waves myocardial-infarction subject:cardiology
6c2fc89062a823d6	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	What is the normal QRS duration and R-wave progression?	QRS complex: <120 milliseconds (less than 3 small blocks). R-wave should increase progressively in height from V1 to V6 (normal R-wave progression). This is because left ventricle has the most mass.	qrs-complex ventricular-depolarization subject:cardiology
4b83c9f5b0ec503c	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	What are the criteria for left ventricular hypertrophy (LVH) on ECG?	LVH criteria: (1) R-wave in V5 or V6 >27 mm, OR (2) S-wave in V1 + R-wave in V5 >35 mm. Also shows deeper S-wave in V1-V2 and taller R-wave in V5-V6.	lvh ventricular-hypertrophy subject:cardiology
8d8b8475f93bba76	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	What are the ECG findings in right ventricular hypertrophy (RVH)?	RVH shows: (1) Dominant R-wave in V1 (R-wave taller than S-wave), (2) Persistent S-wave in V5-V6. Causes: chronic lung disease, pulmonary hypertension.	rvh ventricular-hypertrophy subject:cardiology
9a615a9ccfb14031	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	What causes QRS widening in bundle branch blocks?	Bundle branch blocks cause QRS >120 milliseconds because impulses cannot travel through the blocked bundle and must spread myocyte-to-myocyte through myocardium, which is much slower than normal conduction system.	bundle-branch-block qrs-widening subject:cardiology
08a24def4b17ee86	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	What are the ECG patterns in right bundle branch block (RBBB)?	RBBB shows: (1) QRS >120 ms, (2) M-pattern (RSR') in V1-V2, (3) W-pattern in V6, (4) Right ventricle depolarizes after left ventricle.	rbbb bundle-branch-block subject:cardiology
138fe119701dc6df	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	What are the ECG patterns in left bundle branch block (LBBB)?	LBBB shows: (1) QRS >120 ms, (2) W-pattern in V1-V2, (3) M-pattern (RSR') in V6, (4) Left ventricle depolarizes after right ventricle.	lbbb bundle-branch-block subject:cardiology
0835312df965d570	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	What is the J-point and ST segment elevation significance?	J-point: junction where QRS complex meets the isoelectric line. ST segment elevation >1 mm from the J-point is significant. Main causes: STEMI (most common), pericarditis, LV aneurysm, Prinzmetal angina, LBB, Brugada syndrome.	st-segment st-elevation subject:cardiology
c1a0133fb76a473a	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	How does pericarditis differ from STEMI on ECG?	Pericarditis: (1) Saddle-shaped (concave) ST elevation, (2) No Q-waves, (3) No reciprocal ST depression, (4) Widespread ST elevation over most leads, (5) PR segment depression in II, (6) PR segment elevation in aVR (pathognomonic).	pericarditis st-elevation subject:cardiology
59b025d2e428d9e0	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	What is reciprocal ST change in myocardial infarction?	Reciprocal changes: ST elevation in one area of the heart is accompanied by ST depression in another (opposite) area. Occurs in STEMI but NOT in pericarditis. Indicates a single affected territory.	myocardial-infarction st-changes subject:cardiology
60018df05b3441e1	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	What is ST depression and what causes it?	ST depression: >0.5 mm below baseline. Causes: myocardial ischemia/strain, digoxin (downsloping with T-wave inversion called reverse tick), hypokalemia.	st-segment st-depression subject:cardiology
e0f68ff366d76cde	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	What are the stages of myocardial infarction on ECG?	Stage 1: Peaked/hyperacute T-waves. Stage 2: ST segment elevation. Stage 3: Q-wave formation (necrosis). Stage 4: ST returns to normal, T-waves invert. Stage 5: Q-waves persist as marker of previous MI.	myocardial-infarction mi-stages subject:cardiology
a5f8daffcdfb745d	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	Which ECG leads correspond to which MI territories?	Anterior MI: V1-V4. Lateral MI: I, aVL, V5-V6. Anterolateral MI: V1-V5, I, aVL. Inferior MI: II, III, aVF. Anteroseptal: V1-V3. Infrolateral: II, III, aVF, V5-V6.	myocardial-infarction mi-territories subject:cardiology
07f55e66d597c8e2	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	How do you diagnose right ventricular infarction?	Right ventricular MI usually occurs with inferior MI. Place V4 lead on right side of chest (called V4R) and look for ST elevation. ST elevation in V4R indicates RV infarction.	myocardial-infarction rv-infarction subject:cardiology
7ed6ef78d0b9d8e9	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	How do you diagnose posterior myocardial infarction on ECG?	Posterior MI usually accompanies inferior MI. Signs: (1) Tall R-wave (reciprocal Q-wave) in V1 with ST depression, (2) Can place precordial leads on back of chest (V7, V8, V9) and look for ST elevation.	myocardial-infarction posterior-mi subject:cardiology
a29e91a9230d4e77	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	What are the ECG findings in hypokalemia?	Hypokalemia shows: (1) Flattened T-waves, (2) Prominent U-wave, (3) ST depression, (4) T-wave inversion, (5) Downsloping ST segment similar to digoxin effect.	electrolyte-abnormalities hypokalemia subject:cardiology
3e81d01c33d5f22b	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	What are the ECG findings in hyperkalemia?	Early hyperkalemia: (1) Peaked (tall, narrow) T-waves. As severity increases: (2) Flattened P-waves, (3) Prolonged PR interval, (4) Narrower, broader QRS complex (Splinterung), (5) Can progress to ventricular fibrillation (medical emergency).	electrolyte-abnormalities hyperkalemia subject:cardiology
41bc0eaddcd2dd54	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	What are the ECG findings in hypothermia?	Hypothermia shows: (1) Bradycardia, (2) Irregular baseline (from shivering), (3) J-wave or Osborn wave at end of QRS complex before ST segment.	environmental-effects hypothermia subject:cardiology
8dc2ed903ab94a03	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	What is first-degree AV block?	First-degree AV block: (1) Prolonged PR interval >200 ms (>5 small blocks), (2) Constant PR duration, (3) Every P-wave followed by QRS complex (1:1 conduction). Causes: ischemia, hypokalemia, rheumatic heart disease, aortic root abscess, digoxin, beta-blockers, calcium channel blockers.	av-blocks conduction-disorders subject:cardiology
c8e3d196a66974b4	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	What is Mobitz type 1 (Wenckebach) second-degree AV block?	Mobitz type 1: (1) Progressive prolongation of PR interval beat-to-beat (lengthening), (2) Then sudden P-wave without QRS (dropped beat), (3) Cycle repeats. Most PR intervals are conducted. Usually benign.	av-blocks conduction-disorders subject:cardiology
7378fb46335dfe1d	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	What is Mobitz type 2 second-degree AV block?	Mobitz type 2: (1) Constant PR interval (no lengthening), (2) Sudden dropped beats (P-wave without QRS), (3) Regular pattern of block (e.g., 2:1, 3:1). More serious than Mobitz I, requires pacemaker insertion due to risk of complete heart block.	av-blocks conduction-disorders subject:cardiology
b13c061f010ebda3	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	How do you differentiate Mobitz type 1 and type 2 AV block?	Mobitz type 1: PR interval progressively lengthens before dropped beat. Mobitz type 2: PR interval remains constant, no progressive lengthening.	av-blocks conduction-disorders subject:cardiology
9383a68efa29aba4	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	What is third-degree AV block (complete heart block)?	Third-degree AV block: (1) No relationship between P-waves and QRS complexes, (2) P-waves and PP intervals are regular (atrial rate from SA node), (3) QRS complexes are regular at slower rate (ventricular escape rhythm), (4) Broad QRS complex from ventricular origin, (5) Complete AV dissociation. Requires pacemaker insertion.	av-blocks conduction-disorders subject:cardiology
e073c1ac550343af	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	What approach should be used for tachyarrhythmias?	Approach: (1) Identify rhythm strip (usually lead II), (2) Determine if regular or irregular, (3) Determine if narrow or broad QRS, (4) Narrow complex = always supraventricular tachycardia, (5) Broad complex = 80% ventricular tachycardia, 20% SVT with BBB.	tachyarrhythmias arrhythmia-approach subject:cardiology
4ee832a18aa43c31	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	What is atrial fibrillation and its ECG appearance?	Atrial fibrillation: (1) Rapid chaotic depolarization from multiple foci, (2) Absent P-waves with fibrillating baseline, (3) Irregular (irregularly irregular) QRS complexes, (4) Ventricular rate variable. Most common arrhythmia.	atrial-fibrillation arrhythmias subject:cardiology
96fac69dafaf9e54	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	What is atrial flutter and its ECG appearance?	Atrial flutter: (1) Reentry circuit around right atrium, (2) Atrial rate 250-350 bpm, (3) Typical sawtooth flutter waves on baseline, (4) Regular QRS complexes (usually 2:1, 3:1, or 4:1 AV conduction), (5) AV node cannot conduct all atrial impulses.	atrial-flutter arrhythmias subject:cardiology
12910a79124f50a5	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	What is atrial tachycardia and its characteristics?	Atrial tachycardia: (1) Heart rate often >150 bpm, (2) P-waves present but may not be easily visible, (3) Abnormally shaped P-waves (may appear just after QRS), (4) Due to ectopic focus from abnormal area of atrium.	atrial-tachycardia arrhythmias subject:cardiology
8a1a7dda29dc5889	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	What is ventricular tachycardia and how is it defined?	Ventricular tachycardia: (1) Broad QRS complex (>120 ms), (2) Rate >120 bpm, (3) Three or more successive ventricular beats, (4) Arises from reentry circuit or ectopic ventricular focus (e.g., scar from previous MI), (5) May be self-terminating or sustained, (6) Can degenerate to ventricular fibrillation.	ventricular-tachycardia arrhythmias subject:cardiology
d3b163cabd0d1108	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	What are the types of ventricular tachycardia?	Monomorphic VT: All QRS complexes look the same (from single focus). Polymorphic VT: Varying QRS complexes. Torsades de Pointes: Type of polymorphic VT with twisting pattern, caused by prolonged QT interval from electrolyte abnormalities or drugs.	ventricular-tachycardia arrhythmias subject:cardiology
31246df4c84d9a65	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	What causes accelerated ventricular rhythm and idioventricular rhythm?	Accelerated ventricular rhythm: Heart rate 40-120 bpm (between normal escape rate and VT). Idioventricular rhythm: Heart rate 30-40 bpm (ventricular escape rhythm). Both are slower than VT (>120 bpm).	ventricular-rhythms arrhythmias subject:cardiology
7f42a4c25c6f2121	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	What is ventricular fibrillation and how is it managed?	Ventricular fibrillation: (1) Rapidly fatal arrhythmia, (2) Most common cause is myocardial infarction, (3) Can occur with electrolyte abnormalities, (4) Requires urgent defibrillation (electrical cardioversion).	ventricular-fibrillation arrhythmias subject:cardiology
992e95b4994ae997	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	What is asystole?	Asystole: Absence of any electrical cardiac activity on ECG (flat line). Results in no cardiac output and requires urgent CPR and ACLS protocol.	cardiac-arrest arrhythmias subject:cardiology
2fff06aab7586903	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	What is torsades de pointes?	Torsades de Pointes: Polymorphic VT with twisting pattern around the baseline. Caused by prolonged QT interval from electrolyte abnormalities (hypomagnesemia) or QT-prolonging drugs (antiarrhythmics). Often associated with sudden syncope and VF. Treated with IV magnesium and electrical cardioversion.	torsades-de-pointes polymorphic-vt subject:cardiology
d3f4821b559415f3	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	What is Wolff-Parkinson-White (WPW) syndrome?	WPW syndrome: (1) Accessory pathway (bundle of Kent) between atrium and ventricles, (2) Bypass of AV node causes short PR interval (<120 ms), (3) Delta wave (slurred upstroke) in QRS complex, (4) Can develop reentry tachycardia (AVNRT circuit).	wpw-syndrome pre-excitation subject:cardiology
45c727cbc3ab4470	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	What is the delta wave in WPW syndrome?	Delta wave: Slurred upstroke at the beginning of QRS complex in WPW syndrome. Caused by slow myocyte-to-myocyte depolarization through accessory pathway (not through normal conduction system).	wpw-syndrome pre-excitation subject:cardiology
0b6ffdea2f051a77	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	How does the accessory pathway in WPW cause short PR interval?	In WPW: (1) Accessory pathway bypasses the AV node delay, (2) Conduction through accessory pathway is faster than through AV node, (3) Ventricles depolarize immediately after atrium, (4) Results in short PR interval (<120 ms).	wpw-syndrome pre-excitation subject:cardiology
890d7b773da2c0e0	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	What is AV reentry tachycardia (AVNRT) in WPW?	AVNRT in WPW: (1) Macro reentry circuit develops, (2) Impulse travels down AV node through bundle branches into myocardium, (3) Then back up through accessory pathway, (4) Depolarizes atrium and restarts circuit, (5) Results in rapid narrow or broad complex tachycardia.	wpw-syndrome reentry-tachycardia subject:cardiology
20aafd4387baa5e6	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	What are the differential diagnoses for narrow regular tachycardia?	Narrow regular tachycardia (SVT): (1) Sinus tachycardia, (2) Atrial flutter, (3) Atrial tachycardia, (4) AV nodal reentry tachycardia, (5) AV reentry tachycardia (WPW).	tachyarrhythmias svt subject:cardiology
45b09bbc3b683956	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::ECG Interpretation	What are the differential diagnoses for narrow irregular tachycardia? 9699c04c495eea01	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Infective Endocarditis	What is infective endocarditis?	Infection of the endocardial surface, typically with vegetations on the endocardium. The heart valves are most commonly affected. It is virtually always fatal if untreated.	definition endocarditis subject:cardiology
6f70d27eed8bb4a9	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Infective Endocarditis	How is infective endocarditis classified?	Four categories: 1) Native valve endocarditis 2) Prosthetic valve endocarditis 3) IV drug abuse-related endocarditis 4) Nosocomial endocarditis. Also classified as acute or subacute.	classification endocarditis subject:cardiology
f31be20f45bae2d5	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Infective Endocarditis	What is the difference between acute and subacute infective endocarditis?	Acute: marked toxicity, progresses over days to weeks, causes valvular destruction and metastatic infection. Usually caused by Staphylococcus aureus. Fatal in <6 weeks if untreated. Subacute: evolves over weeks to months with modest toxicity. Usually caused by Streptococcus viridans. Takes longer than 6 weeks to be fatal if untreated.	acute subacute endocarditis prognosis subject:cardiology
f95d0f98cd2e1678	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Infective Endocarditis	What is the annual incidence and epidemiology of infective endocarditis?	Annual incidence: 15-20,000 cases per year. Fourth leading cause of life-threatening infectious disease. Slight predilection for males (ratio 1.7:1). Mitral valve affected in up to 45% of cases, aortic valve in 36%.	epidemiology endocarditis incidence subject:cardiology
de50ca8228d22560	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Infective Endocarditis	What are the most common etiological agents of infective endocarditis?	Streptococcus group: up to 80% of cases. Staphylococcus: 20-35%. Gram-negative aerobic bacilli: up to 13%. Other bacteria and fungi. Culture-negative endocarditis: up to 24% of cases.	microbiology endocarditis organisms subject:cardiology

Approximately 75% of patients have pre-existing structural cardiac abnormality.	risk_factors predisposing_factors endocarditis subject:cardiology
cf20c5b76bae7407	Basic	Corpus::Cardiology::Infective Endocarditis	What is the pathogenesis of infective endocarditis?	The valve surface is altered to produce a suitable site for bacterial attachment. Platelets and fibrin deposit, forming sterile vegetation (NBTE). When bacteria reach the site, they colonize and are covered with platelets and fibrin, leading to bacterial multiplication and vegetation growth.	pathophysiology pathogenesis endocarditis subject:cardiology

Immune-mediated: Hashimoto's thyroiditis or drug-induced (interferon, immune checkpoint inhibitors). 2. 8394c8031431db1e	Basic	Corpus::Diabetes::Hyperthyroidism	What is the clinical presentation of inflammatory thyroid disease?	Mild thyroid toxicosis, pain over thyroid often referred to jaw, fever, systemic illness. Isotope uptake absent. Antibodies very high in immune-mediated, absent in non-immune. Self-limiting with spontaneous resolution.	thyroiditis clinical-presentation subject:diabetes
fbc7a61099c52204	Basic	Corpus::Diabetes::Hyperthyroidism	What are the three main treatment categories for thyroid toxicosis?	1. Symptomatic therapy: beta-blockers for heart rate, tremor control, and reducing T4 to T3 conversion. 2. Specific therapy: anti-thyroid drugs, thyroidectomy, radioactive iodine. 3. Duration and choice depends on etiology and circumstances.	treatment management subject:diabetes
13ac89ec7fe28e57	Basic	Corpus::Diabetes::Hyperthyroidism	What is carbimazole and what is its mechanism of action?	Anti-thyroid drug available in South Africa (trade name Neomerkazole). Inhibits thyroid hormone synthesis by inhibiting thyroid peroxidase enzyme activity. Used for ~75 years, highly effective, generally well-tolerated.	pharmacology antithyroid-drugs subject:diabetes
d8978b6362401b9d	Basic	Corpus::Diabetes::Hyperthyroidism	What are the adverse effects of anti-thyroid drugs?	Primary adverse effect: bone marrow toxicity (agranulocytosis). Relative risk of pancreatitis. Long-term use may be associated with vasculitis.	pharmacology adverse-effects subject:diabetes
671114e9906ed4bb	Basic	Corpus::Diabetes::Hyperthyroidism	How does radioactive iodine work and what are its considerations?	Radioactive iodine (technetium 99) concentrates in thyroid, emits radiation destroying thyroid tissue. Teratogenic, may worsen Graves orbitopathy. Has slow onset; anti-thyroid drugs used meanwhile. Dose may need repetition after 6 months.	treatment radioactive-iodine subject:diabetes
76f90176bf7bbe37	Basic	Corpus::Diabetes::Hyperthyroidism	When is total thyroidectomy indicated for thyroid toxicosis?	Reserved for: very large goiter with mediastinal compression, patient preference, mid-trimester pregnancy, inadequate response to medical therapy. Patient must be rendered euthyroid first with anti-thyroid drugs, Lugol's iodine, and beta-blockers.	surgery thyroidectomy subject:diabetes
0dbf11acd554a040	Basic	Corpus::Diabetes::Hyperthyroidism	What are the complications of thyroidectomy?	Patient becomes hypothyroid (expected). Rare: hypoparathyroidism, recurrent laryngeal nerve injury. Total thyroidectomy is now preferred over subtotal for Graves disease.	surgery complications subject:diabetes
ab54472c140abebe	Basic	Corpus::Diabetes::Hyperthyroidism	What is the GREAT score and what does it predict?	Clinical tool for estimating risk of relapse in Graves disease patients treated medically. Includes: age, thyroid hormone level, TSH receptor antibody level, goiter size (WHO classification). Helps determine likelihood of response to medical therapy.	scoring-system prognosis graves-disease subject:diabetes
479b51edbc3f9fae	Basic	Corpus::Diabetes::Hyperthyroidism	What is the management algorithm for Graves disease based on relapse risk?	Low relapse risk: medical management with carbimazole ~18 months; many undergo spontaneous remission. High relapse risk/low response chance: radioactive iodine (if small goiter, patient preference) or surgery (if large goiter, suspicious nodules, patient preference, orbitopathy).	management graves-disease subject:diabetes
37175a7ff9589bf4	Basic	Corpus::Diabetes::Hyperthyroidism	How is toxic multinodular goiter managed differently from Graves disease?	Medical treatment (carbimazole) controls but does not cure disease. Definitive therapy requires radioactive iodine or thyroidectomy. Choice depends on individual circumstances, patient preference, and indications like large goiter or compressive symptoms.	management nodular-goiter subject:diabetes
280debd7ce948ace	Basic	Corpus::Diabetes::Hyperthyroidism	Why are anti-thyroid drugs and radioactive iodine ineffective in inflammatory thyroid disease?	Inflammatory diseases have no thyroid autonomy; the inflammation itself causes hormone release. Anti-thyroid drugs (which block synthesis) and radioactive iodine (which destroys autonomous tissue) cannot address the inflammatory process.	thyroiditis treatment subject:diabetes
ded094ae458ae726	Basic	Corpus::Diabetes::Hyperthyroidism	What is Graves orbitopathy and what are the risk factors for development?	Inflammatory eye disease in Graves disease related to cross-reactivity of immune responses to thyroid and orbital antigens. Risk factors: cigarette smoking, severe hyperthyroidism, radioactive iodine, untreated/uncontrolled hypothyroidism.	orbitopathy graves-disease subject:diabetes
1669464d79834ab6	Basic	Corpus::Diabetes::Hyperthyroidism	How is activity of Graves eye disease assessed?	Grading system identifies if 3+ components present = active disease. Severity assessed as mild, moderate, or severe. Severity criteria: degree of lid retraction, exophthalmos, corneal dryness, presence/absence of diplopia.	orbitopathy assessment subject:diabetes
5a604203b056ef10	Basic	Corpus::Diabetes::Hyperthyroidism	What is sight-threatening Graves orbitopathy and how does it present?	Optic nerve compression or corneal breakdown risk, leading to significant visual impairment. Requires urgent therapy with surgical decompression and powerful immunosuppressive agents.	orbitopathy emergency subject:diabetes
2077409df19b0d31	Basic	Corpus::Diabetes::Hyperthyroidism	What is the stepped treatment approach for Graves orbitopathy by severity?	Mild: smoking cessation, normal thyroid function, topical lubricating gels/drops, oral selenium 6 months. Moderate: add IV methylprednisolone. Severe/site-threatening: IV methylprednisolone (12 weeks), surgical decompression, orbital radiation, immunosuppressive agents.	orbitopathy treatment subject:diabetes
2d4f1c78f30dffb4	Basic	Corpus::Diabetes::Hyperthyroidism	What is thyroid crisis (thyroid storm) and how is it treated?	Medical emergency with very high thyroid hormone levels. Diagnosis usually clinical. Treated in ICU/high-care with: high-dose carbimazole, Lugol's iodine, high-dose beta-blockers, IV hydrocortisone, treatment of atrial fibrillation. Alternatives if no response: lithium, potassium perchlorate, cholestyramine, plasma pheresis.	emergency thyroid-crisis subject:diabetes
e0bc9e57de1b9717	Basic	Corpus::Diabetes::Hypothyroidism	What is hypothyroidism?	A clinical syndrome resulting from inadequate tissue exposure to thyroid hormones, characterized by mental and physical lethargy, developing slowly.	definition pathophysiology subject:diabetes
f7603379da11a923	Basic	Corpus::Diabetes::Hypothyroidism	What is primary hypothyroidism?	Hypothyroidism due to disease of the thyroid gland itself (most common cause of hypothyroidism).	classification etiology subject:diabetes
55e1a56318a20c16	Basic	Corpus::Diabetes::Hypothyroidism	What is secondary hypothyroidism?	Hypothyroidism due to disease of the pituitary gland.	classification etiology subject:diabetes
752bbc4440b9cb88	Basic	Corpus::Diabetes::Hypothyroidism	What is tertiary hypothyroidism?	Hypothyroidism due to disease of the hypothalamus.	classification etiology subject:diabetes

Does not stimulate insulin secretion, so hypoglycemia does not occur with monotherapy.	diabetes treatment management Type1 Type2 pharmacology insulin difficulty:medium source:Treatment_Of_Diabetes_Mellitus_-_CMED4IIM1_-_2026_pages-001-010 subject:diabetes

11e30815b4834be3	Basic	Corpus::Endocrine::Adrenal Gland	What are the treatment options for pituitary Cushing's syndrome?	Surgery, radiotherapy, bromocriptine, or cabergoline. If these fail, bilateral adrenalectomy may be needed but carries risk of secondary adrenal insufficiency.	subject:endocrine
127ec024fc67bad5	Basic	Corpus::Endocrine::Adrenal Gland	How is adrenal Cushing's syndrome managed?	If adenoma: surgery. If carcinoma: surgery (if resectable), radiotherapy, mitotane, OPDDD, or enzyme blockers like aminoglutethimide.	subject:endocrine
f9fb4bee8e5badb2	Basic	Corpus::Endocrine::Adrenal Gland	What is medical adrenalectomy and when is it used?	Ketoconazole, used for ectopic ACTH Cushing's when the tumor cannot be located or is aggressive. Blocks cortisol synthesis.	subject:endocrine
23e08705d4eba259	Basic	Corpus::Endocrine::Adrenal Gland	What is mifepristone and its role in Cushing's syndrome?	Glucocorticoid receptor blocker used for severe cortisol-induced symptoms. Not widely available.	subject:endocrine
934c76af2187bf45	Basic	Corpus::Endocrine::Adrenal Gland	What is Nelson syndrome and how does it develop?	Enlargement of pituitary tumor with increased ACTH after bilateral adrenalectomy. Occurs when pituitary is the primary cause but was masked by high cortisol, causing hyperpigmentation and visual/cranial nerve symptoms.	subject:endocrine
682d654f62ccad10	Basic	Corpus::Endocrine::Adrenal Gland	How is Nelson syndrome treated?	Surgery to remove the enlarged pituitary tumor or dopamine agonists (bromocriptine, analogs, pasireotide).	subject:endocrine
3ed49132ad69c386	Basic	Corpus::Endocrine::Adrenal Gland	What is the prognosis of untreated Cushing's syndrome?	Fatal within 5 years if untreated. Death results from cardiovascular disease, diabetes, hypertension complications, or infection.	subject:endocrine
4d4ba08e8d5a24c6	Basic	Corpus::Endocrine::Adrenal Gland	What are the cure rates for treated Cushing's syndrome by cause?	Primary adrenal adenoma: 100% cure rate. Primary adrenal carcinoma: poor prognosis. Pituitary (Cushing's disease): 95% cure rate.	subject:endocrine
ca93998e59834e34	Basic	Corpus::Endocrine::Adrenal Gland	What indicates successful surgical treatment of Cushing's syndrome?	Serum cortisol becomes normal or low within 24 hours of successful intervention.	subject:endocrine
d8b3d659eaab9b32	Basic	Corpus::Endocrine::Adrenal Gland	What is primary hyperaldosteroneism?	Autonomous hypersecretion of aldosterone by the adrenal gland (zona glomerulosa) causing hypertension, hypokalemia, and metabolic alkalosis.	subject:endocrine
6eeacc5f5ab0fda3	Basic	Corpus::Endocrine::Adrenal Gland	What are the major causes of primary hyperaldosteroneism?	Aldosterone-producing adenoma (Conn's syndrome): 99% of cases, usually unilateral. Idiopathic hyperaldosteroneism (bilateral adrenal hyperplasia). Rarely carcinoma or glucocorticoid-remediable hyperaldosteroneism.	subject:endocrine
572e5b2f2a21f42c	Basic	Corpus::Endocrine::Adrenal Gland	Who is most commonly affected by primary hyperaldosteroneism?	More common in women than men. Typically age 30-50 years.	subject:endocrine
6e00fa9e522b9f5b	Basic	Corpus::Endocrine::Adrenal Gland	What are the key clinical features of primary hyperaldosteroneism?	Hypertension (mild to severe), hypokalemia effects (muscle weakness, myopathy, tetany), polyuria, polydipsia, NO edema (escape phenomenon), nocturia.	subject:endocrine
b3d1218ba53e5bd0	Basic	Corpus::Endocrine::Adrenal Gland	What is the significance of finding tetany with hypertension?	Tetany + hypertension in untreated patient is characteristic of Conn's syndrome (primary hyperaldosteroneism), indicating severe hypokalemia.	subject:endocrine
98f21220a3e7250c	Basic	Corpus::Endocrine::Adrenal Gland	Why is there no edema in primary hyperaldosteroneism?	Escape phenomenon occurs: initial sodium retention triggers volume expansion, which suppresses aldosterone, and natriuretic peptide is released to prevent edema.	subject:endocrine
58ed38eed5e3dfe2	Basic	Corpus::Endocrine::Adrenal Gland	What renal effects occur from mineralocorticoid excess?	Nephrogenic diabetes insipidus (inability to concentrate urine causing polyuria/polydipsia), vacuolar nephropathy, recurrent pyelonephritis, and possible kidney damage from hypertension.	subject:endocrine
b7db980a31495b01	Basic	Corpus::Endocrine::Adrenal Gland	What are the basal biochemical tests for primary hyperaldosteroneism?	Urine electrolytes (show hypokalemic alkalosis), plasma renin (decreased), plasma aldosterone (increased, measured as concentration or activity).	subject:endocrine
ecd53dff53922f5c	Basic	Corpus::Endocrine::Adrenal Gland	What provocative tests establish aldosterone non-suppressibility?	Saline infusion test or captopril test to confirm that aldosterone cannot be suppressed despite attempted suppression.	subject:endocrine
4debe5df5d609466	Basic	Corpus::Endocrine::Adrenal Gland	What radiological investigations are used?	CT of adrenal glands to determine if unilateral (adenoma) or bilateral (hyperplasia). Adrenal vein sampling may also be used.	subject:endocrine
56b6890705f13406	Basic	Corpus::Endocrine::Adrenal Gland	What aldosterone-to-renin ratio indicates primary hyperaldosteroneism?	If measuring plasma renin activity: ratio >50 with aldosterone >555 pmol/L. If measuring renin concentration: ratio >90.	subject:endocrine
42ba0d0242b8e83e	Basic	Corpus::Endocrine::Adrenal Gland	How is primary hyperaldosteroneism managed?	Adenoma: surgical removal. Idiopathic hyperaldosteroneism (bilateral hyperplasia): poorly responsive to surgery; use mineralocorticoid antagonists (spironolactone non-selective or eplerenone selective).	subject:endocrine
a7a429a16becc7e9	Basic	Corpus::Endocrine::Adrenal Gland	How do you differentiate primary from secondary hyperaldosteroneism?	Primary: low renin + high aldosterone + non-suppressible. Secondary (renal disease, liver, cardiac failure): both renin and aldosterone increased. Other causes: low renin + low aldosterone.	subject:endocrine
89145af8179b8ff7	Basic	Corpus::Endocrine::Adrenal Gland	What is pheochromocytoma?	Benign or malignant tumor of chromaffin cells (adrenal medulla = pheochromocytoma; extra-adrenal sympathetic ganglia = paraganglioma) causing excessive catecholamine secretion.	subject:endocrine
315ccb8f4bf65110	Basic	Corpus::Endocrine::Adrenal Gland	What percentage of secondary hypertension is caused by pheochromocytoma?	About 1% of secondary hypertension cases.	subject:endocrine
3f397f469a0f476e	Basic	Corpus::Endocrine::Adrenal Gland	What is the difference between pheochromocytoma and paraganglioma?	Pheochromocytoma: adrenal medulla origin. Paraganglioma: extra-adrenal chromaffin cell origin (sympathetic ganglia). Both can be called pheochromocytoma broadly.	subject:endocrine
573ce33ea6223f95	Basic	Corpus::Endocrine::Adrenal Gland	What percentage of pheochromocytomas are sporadic vs familial?	95% sporadic, <5% familial. Familial ones are often bilateral and associated with MEN 2A or 2B syndromes.	subject:endocrine
934ecc2e26716919	Basic	Corpus::Endocrine::Adrenal Gland	What is the rule of 10s for pheochromocytoma?	10% extra-adrenal, 10% malignant, 10% bilateral (especially in children), 10% occur in children. Note: this rule doesn't hold universally.	subject:endocrine
38b635b293835a86	Basic	Corpus::Endocrine::Adrenal Gland	What is the most common site of extra-adrenal pheochromocytoma?	Organs of Zuckerkandl (celiac and superior mesenteric ganglia), which lie between the inferior and superior mesenteric arteries. Other sites: thorax, bladder (~1% each), neck (<1%).	subject:endocrine
db29759e4cba507d	Basic	Corpus::Endocrine::Adrenal Gland	Which genes are associated with familial pheochromocytoma?	RET gene (MEN 2), NF1 gene (neurofibromatosis type 1), succinate dehydrogenase (SDH) mutations, and VHL gene.	subject:endocrine
df62aa6163cc09a2	Basic	Corpus::Endocrine::Adrenal Gland	What are the classic symptoms of pheochromocytoma?	Headache, sweating, palpitations. Now called the 5 Ps: Pressure (hypertension), Pain (headache), Perspiration (sweating), Palpitations, Pallor.	subject:endocrine
e385edecb2f4eed6	Basic	Corpus::Endocrine::Adrenal Gland	How is hypertension characterized in pheochromocytoma?	60% sustained, 40% paroxysmal. Often severe or malignant. Resistant to standard therapy (requires >5 agents). Half of sustained HTN patients also have paroxysms.	subject:endocrine
2f6ed768b0177f43	Basic	Corpus::Endocrine::Adrenal Gland	What precipitates paroxysmal crises in abdominal pheochromocytoma?	Any factor displacing abdominal contents: palpation of abdomen, exertion, or physical manipulation. Crises last 10-15 minutes to several hours.	subject:endocrine
108b9d8bc6b6a142	Basic	Corpus::Endocrine::Adrenal Gland	What symptoms occur during pheochromocytoma crises?	Headache, sweating, palpitations, pallor, apprehension, nausea, vomiting, abdominal pain, tachycardia, angina, dyspnea. 1/3 have glycosuria only during attack; 1/3 have HTN only during attack.	subject:endocrine
6690badeafda3b43	Basic	Corpus::Endocrine::Adrenal Gland	Why can pheochromocytoma cause postural hypotension?	Occurs with predominantly epinephrine-secreting tumors. Due to decreased plasma volume and blunted sympathetic response.	subject:endocrine
79a3042112ec77b3	Basic	Corpus::Endocrine::Adrenal Gland	What other complications can pheochromocytoma cause? 78efad1380313031	Basic	Corpus::Endocrine::Adrenal Gland	What biochemical test is the gold standard for pheochromocytoma?	Plasma free metanephrine (gold standard, most sensitive). Alternative: 24-hour urine metanephrine.	subject:endocrine

dfa15b5bed5485d5	Basic	Corpus::Endocrine::Disorders Of The Pituitary Gland - CMEDIIM1 - 2026	What is the anatomical basis of cranial diabetes insipidus?	ADH is produced in the hypothalamus (paraventricular and supraoptic nuclei), travels via the hypothalamic-pituitary tract, and is stored in the posterior pituitary before release. Damage at any of these sites (hypothalamus, tract, or posterior pituitary) can cause cranial diabetes insipidus.	subject:endocrine

One-third due to tumors (hypothalamic or pituitary), one-third idiopathic, and one-third miscellaneous (infections, trauma, etc.).	subject:endocrine

Otherwise, ADH deficiency typically occurs with other anterior pituitary hormone deficiencies.	subject:endocrine
