c1b08d7451120cdf	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::An Approach To Ascites	What is ascites?	Ascites is defined as the pathologic accumulation of fluid within the peritoneal cavity. It is important to distinguish ascites from other causes of abdominal swelling, which can be remembered by the acronym 'six ifs': fluid, fat, flatus, fetus, feces, and fatal growth.	ascites definition diagnosis subject:gastroenterology
daeefd0890b5f959	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::An Approach To Ascites	What is the most common cause of ascites?	Portal hypertension related to liver cirrhosis is the most common cause of ascites, accounting for 75-80% of cases. The mechanism is increased hydrostatic pressure or venous hypertension.	ascites etiology portal-hypertension subject:gastroenterology
63c1f6fbe12b2365	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::An Approach To Ascites	What are the major pathophysiologic mechanisms for ascites formation?	Four main mechanisms: (1) Increased hydrostatic pressure from portal hypertension (cirrhosis, hepatic vein thrombosis, IVC obstruction, constrictive pericarditis, congestive heart failure); (2) Decreased colloidal osmotic pressure (nephrotic syndrome, malnutrition, protein-losing enteropathy); (3) Increased permeability from peritoneal disease (tuberculosis peritonitis, bacterial peritonitis, malignancy metastases); (4) Leakage of fluid from organs (biliary leak, pancreatic ascites, chylous ascites, urinary ascites).	ascites pathophysiology mechanisms subject:gastroenterology
aa6acc534d857e8e	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::An Approach To Ascites	How is ascites graded by severity?	Ascites is graded as: Grade 1 - detectable only by ultrasound examination; Grade 2 - moderate symmetrical distension of the abdomen manifested clinically; Grade 3 - marked abdominal distension with large volume of gross ascites.	ascites classification severity subject:gastroenterology
7ee867227a4d7c42	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::An Approach To Ascites	What is the difference between complicated and uncomplicated ascites?	Uncomplicated ascites is fluid in the abdomen that is not infected and not associated with complications like hepatorenal syndrome. Refractory ascites is ascites that does not disappear even after satisfactory medical treatment and large volume paracentesis. Diuretic-resistant ascites means the patient has no response to intensive diuretic therapy or develops intolerable side effects.	ascites classification complications subject:gastroenterology
f149a25fcc804ebe	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::An Approach To Ascites	What are the clinical symptoms of ascites?	Patients with small amounts of fluid (Grade 1) are usually asymptomatic. As ascites progresses, patients develop abdominal distension, discomfort, anorexia, nausea, and heartburn. Tension ascites can cause respiratory symptoms due to compression on the diaphragm and lungs, presenting as dyspnea and respiratory distress. Early satiety occurs due to pressure effects from increasing fluid.	ascites symptoms presentation subject:gastroenterology
c415bb217a9d61ad	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::An Approach To Ascites	What are the clinical signs of ascites?	Specific signs of ascites include: inverted umbilicus, bulging of the flanks, shifting dullness, and fluid thrill on percussion. Note: absence of dullness does not exclude ascites in early cases. Associated signs depend on etiology - cirrhosis signs include jaundice, gynecomastia, clubbing, pedal edema, palmar erythema with spider telangiectasia, and splenic enlargement.	ascites physical-examination signs subject:gastroenterology
0cd6b5e3aa77d81c	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::An Approach To Ascites	What complications of ascites should be examined for?	Complications include umbilical hernia due to pressure effects and weakening of abdominal muscles, and hepatic hydrothorax where fluid tracks into the lungs, causing plural effusion (usually on the right side) with profound dyspnea.	ascites complications examination subject:gastroenterology
f9492f94c6285922	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::An Approach To Ascites	What imaging modality is preferred to confirm ascites?	Ultrasound is the imaging modality of choice to confirm the presence of ascites. It is cost-effective, can provide information about possible etiology, assess the liver, evaluate the portal venous system, and is essential for guiding paracentesis safely. Chest radiographs are useful when complications like hepatic hydrothorax are suspected.	ascites investigation ultrasound subject:gastroenterology
9c0f28d726f64b5c	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::An Approach To Ascites	Why is paracentesis important in ascites evaluation?	Abdominal paracentesis is of paramount importance because it provides both therapeutic benefit (especially for tense ascites or refractory ascites) and diagnostic information. Samples should be sent for macroscopic appearance, cell count, microscopy, culture, albumin levels, and serum ascitic albumin gradient (SAG) calculation. At bedside, if bacterial peritonitis is suspected, 10 mL of fluid should be inoculated into both aerobic and anaerobic blood culture bottles before antibiotics are given.	ascites paracentesis diagnostic-procedure subject:gastroenterology
cedcf09aa4f271a9	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::An Approach To Ascites	What does the macroscopic appearance of ascitic fluid indicate?	Clear fluid indicates uncomplicated ascites. Turbid/cloudy fluid may indicate spontaneous bacterial peritonitis (SBP). Milky fluid indicates chylous ascites (from lymphatic vessel damage with elevated triglycerides). Bloody fluid may indicate traumatic tap or malignancy. Brown fluid may indicate bile leak from biliary tree perforation. Black fluid is rare and seen with malignant melanoma.	ascites paracentesis fluid-analysis subject:gastroenterology
49822418e185e638	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::An Approach To Ascites	What is the serum ascitic albumin gradient (SAG) and how is it calculated?	The SAG is calculated by subtracting ascitic albumin from serum albumin. SAG is the most useful test to distinguish causes of ascites. A high SAG (≥1.1 g/dL or ≥11 g/L) indicates portal hypertension in most cases. A low SAG (<1.1 g/dL or <11 g/L) suggests other causes like tuberculosis, serositis, nephrotic syndrome, or peritoneal metastases. The SAG should always be ordered when ascitic fluid is obtained.	ascites serum-gradient diagnostic-test subject:gastroenterology
50730c9dd7ce46ae	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::An Approach To Ascites	What is spontaneous bacterial peritonitis (SBP) and how is it diagnosed?	SBP is a complication of ascites, usually in cirrhosis patients, caused by translocation of gut bacteria into the peritoneal space. Patients present with pain and worsening ascites. Diagnosis requires: neutrophil cell count >250 cells/mm³ on fluid analysis. Cultures may grow E. coli, Klebsiella, or Streptococcus (common gastrointestinal bacteria). Fluid should be inoculated into blood culture bottles at bedside before antibiotics are administered.	ascites spontaneous-bacterial-peritonitis complications subject:gastroenterology
ae5038246ffd0a17	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::An Approach To Ascites	What does lymphocyte-predominant ascitic fluid indicate?	Lymphocyte-predominant fluid may indicate tuberculosis peritonitis or peritoneal carcinomatosis from metastatic cancer. The adenosine deaminase (ADA) level is a useful test - if ADA is elevated above 10 (typically >30 in TB), it has >90% sensitivity for TB peritonitis and helps distinguish between TB and other causes of low-gradient ascites with lymphocyte predominance.	ascites cell-count tuberculosis subject:gastroenterology
18a9593e811f2a30	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::An Approach To Ascites	What is the pathophysiology of ascites in cirrhosis and portal hypertension?	In cirrhosis, hepatic architecture is distorted with increased hepatic vascular tone, causing portal hypertension. This activates vasodilation mechanisms mediated by nitric oxide overproduction, which dilates splanchnic and peripheral vessels. This causes underfilling of the systemic arterial space and triggers baroreceptor activation, increasing renin-angiotensin-aldosterone system (RAAS) and antidiuretic hormone (ADH) release. Sodium and water retention develops, leading to ascites. Simultaneously, splanchnic vasodilation increases lymph production, which exceeds transport capacity and leaks into the peritoneal cavity.	ascites pathophysiology cirrhosis subject:gastroenterology
e059be13ece39d2e	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::An Approach To Ascites	What is the first step in managing ascites?	The first step is to diagnose the underlying cause of ascites, as treatment should target the etiology. For TB peritonitis, TB treatment is needed. For malignancy, chemotherapy may help. For heart failure, diuretics are required. For portal hypertension from cirrhosis, treatment aims to minimize ascites formation since cirrhosis cannot be reversed.	ascites management etiology subject:gastroenterology
57e4605de920214d	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::An Approach To Ascites	What dietary modifications are used in ascites management?	Sodium restriction is the cornerstone of dietary management for ascites due to portal hypertension. Patients should be restricted to approximately 2 grams of sodium per day. This requires patient counseling and dietitian involvement, as sodium is found in table salt, processed foods, and various food additives.	ascites management diet subject:gastroenterology
9a19c32b47b45f8d	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::An Approach To Ascites	What is spironolactone and why is it used in ascites?	Spironolactone is an aldosterone antagonist used as first-line diuretic therapy for ascites. It blocks the renin-angiotensin-aldosterone system by inhibiting sodium reabsorption in the renal collecting duct, thus promoting sodium and water excretion. Starting dose is 100 mg daily, titrated up to a maximum of 400 mg daily. Side effects include fluctuations in potassium, sodium, and creatinine (requiring electrolyte/renal function monitoring), muscle cramps, and gynecomatia (even with early/long-term use).	ascites management spironolactone subject:gastroenterology
fe105ff1d96e5361	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::An Approach To Ascites	What is furosemide and how is it used in ascites management?	Furosemide is a loop diuretic used as second-line or adjunct therapy for ascites. Starting dose is approximately 40 mg daily, titrated up to a maximum of 160 mg daily. It must be used cautiously as high doses can cause significant electrolyte abnormalities. Close monitoring of electrolytes is essential.	ascites management furosemide subject:gastroenterology
4b3147738fd7626b	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::An Approach To Ascites	What are the principles of diuretic dose adjustment in ascites?	Once ascites mobilizes and clinically improves (no longer apparent), diuretic doses should be titrated down to the maximum tolerated dose that maintains symptom control. Patients should not be kept on high doses for prolonged periods once improved. Daily weight monitoring helps assess treatment response. Aim for 0.5 kg weight loss per day if ascites alone is present, or up to 1 kg per day if peripheral edema is also present.	ascites management monitoring subject:gastroenterology
41718807f160b6a7	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::An Approach To Ascites	When is large volume paracentesis indicated?	Large volume paracentesis is indicated for: (1) Tense ascites causing respiratory compromise - to relieve dyspnea and respiratory distress; (2) Refractory ascites that fails to respond to medical management. Large volume paracentesis becomes part of the treatment strategy for refractory ascites.	ascites paracentesis indications subject:gastroenterology
782235f801b73e95	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::An Approach To Ascites	What precautions must be taken during large volume paracentesis?	To prevent hemodynamic disturbances and renal impairment during fluid removal, intravenous albumin infusion should be administered to provide intravascular volume replacement. Specifically, 8 grams of albumin should be given for each liter of fluid drained, especially when removing fluid volumes exceeding 5 liters.	ascites paracentesis albumin-replacement subject:gastroenterology
915d69c3c25497f0	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::An Approach To Ascites	What is TIPS and how does it work in refractory ascites?	TIPS (Transjugular Intrahepatic Portosystemic Shunt) is an intervention for refractory ascites when patients fail medical therapy including large volume paracentesis. A shunt is placed between the portal vein and hepatic vein, providing an escape route for fluid from the portal system into the venous system, thereby reducing portal pressures and complications including ascites. A concern is precipitation of hepatic encephalopathy, though newer covered stents have reduced this complication compared to older stents.	ascites refractory-ascites TIPS subject:gastroenterology
1041cd5ccc57343f	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::An Approach To Ascites	What is the role of liver transplantation in ascites management?	Liver transplantation should always be considered in patients with cirrhosis failing to respond to ascites management. Such patients should be referred to the appropriate liver transplant center.	ascites refractory-ascites transplantation subject:gastroenterology
7ef1c9fd0b93dbed	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::An Approach To Ascites	What are the key points to remember about ascites classification?	The terms 'exudate' and 'transudate' are not used for ascitic fluid analysis (unlike pleural fluid analysis). Instead, ascites is classified based on the serum ascitic albumin gradient to determine etiology. Understanding the grading system (Grade 1, 2, 3) and classification (complicated vs uncomplicated) is important for clinical management.	ascites classification terminology subject:gastroenterology
9e39d19233012487	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::An Approach To Ascites	What is the take-home message about ascites epidemiology and management?	Remember: (1) 75-80% of ascites is due to portal hypertension; (2) The initial diagnostic test is the serum ascitic albumin gradient; (3) High SAG indicates portal hypertension; (4) Multiple treatment options exist ranging from sodium restriction and diuretics to large volume paracentesis and TIPS in refractory cases; (5) Always consider the underlying etiology when managing ascites.	ascites epidemiology management-summary subject:gastroenterology

The spleen is not actually hyperactive (hypersplenism) in portal hypertension. Rather, it undergoes splenic sequestration—the organ enlarges and pools more platelets and red blood cells, explaining declining platelet counts in progressive cirrhosis.	subject:gastroenterology
b0a38aec2b9fd800	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::Cirrhosis  Liver Failuer	What is the significance of a declining platelet count in chronic liver disease?	A gradual decline in platelet count may indicate progression toward cirrhosis and development of portal hypertension that may not yet be clinically apparent.	subject:gastroenterology
4bb2ce4be4d8c40a	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::Cirrhosis  Liver Failuer	What characterizes ascites in portal hypertension?	Ascites in portal hypertension is typically a high gradient serum-ascites albumin gradient (SAAG), reflecting the high pressure gradient between portal and systemic circulation.	subject:gastroenterology
7a30a3e6d83e8711	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::Cirrhosis  Liver Failuer	What are the three categories of portal hypertension based on location?	Pre-hepatic (obstruction before the liver, e.g., splenic vein thrombosis, schistosomiasis), intra-hepatic (within the liver, e.g., cirrhosis), and post-hepatic (obstruction after the liver, e.g., constrictive pericarditis, IVC obstruction, Budd-Chiari syndrome).	subject:gastroenterology
6461bcc12315086f	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::Cirrhosis  Liver Failuer	What percentage of hepatic blood supply comes from the portal vein vs hepatic artery?	The portal vein provides 75-80% of the hepatic blood supply, while the hepatic artery provides 20-25%.	subject:gastroenterology
4be929d826bd8d9e	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::Cirrhosis  Liver Failuer	What are the major complications of advanced cirrhosis?	Major complications include esophageal varices with bleeding, hepatic encephalopathy, spontaneous bacterial peritonitis, hepatorenal syndrome, hepatopulmonary syndrome, and portal pulmonary hypertension.	subject:gastroenterology
1e16cfe0db9fd6e3	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::Cirrhosis  Liver Failuer	What are the major causes of mortality in cirrhosis?	The major causes are variceal bleeds (esophageal varices) and infections, particularly spontaneous bacterial peritonitis (SBP), which can precipitate hepatic encephalopathy.	subject:gastroenterology
25ad405acd3c2817	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::Cirrhosis  Liver Failuer	What is the endoscopic treatment for esophageal varices?	Esophageal varices are treated with variceal rubber band ligation (VRBL). The varices are suctioned into the endoscope hood and a rubber band is deployed around them. Patients should also receive non-selective beta-blockers (e.g., carvedilol).	subject:gastroenterology
4adfeb6e67f484e0	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::Cirrhosis  Liver Failuer	What is the clinical significance of hepatic encephalopathy in cirrhosis?	Hepatic encephalopathy in an already-decompensated cirrhotic patient is associated with significant mortality. It requires counseling of family members as there is substantial risk of death on each hospital admission.	subject:gastroenterology
0d9861db19f0ff68	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::Cirrhosis  Liver Failuer	What are the key precipitants of hepatic encephalopathy that must be actively sought?	Key precipitants include infections (especially SBP), variceal bleeds, dehydration, electrolyte abnormalities, hepatotoxic drugs (including sedatives), alcohol consumption, and renal failure.	subject:gastroenterology
bfa02d9b85d9411a	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::Cirrhosis  Liver Failuer	What is the diagnostic threshold for spontaneous bacterial peritonitis?	SBP is diagnosed when the ascitic fluid has >250 PMN (polymorphonuclear neutrophils) per mm3. This criterion is sensitive, and antibiotics with gram-negative coverage should be initiated empirically. Blood cultures can increase specificity.	subject:gastroenterology
ba11e4415a78af85	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::Cirrhosis  Liver Failuer	How should vitamin K be managed in cirrhotic patients with elevated INR?	While an initial IV dose of vitamin K may be given if INR is elevated, repeated doses over several days are of no benefit due to impaired synthesis of coagulation factors. An elevated INR in cirrhosis reflects synthetic dysfunction and is not corrected by vitamin K alone.	subject:gastroenterology
6e19d7e854bfbc5a	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::Cirrhosis  Liver Failuer	What is the relationship between hepatitis B and hepatocellular carcinoma?	Hepatitis B virus is directly oncogenic and can cause HCC even in the absence of cirrhosis, though most patients with hepatitis B-associated HCC have underlying cirrhosis. Hepatitis B vaccination can prevent this scenario.	subject:gastroenterology

21e9c08776a93205	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::Cirrhosis  Liver Failuer	Where can varices develop in cirrhosis?	While esophageal varices are the most common and clinically important, varices can develop anywhere in the GI tract including the duodenum, stomach (gastric varices are rare), and rectum (anorectal varices).	subject:gastroenterology
7d4041e02489a288	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::Cirrhosis  Liver Failuer	What does a very high AFP (alpha-fetoprotein) suggest in the context of cirrhosis?	A very high AFP is a tumor marker for hepatocellular carcinoma and can be quite specific and sensitive for HCC, especially when elevated substantially. Patients with cirrhosis should ultimately be evaluated for liver transplantation, which may be the definitive treatment for advanced disease.	subject:gastroenterology
bb7c167a10513c1e	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::Gastro-Oesophageal Reflux Disease  Peptic Ulcer Disease	What is gastro-esophageal reflux disease (GERD)?	A condition that results from failure of the normal anti-reflux mechanism to protect against frequent and abnormal amounts of gastro-esophageal reflux. GERD (United States terminology) or GORD (UK English) is one of the most common human diseases worldwide.	subject:gastroenterology
fee34dec4893db26	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::Gastro-Oesophageal Reflux Disease  Peptic Ulcer Disease	What is the key difference between normal gastric reflux and GERD?	Occasional acid reflux is a normal physiological process (e.g., after large meals). GERD develops when reflux of gastric contents causes troublesome symptoms or complications that impact quality of life. It is patient-centered diagnosis based on symptom severity.	subject:gastroenterology
a885a56210e22718	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::Gastro-Oesophageal Reflux Disease  Peptic Ulcer Disease	What are the defensive factors that prevent GERD?	The lower esophageal sphincter and diaphragm (internal and external sphincters coordinating similar to anal sphincters), esophageal acid clearance via peristalsis (coordinated contractions), and esophageal tissue resistance.	subject:gastroenterology
571ec9a4aa2f9f6a	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::Gastro-Oesophageal Reflux Disease  Peptic Ulcer Disease	What are the aggressive factors that promote GERD?	Gastric acidity, volume of gastric contents, and duodenal contents. 3773a5ee910c0ae8	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::Gastro-Oesophageal Reflux Disease  Peptic Ulcer Disease	What are the classic reflux symptoms?	Burning retrosternal pain (heartburn) and sensation of acid coming up into the throat. These are the most frequent presenting complaints in patients with GERD.	subject:gastroenterology
ae562ca09db9cab8	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::Gastro-Oesophageal Reflux Disease  Peptic Ulcer Disease	What are atypical symptoms of GERD?	Water brash, odynophagia, and dysphagia. These symptoms have other important causes and if present should prompt further investigation beyond simple GERD diagnosis to rule out other pathology.	subject:gastroenterology
2fb0ca66a58f94d6	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::Gastro-Oesophageal Reflux Disease  Peptic Ulcer Disease	What is the Montreal Classification system?	A classification defining esophageal syndromes: (1) Symptomatic syndrome (heartburn, acid regurgitation), (2) Reflux chest pain syndrome, (3) Syndromes associated with esophageal injury (erosions, strictures, Barrett esophagus), and (4) Extra-esophageal syndromes (reflux asthma, laryngitis - though associations not well-validated).	subject:gastroenterology
67836f3ee35b40a1	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::Gastro-Oesophageal Reflux Disease  Peptic Ulcer Disease	When does Barrett esophagus develop?	Long-standing, poorly treated GERD can cause the normal squamous epithelium of the esophagus to change to columnar epithelium. This is a pre-neoplastic lesion that increases risk for esophageal adenocarcinoma.	subject:gastroenterology
c3d71b9fcfd83870	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::Gastro-Oesophageal Reflux Disease  Peptic Ulcer Disease	What is the 2006 definition of reflux disease?	Gastro-esophageal reflux disease develops when reflux of gastric contents causes troublesome symptoms or complications. This shifted diagnosis from physician-driven to patient-centered, based on symptoms affecting quality of life (work, home, leisure).	subject:gastroenterology
3c8aec29b323b225	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::Gastro-Oesophageal Reflux Disease  Peptic Ulcer Disease	What is the 2018 Lyon Consensus view on GERD diagnosis?	History, structured questionnaires, and response to treatment are insufficient for conclusive GERD diagnosis. fa4f4565a39163f2	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::Gastro-Oesophageal Reflux Disease  Peptic Ulcer Disease	What is esophageal pH monitoring?	A pH probe is placed in the lower esophagus for 24-hour monitoring. It records pH readings and patient-triggered events. Measures esophageal contraction and lower esophageal sphincter function. Useful to exclude achalasia (failure of lower esophageal relaxation), assess esophageal motility, and evaluate candidates for anti-reflux surgery.	subject:gastroenterology

The R-value is a formula used to classify patients into cholestatic vs hepatitic patterns of LFT. Originally developed primarily for drug-induced liver injury research, but is increasingly being used clinically for various etiologies.	subject:gastroenterology
6754c301d4628efc	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::Jaundice	What imaging modalities are available for evaluating jaundice?	Transabdominal ultrasound is the standard starting point. Additional modalities include CT scan, endoscopic ultrasound (EUS), magnetic resonance cholangiopancreatogram (MRCP), ERCP (performed with therapeutic intent), and sometimes liver biopsy for specific diagnoses like autoimmune hepatitis.	subject:gastroenterology
f1bb9613cd87eca8	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::Jaundice	Why is ERCP only performed with therapeutic intent?	ERCP carries significant risks including acute pancreatitis, bowel injury, and hemorrhage. It is not routinely used for diagnosis alone but rather when intervention is needed, such as stent placement for strictures or stone extraction.	subject:gastroenterology
8f43057394e3884d	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::Jaundice	What is fibroscan and what techniques does it use?	Fibroscan is an ultrasound-based technique that assesses organ stiffness to determine if a patient is progressing toward cirrhosis. Uses vibration-controlled elastography or shear wave elastography. Increasingly standard in liver disease management.	subject:gastroenterology
924c584e7ec43857	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::Jaundice	What does cholestasis mean in terms of lab findings?	Cholestasis means an increase in alkaline phosphatase and gamma-glutamyl transferase, with or without an increase in bilirubin. Cholestasis can exist without jaundice if bilirubin is not elevated.	subject:gastroenterology
8c3abdd14d1cc10a	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::Jaundice	What is the key distinction between intrahepatic and extrahepatic cholestasis?	Intrahepatic cholestasis: obstruction at the level of the liver parenchyma, not visible on imaging (ultrasound/CT). Extrahepatic cholestasis: obstruction in the bile duct system outside the liver, typically shows bile duct dilatation on imaging.	subject:gastroenterology
256817b62b8f3d60	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::Jaundice	What are causes of acute hepatic jaundice?	Causes include viral hepatitis, drug-induced liver injury, alcohol, autoimmune hepatitis, aminita (death cap mushroom), Wilson's disease (copper overload from ATP-7B gene mutation), and hemolytic causes.	subject:gastroenterology
f13e678a256b999d	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::Jaundice	Why are Wilson's disease and hereditary hemochromatosis important to diagnose?	These are among the few etiologies of jaundice that are reversible or can be stabilized with treatment if diagnosed early. Wilson's disease involves copper overload; hemochromatosis involves iron overload.	subject:gastroenterology
9e66b15cb5ce7d8d	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::Jaundice	What causes of chronic hepatic jaundice should be considered?	Cirrhosis from various etiologies, non-alcoholic fatty liver disease (NAFLD, now called steatotic liver disease), alpha-1 antitrypsin deficiency, large tumor burden in liver, hepatic ischemia from hepatic vein or IVC occlusion (Budd-Chiari syndrome).	subject:gastroenterology
a90251da3cea635e	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::Jaundice	What are causes of intrahepatic cholestasis?	Include primary sclerosing cholangitis (PSC), AIDS-cholangiopathy, drug-induced liver injury, progressive familial intrahepatic cholestasis, benign recurrent intrahepatic cholestasis, infiltrative etiologies (granulomas, sarcoid, tumors), and primary biliary cholangitis.	subject:gastroenterology
c1a4f6d086983fe5	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::Jaundice	What infiltrative etiologies can cause intrahepatic cholestasis?	Granulomas from tuberculosis or sarcoidosis, and tumors (either primary hepatic tumors or secondary spread from lymphoma, colorectal cancer). These cause cholestasis by infiltrating the liver parenchyma with focal lesions.	subject:gastroenterology
cd0e20be81a3178f	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::Jaundice	What are causes of extrahepatic cholestasis?	Include strictures (benign or malignant), PSC strictures, pancreatic cancer, chronic pancreatitis, choledocholithiasis (bile duct stones), lymph nodes obstructing bile ducts, ascaris worms (rare), and iatrogenic bile duct injury during gallbladder surgery.	subject:gastroenterology
57e8f284b5aca7b1	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::Jaundice	What imaging findings indicate extrahepatic cholestasis?	Bile duct dilatation is the classic sign. Specific lesions include stones (hyperechoic with acoustic shadowing on ultrasound), strictures, and masses. Absence of dilatation does not exclude obstruction, especially with small lesions or early/shifting stones.	subject:gastroenterology
b61d24c765f8227e	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::Jaundice	What are the features of primary sclerosing cholangitis (PSC) on imaging?	Characteristic "onion skin" ring fibrosis pattern on histology. Imaging shows irregular stricturing of intrahepatic bile ducts. Can affect intrahepatic and/or extrahepatic bile ducts. Multiple irregular areas of stricturing with focal dilation (beaded appearance).	subject:gastroenterology
87a84c177e2410f3	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::Jaundice	Why is MRCP sufficient for PSC diagnosis?	MRCP provides excellent visualization of the bile duct anatomy and is sufficient for PSC diagnosis. ERCP is reserved for therapeutic interventions like stricture ballooning, as it adds unnecessary procedural risk without additional diagnostic benefit.	subject:gastroenterology
00faf84b79fedd49	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::Jaundice	What systemic signs can accompany primary biliary cholangitis and PSC?	Xanthomas and xanthelasmas can be seen in patients with long-standing cholestasis. Malabsorption of fat-soluble vitamins may occur due to impaired bile flow.	subject:gastroenterology
9f7487d37af1df06	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::Jaundice	What scoring systems assess hepatic function and cirrhosis severity?	Child-Pugh score assesses liver synthetic function and includes INR, albumin, and bilirubin. MELD score (Model for End-stage Liver Disease) is more commonly used to stratify patients for transplant listing and assess severity of hepatic dysfunction.	subject:gastroenterology
0482872ab925f681	Basic	Corpus::Gastroenterology::Jaundice	What is the systematic approach to a patient with jaundice?	Collection: gather history, examination findings, and appropriate investigations. Analysis: reflect on data, consider anatomy, physiology, and likely pathology. Synthesis: determine the most likely cause (clinical assessment). Action: proceed with definitive investigation (MRCP, biopsy) or treatment as indicated.	subject:gastroenterology

Motor coordination (frontal lobe, motor cortex, basal ganglia, cerebellar integration), sensory inputs (visual, vestibular, proprioception), biomechanics (skeletal integrity, joint stability, muscle strength).	subject:geriatric

c7fb46545d8db9e1	Basic	Corpus::Geriatric::Geriatric - Falls	How do musculoskeletal problems contribute to falls?	Pain, deformity, or loss of function in joints, bones, and feet increase fall risk.	subject:geriatric

3aac4a8b6ea68c40	Basic	Corpus::Geriatric::Geriatric - Falls	How do medications increase fall risk?	Drugs can cause sedation, dizziness, confusion, affecting the central nervous system and balance.	subject:geriatric
f7c34c2ae9133956	Basic	Corpus::Geriatric::Geriatric - Falls	How does stroke increase fall risk?	Stroke can cause hemiplegia or hemiparesis, which impairs motor control and balance.	subject:geriatric

Nervous system (confusion, seizures, weakness, hallucinations), cardiovascular system (chest pain, orthostatic symptoms), musculoskeletal (joint pain), and vision.	subject:geriatric

756517997ee695fe	Basic	Corpus::Geriatric::Geriatric - Falls	What is the Timed Up and Go test and what does it measure?	Patient sits, stands without holding on, walks 3 meters, turns, and sits down. Assesses proximal muscle strength, gait, arm swing, turning ability, and walking speed.	subject:geriatric
ef63778658e2ed3d	Basic	Corpus::Geriatric::Geriatric - Falls	What Timed Up and Go test times indicate fall risk?	Less than 10 seconds indicates freely mobile with lower fall risk. More than 30 seconds indicates impaired mobility and higher fall risk.	subject:geriatric
3294c9738f5c9cf7	Basic	Corpus::Geriatric::Geriatric - Falls	What is the significance of steps taken during turning in fall assessment?	Less than 3 steps to turn indicates lower fall risk. More than 3 steps indicates higher risk of falling.	subject:geriatric
ffd9505b90413317	Basic	Corpus::Geriatric::Geriatric - Falls	What types of exercise are beneficial for fall prevention?	Tai Chi and Yoga (likely referred to as "Rekhae") improve muscle strength and postural reflexes.	subject:geriatric

57085f1543681862	Basic	Corpus::Geriatric::Geriatric - Falls	What is the role of the medical team in fall management?	Manage contributory medical problems, conduct medication review, and stop medications that increase fall risk.	subject:geriatric

6ef73094d1c39d98	Basic	Corpus::Geriatric::GeriatriC - Frailty	What physiological change occurs from birth until age 35?	All body systems and organs develop and reach peak function at age 35. Peak function depends on lifestyle factors like exercise and smoking status.	subject:geriatric
a99843fce0b7967b	Basic	Corpus::Geriatric::GeriatriC - Frailty	What happens to physiological function after age 35?	There is a decline in physiological function after age 35. The rate of decline depends on the peak function achieved - those with higher peaks decline less severely than those with lower peaks.	subject:geriatric
7400513131d91aee	Basic	Corpus::Geriatric::GeriatriC - Frailty	Define frailty.	Frailty is defined as the loss of a patient's ability to extend minor environmental stresses because of reduced reserve in the physiological function of several organ systems.	subject:geriatric
8294c44de83f3f64	Basic	Corpus::Geriatric::GeriatriC - Frailty	What is a physiological reserve line in frailty?	The physiological reserve line is the level below which patients cannot function normally. When physiological decline passes this reserve line, patients are deemed frail and have high risk of disability.	subject:geriatric
8895dfb0202fe024	Basic	Corpus::Geriatric::GeriatriC - Frailty	How do robust individuals respond to stressors?	Robust individuals (without frailty) experience a dip in physiological function when exposed to stressors (infection, fall, trauma, surgery), but recover back to their baseline.	subject:geriatric
221ba802cff81db0	Basic	Corpus::Geriatric::GeriatriC - Frailty	How do pre-frail individuals respond to stressors?	Pre-frail individuals experience a dip in physiological function when exposed to stressors, but do not fully recover back to their baseline after recovery.	subject:geriatric
6aef572e75d156ca	Basic	Corpus::Geriatric::GeriatriC - Frailty	How do frail individuals respond to stressors?	Frail individuals hardly recover back to their baseline after exposure to stressors and typically become dependent, often unable to return home after hospitalization and requiring institutional care.	subject:geriatric
cdc28885c02e45aa	Basic	Corpus::Geriatric::GeriatriC - Frailty	What is sarcopenia and its relationship to frailty?	Sarcopenia is reduced muscle strength and is a key feature of frailty. Frailty is associated with poor health outcomes.	subject:geriatric
6541ffe05ac2eac9	Basic	Corpus::Geriatric::GeriatriC - Frailty	What are the five components of the Cardiovascular Health Study (CHS) frailty phenotype?	The five components are: (1) unintentional weight loss >5% in previous year, (2) weakness measured by hand grip strength, (3) self-reported exhaustion or poor endurance, (4) slowness (timed 3-meter walk), and (5) low physical activity (self-reported).	subject:geriatric
54e061825f0d0e2c	Basic	Corpus::Geriatric::GeriatriC - Frailty	How is frailty classified using the CHS frailty phenotype?	Patients meeting 3 or more of the 5 CHS criteria are regarded as frail. Patients with 1-2 criteria are classified as pre-frail.	subject:geriatric
6cfa38f2856875e2	Basic	Corpus::Geriatric::GeriatriC - Frailty	What are the criteria for the Osteoporotic Fracture (OF) Frailty Index?	The OF Frailty Index assesses: (1) weight loss >5% over two-year period (regardless of intent), (2) inability to rise from a chair 5 times without using arms, and (3) reduced energy levels. Patients with 2 or more criteria are regarded as frail.	subject:geriatric
cc71743f8c4e0930	Basic	Corpus::Geriatric::GeriatriC - Frailty	What is the Frailty Index or Deficit Accumulation method?	The Frailty Index involves selecting 30 health deficits (symptoms, signs, diseases, disabilities, abnormal labs/imaging/ECGs) and counting how many the patient has. The score is calculated by dividing the number of deficits present by 30. A score ≥0.25 indicates frailty.	subject:geriatric
c7d3f8643ade7066	Basic	Corpus::Geriatric::GeriatriC - Frailty	What is the Frailty Scale classification system?	The Frailty Scale classifies patients by functional ability: 1 = very fit; 5 = mild frail (need help with instrumental ADLs like transport, household chores, medication, finances); 9 = terminally ill (life expectancy <6 months).	subject:geriatric
76307bc1b6607260	Basic	Corpus::Geriatric::GeriatriC - Frailty	What are the two main pathways to frailty?	Primary frailty: genetic factors, atrocities, chronic inflammation, low exercise, malnutrition. Secondary frailty: chronic inflammation, genetic factors, low exercise leading to clinical disease, which then causes frailty and disability.	subject:geriatric
4749bbf3b4d34c86	Basic	Corpus::Geriatric::GeriatriC - Frailty	What is the relationship between frailty and chronic disease?	Patients with primary frailty are at higher risk of developing chronic diseases, which can lead to secondary frailty - creating a vicious cycle. Patients with secondary frailty may also develop primary frailty characteristics.	subject:geriatric
1246c351b33ee37c	Basic	Corpus::Geriatric::GeriatriC - Frailty	What preventive measures can reduce frailty?	Prevention includes: regular exercise, balanced diet, smoking cessation, alcohol reduction, disease management (e.g., diabetes control), and lifestyle modifications. Prevention is possible before reaching irreversible frailty stages.	subject:geriatric
db461d90ac66aa90	Basic	Corpus::Geriatric::GeriatriC - Frailty	What happens once a patient reaches irreversible frailty?	Once a patient reaches an irreversible stage of frailty, treatment shifts from prevention/rehabilitation to palliation (comfort care and symptom management).	subject:geriatric
51f841efa2ea8de8	Basic	Corpus::Geriatric::GeriatriC - Frailty	What are the main risk factors for frailty?	Risk factors include: age >80 years, female gender, smoking, depression, chronic diseases, and being underweight.	subject:geriatric
1eb5e576c00afabc	Basic	Corpus::Geriatric::GeriatriC - Frailty	What are the major consequences of frailty?	Consequences include: falls, loss of ability to perform activities of daily living, disability, increased hospitalization risk with infections, malnutrition, cognitive impairment, osteoporosis, orthostatic hypertension, and increased mortality risk.	subject:geriatric
9e1d3566dd9db95b	Basic	Corpus::Geriatric::GeriatriC - Frailty	What rehabilitation options are available for frail patients?	Rehabilitation options include: exercise programs, disease management (even though genetics and age cannot be changed), lifestyle modifications, and activity enhancement. ae3caa0aa2ebde8c	Basic	Corpus::Geriatric::Geriatric - Urinary Incontinence	What happens to detrusor contractility with age?	Detrusor contractility decreases with age. c15c23e24f2ed3ef	Basic	Corpus::Geriatric::Geriatric - Urinary Incontinence	What is the ability to postpone micturition and how does it change with age?	The ability to postpone urination (to delay going to the bathroom) decreases with age. Younger individuals can hold urine longer, but this ability reduces in the elderly.	subject:geriatric
5c5923219a52fab9	Basic	Corpus::Geriatric::Geriatric - Urinary Incontinence	How does urethral closing ability change in aging women?	There is decreased urethral closing ability in women as they age, especially in menopausal women, due to estrogen deficiency.	subject:geriatric
9cb6f89ed8721b5b	Basic	Corpus::Geriatric::Geriatric - Urinary Incontinence	What are the six types of urinary incontinence?	The six types are: (1) stress, (2) urge, (3) overflow, (4) reflex, (5) functional, and (6) mixed incontinence.	subject:geriatric
0e37e83447bc7c19	Basic	Corpus::Geriatric::Geriatric - Urinary Incontinence	Define stress urinary incontinence and its mechanism. 046c9e43a475cbe0	Basic	Corpus::Geriatric::Geriatric - Urinary Incontinence	Define urge incontinence and its mechanism.	Urge incontinence is characterized by an overwhelming urge to urinate frequently, driven by an overactive bladder (detrusor overactivity).	subject:geriatric
23ccbdb224a36e72	Basic	Corpus::Geriatric::Geriatric - Urinary Incontinence	Define overflow incontinence and its mechanism.	Overflow incontinence occurs when something blocks urinary flow. The bladder becomes distended and eventually stretches the urethra, causing urine to leak.	subject:geriatric
5693e71967de99b5	Basic	Corpus::Geriatric::Geriatric - Urinary Incontinence	Define reflex incontinence and its mechanism.	Reflex incontinence occurs due to damage in the neural connections between the urinary system and the brain. The person loses the ability to sense the need to urinate, and urine leaks involuntarily.	subject:geriatric
b6ebb567a458aa49	Basic	Corpus::Geriatric::Geriatric - Urinary Incontinence	Define functional incontinence and its mechanism.	Functional incontinence occurs when there is no underlying problem with the urogenital system itself, but something prevents the patient from reaching the bathroom in time, such as severe illness, fractures, or cognitive impairment.	subject:geriatric
776679a7c4a77613	Basic	Corpus::Geriatric::Geriatric - Urinary Incontinence	Define mixed incontinence.	Mixed incontinence is usually a combination of stress and urge incontinence.	subject:geriatric
51e1dbb26ddbfcfc	Basic	Corpus::Geriatric::Geriatric - Urinary Incontinence	What is the DRIP mnemonic for reversible causes of acute urinary incontinence?	DRIP stands for: D=Delirium, R=Restricted mobility, I=Infection/Inflammation, P=imperfection/Polyuria/Pills. These are reversible conditions causing acute incontinence.	subject:geriatric
50c8887dc4a307d8	Basic	Corpus::Geriatric::Geriatric - Urinary Incontinence	What does the letter D stand for in the DRIP mnemonic?	D = Delirium, a reversible cause of acute urinary incontinence.	subject:geriatric
548f091c92565440	Basic	Corpus::Geriatric::Geriatric - Urinary Incontinence	What does the first R stand for in the DRIP mnemonic?	R = Restricted mobility. Patients with pain, osteoarthritis, rheumatoid arthritis, fractures, or postoperative status cannot get to the toilet in time.	subject:geriatric
5a9ee656f714153a	Basic	Corpus::Geriatric::Geriatric - Urinary Incontinence	What does I stand for in the DRIP mnemonic and what are examples?	I = Infection and Inflammation. Examples include urinary tract infections and atrophic urethritis or vaginitis in postmenopausal women.	subject:geriatric
caf7b364d2c42d50	Basic	Corpus::Geriatric::Geriatric - Urinary Incontinence	What does the second R stand for in the DRIP mnemonic?	R = Retention, which can result from anticholinergic drugs, fecal impaction, or bladder outflow obstruction.	subject:geriatric
1679da2c250a2396	Basic	Corpus::Geriatric::Geriatric - Urinary Incontinence	What does P stand for in the DRIP mnemonic (first meaning)?	P = imperfection (fecal impaction), a reversible cause of acute urinary incontinence.	subject:geriatric
bbbc0e506ee99f93	Basic	Corpus::Geriatric::Geriatric - Urinary Incontinence	What does P stand for in the DRIP mnemonic (second meaning)?	P = Polyuria. Causes include glycosuria (glucose in urine), hypercalcemia, and excessive intake of caffeine or alcohol.	subject:geriatric
8142680032146465	Basic	Corpus::Geriatric::Geriatric - Urinary Incontinence	What does P stand for in the DRIP mnemonic (third meaning)?	P = Pills (medications) that cause incontinence, including diuretics, anticholinergic drugs, alpha blockers, alcohol, and calcium channel blockers.	subject:geriatric

52ef4577f50d237e	Basic	Corpus::Nephrology::Acute_Kidney_Disease_CMED4IIM1	What is the definition of Acute Kidney Injury (AKI)?	A syndrome characterized by rapid fall of GFR (generally within hours to days), retention of nitrogenous and non-nitrogenous waste products, and disordered electrolytes, acid-base, and fluid homeostasis.	subject:nephrology
83242de1c9534258	Basic	Corpus::Nephrology::Acute_Kidney_Disease_CMED4IIM1	What is the epidemiology of AKI?	AKI is responsible for 5-10% of general hospital admissions, 20-25% of sepsis patients develop AKI, 50% with septic shock develop AKI, and 50% of ICU admissions develop AKI. Mortality ranges from 20-60% in ICU.	subject:nephrology
9fd192ff11d6a911	Basic	Corpus::Nephrology::Acute_Kidney_Disease_CMED4IIM1	What are the three stages of AKI classification?	Stage 1: Serum creatinine increases >26 μmol/L within 48h or 1.5-1.9x baseline, or urine output <0.5 mL/kg/hr for >6h. Stage 2: Serum creatinine 2-3x baseline or urine output <0.5 mL/kg/hr for >12h. Stage 3: Serum creatinine >3x baseline or >354 μmol/L, or urine output <0.3 mL/kg/hr for >24h.	subject:nephrology
af5620b39097053b	Basic	Corpus::Nephrology::Acute_Kidney_Disease_CMED4IIM1	What are the three main classifications of AKI?	Pre-renal AKI (reduced kidney blood flow), Intrinsic AKI (kidney parenchymal damage to vasculature, glomeruli, or tubules), and Post-renal AKI (obstruction of urinary flow).	subject:nephrology
0159b3fd1f8f56f5	Basic	Corpus::Nephrology::Acute_Kidney_Disease_CMED4IIM1	What causes Pre-renal AKI?	Acute hemorrhage, volume depletion, low cardiac output, sepsis, chronic congestive cardiac failure, cirrhosis, and vasomotor drugs (NSAIDs, ACE inhibitors, ARBs).	subject:nephrology
a683c26269242f42	Basic	Corpus::Nephrology::Acute_Kidney_Disease_CMED4IIM1	What causes Intrinsic AKI?	Acute Tubular Necrosis (ATN) from ischemic or nephrotoxic injury (hemoglobin, myoglobin, myeloma casts, nephrotoxic drugs, contrast), tubulointerstitial nephritis, acute glomerulonephritis, and vascular causes (vasculitis, thrombosis, severe hypertension).	subject:nephrology
95e83b5535b4d8ca	Basic	Corpus::Nephrology::Acute_Kidney_Disease_CMED4IIM1	What causes Post-renal AKI?	Obstruction of urinary flow at any level - bladder outflow obstruction or ureteral obstruction. If untreated, leads to permanent structural and functional kidney damage.	subject:nephrology
d99b23adf19aa110	Basic	Corpus::Nephrology::Acute_Kidney_Disease_CMED4IIM1	What are the risk factors for developing AKI?	Increasing age, pre-existing chronic kidney disease, recent surgery, diabetes, volume depletion (vomiting, diarrhea, burns), RV dysfunction, cirrhosis, drugs causing vasomotor changes (NSAIDs, ACE inhibitors, ARBs), and systemic conditions (multiple myeloma).	subject:nephrology
3a8bc990c948d96a	Basic	Corpus::Nephrology::Acute_Kidney_Disease_CMED4IIM1	What nephrotoxic agents cause AKI?	NSAIDs, diuretics, ACE inhibitors, ARBs (especially in volume-depleted patients), aminoglycosides, vancomycin, amphotericin B, chemotherapy agents (cisplatin), and IV contrast.	subject:nephrology
e5162104129b617e	Basic	Corpus::Nephrology::Acute_Kidney_Disease_CMED4IIM1	How can perioperative AKI risk be reduced?	Adequate hydration, remove nephrotoxic drugs, review volume status and kidney function, optimize volume status preoperatively, monitor daily weights and intake/output, control glucose in diabetics, optimize nutrition, screen for obstruction, avoid surgery immediately after contrast, and reduce antihypertensives in early postoperative period.	subject:nephrology
f58fa666b2ae569d	Basic	Corpus::Nephrology::Acute_Kidney_Disease_CMED4IIM1	What are the presenting features of acute kidney injury?	Rise in serum urea and creatinine, reduced urine output (earliest change, precedes urea/creatinine rise), volume depletion or overload, pulmonary edema, hyperkalemia (risk for arrhythmia/cardiac arrest), and nonspecific illness.	subject:nephrology
1a68a158e077abbd	Basic	Corpus::Nephrology::Acute_Kidney_Disease_CMED4IIM1	What clinical findings help diagnose AKI?	Falling urine output and urea, persistent nausea/vomiting, dizziness, impaired consciousness, signs of systemic sepsis, evidence of severe hypertension or hypotension, pulmonary edema (crackles), peripheral edema, and metabolic acidosis.	subject:nephrology
b53c754c2894f4ce	Basic	Corpus::Nephrology::Acute_Kidney_Disease_CMED4IIM1	How do you distinguish AKI from Chronic Kidney Disease?	Review previous kidney function tests. On ultrasound, chronic kidney disease shows shrunken kidneys (<9-10 cm), while AKI shows normal-sized kidneys. Exception: diabetics have normal kidney size even with reduced function. Laboratory findings (anemia, calcium-phosphate abnormalities) are often unhelpful.	subject:nephrology
818b81a75a9c103f	Basic	Corpus::Nephrology::Acute_Kidney_Disease_CMED4IIM1	What are the priorities in managing AKI?	Make the patient safe (airway, breathing, circulation), manage hyperkalemia urgently, assess volume status, perform urine dipstick analysis, look for reversible causes, assess hemodynamic status, check for post-renal obstruction with ultrasound, and review drug charts to stop nephrotoxic agents.	subject:nephrology
aa290c2a6ab7b189	Basic	Corpus::Nephrology::Acute_Kidney_Disease_CMED4IIM1	What clinical clues suggest Pre-renal AKI?	History of reduced intravascular volume (dehydration), heart failure, or sepsis. Clinical signs: abnormal blood pressure, elevated heart rate, poor peripheral perfusion, abnormal jugular venous pressure, peripheral and pulmonary edema, and reduced urine output.	subject:nephrology
d07726ccac96f54b	Basic	Corpus::Nephrology::Acute_Kidney_Disease_CMED4IIM1	What clinical clues suggest Intrinsic kidney AKI?	History of cardiovascular disease, hypertension, diabetes, hypercholesterolemia, peripheral vascular disease, stroke, ischemic heart disease, recent invasive/endovascular procedures, stem cell therapy, or surgery. Signs: signs of atherosclerosis, missing pulses, large heart, glaucoma, pediatric aneurysms, or ischemic toes.	subject:nephrology
b85f36d30ce09ea4	Basic	Corpus::Nephrology::Acute_Kidney_Disease_CMED4IIM1	What investigations help identify intrinsic glomerular AKI?	History of throat/skin infections, fever, night sweats, systemic features. Examination: rash, elevated blood pressure, evidence of systemic involvement (uveitis, ulcers, connective tissue disease). Investigations: blood or protein in urine.	subject:nephrology
05b5baced621c976	Basic	Corpus::Nephrology::Acute_Kidney_Disease_CMED4IIM1	What investigations help identify tubulointerstitial AKI?	History of drug use or systemic infections/diseases (sarcoid). Examination: secondary features (fever, rash, bruising). Typical clue: eosinophilia on urine microscopy or peripheral smear or in blood.	subject:nephrology
3f7236f962760792	Basic	Corpus::Nephrology::Acute_Kidney_Disease_CMED4IIM1	What clinical clues suggest Post-renal AKI?	Lower urinary tract symptoms, evidence of prostatic disease in elderly males, presence of cancers, and hematuria. Essential investigation: early ultrasound to exclude obstruction.	subject:nephrology
d4d84888ebadda6a	Basic	Corpus::Nephrology::Acute_Kidney_Disease_CMED4IIM1	What findings on urine analysis help in AKI diagnosis?	Presence of blood, protein, or red blood cells suggests glomerular or intrinsic kidney disease. Positive urine culture indicates UTI which can cause severe dysfunction. Urine electrolytes and specific gravity are generally not helpful in AKI assessment.	subject:nephrology
5acf61eec8c91077	Basic	Corpus::Nephrology::Acute_Kidney_Disease_CMED4IIM1	What blood test findings are important in AKI?	Reduced hemoglobin (hemolysis or bleeding), elevated white cell count (infection), eosinophilia (systemic arthritis), reduced white cell count (sepsis), reduced platelets (DIC or thrombotic microangiopathy). Peripheral smear shows fragmented RBCs in microangiopathy.	subject:nephrology
929db0a7b4d7fe5f	Basic	Corpus::Nephrology::Acute_Kidney_Disease_CMED4IIM1	What biochemical findings are important in AKI?	Elevated potassium (hyperkalemia), reduced sodium (volume overload or excessive diuretics), reduced bicarbonate (metabolic acidosis). Urine and electrolytes are key for assessing AKI severity.	subject:nephrology
76f8c9976cb11f6c	Basic	Corpus::Nephrology::Acute_Kidney_Disease_CMED4IIM1	What imaging findings help in AKI diagnosis?	Chest X-ray: excludes pulmonary edema, respiratory infections, assesses cardiac size, presence of lymph nodes, or cystic lesions. Kidney ultrasound: excludes obstruction (urgent if obstruction suspected). Kidney size <9 cm suggests chronic kidney disease; normal size suggests acute kidney injury.	subject:nephrology
b43b085a0f5f657e	Basic	Corpus::Nephrology::Acute_Kidney_Disease_CMED4IIM1	What is the role of volume replacement in Pre-renal AKI?	Prompt volume replacement early in pre-renal AKI improves outcomes. Must ensure patient not overloaded to avoid pulmonary edema. Fluids: crystalloid saline or crystalloids, with sodium bicarbonate. Assess adequacy by tachycardia, central venous pressure, and urine output.	subject:nephrology
c68efead2f83038e	Basic	Corpus::Nephrology::Acute_Kidney_Disease_CMED4IIM1	What are the ECG changes in hyperkalemia?	Progressive changes: peaked T waves, flattened P waves, prolonged PR interval, widened QRS complex, and sine wave (terminal event leading to ventricular fibrillation or asystolic cardiac arrest). Potassium >6 mmol/L requires close cardiac monitoring and ECG.	subject:nephrology
eb300e458b77dfd2	Basic	Corpus::Nephrology::Acute_Kidney_Disease_CMED4IIM1	How is hyperkalemia managed?	Calcium carbonate 10% solution over 2-5 minutes, insulin-glucose (monitor sugars after), sodium bicarbonate (15-60 minutes) if volume-depleted. If unresponsive to these three measures, consider dialysis, especially if kidney function not improving and potassium continues to rise.	subject:nephrology
27bdf64b52a17236	Basic	Corpus::Nephrology::Acute_Kidney_Disease_CMED4IIM1	How is pulmonary edema managed in AKI?	Stop IV fluids, give oxygen, use diuretics, give opiates or morphine for mild relief, prop patient up. If unresponsive to diuretics, consider dialysis and ventilation.	subject:nephrology
62bbf0dd2f0ddc91	Basic	Corpus::Nephrology::Acute_Kidney_Disease_CMED4IIM1	How is hyperphosphatemia managed in AKI?	Dietary phosphate restriction, oral phosphate binders, and dialysis if severe.	subject:nephrology

4ce633114ebc006a	Basic	Corpus::Nephrology::Acute_Kidney_Disease_CMED4IIM1	What is the relationship between hypomagnesemia and hypocalcemia?	Hypomagnesemia is associated with both hypercalcemia and hypocalcemia. Unless magnesium is corrected, calcium will not correct. Therefore, magnesium correction is necessary for successful calcium management.	subject:nephrology
0d5eb713ea71f74b	Basic	Corpus::Nephrology::Acute_Kidney_Disease_CMED4IIM1	How is metabolic acidosis managed in severe AKI?	High-dose metabolic acidosis in AKI; if severe, consider dialysis. 238da4d9f93f8115	Basic	Corpus::Nephrology::Approach To Chronic Kidney Disease	What is the definition of Chronic Kidney Disease (CKD)?	Chronic Kidney Disease is an abnormality of kidney structure and function that is present for more than three months. Functional abnormality is defined by eGFR <60 mL/min, while structural abnormality includes proteinuria (elevated protein-creatinine ratio), hematuria, or structural abnormalities on imaging.	CKD definition diagnosis subject:nephrology
3859226295515547	Basic	Corpus::Nephrology::Approach To Chronic Kidney Disease	What are the eGFR-based CKD stages? fadfba72d769e65c	Basic	Corpus::Nephrology::Approach To Chronic Kidney Disease	What is the epidemiology of CKD in South Africa?	The prevalence of CKD in South Africa is estimated at approximately 10% of the adult population. CKD numbers are expected to double in the next decade. It is currently under-recognized and under-treated, and prevalence increases with age.	epidemiology CKD South-Africa prevalence subject:nephrology
2d815b1d708f17f6	Basic	Corpus::Nephrology::Approach To Chronic Kidney Disease	What is the disease iceberg concept in CKD?	For every 1 patient with kidney failure, there are approximately 200 patients in stages 3-4, and about 5,000 in stages 1-2. 5d1d721dc620f07f	Basic	Corpus::Nephrology::Approach To Chronic Kidney Disease	What is the pathogenesis of CKD progression?	Nephronal destruction leads to increased blood flow and permeability in remaining nephrons. The glomerulus becomes damaged with strain on mesangial cells, causing proteinuria that leaks into tubules, resulting in tubular-interstitial scarring and chronic ischemic damage.	pathophysiology CKD progression mechanisms subject:nephrology

Residual kidney function is preserved longer, ability to pass urine is maintained, more readily available (not limited by machine availability), less hospital time, and patients become more empowered and aware of treatment. 3cd2d2ef7e177335	Basic	Corpus::Nephrology::kidney-replacement-therapy-srs-cards	What types of catheters are used for peritoneal dialysis?	Temporary: stink catheter for acute peritoneal dialysis. aba992970df4f0c0	Basic	Corpus::Nephrology::kidney-replacement-therapy-srs-cards	When was kidney transplantation first performed and by whom?	1954 by Joseph Murray and colleagues, who won the Nobel Prize in 1990 for this achievement.	subject:nephrology

48b1191e536b71c9	Basic	Corpus::Nephrology::kidney-replacement-therapy-srs-cards	What are the benefits of kidney transplantation?	1) Improved patient survival compared to dialysis, 2) Prevents metabolic consequences of CKD (uremia, vitamin D, immunometabolism), 3) Significantly improves quality of life for patient and family, 4) More cost-effective than dialysis after the first year.	subject:nephrology

A urinary tract infection is a bacterial infection that occurs anywhere in the urinary tract, from the urethra to the kidneys. 0ac36b9a703bb2b3	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	How do carcinogens in cigarette smoke cause lung cancer?	Carcinogens cause DNA damage and mutations within tumor suppressor genes, which interrupt DNA repair and cell cycle regulation mechanisms, leading to tumor formation.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
7dd8370164923d71	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	How does duration versus intensity of smoking affect lung cancer risk?	For patients with similar smoking histories, longer duration of smoking puts them at greater risk of cancer compared with higher intensity of smoking.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
ccb1a57f951f21a2	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	What is the relative lung cancer risk in current smokers?	Current smokers have the highest risk. Persistent smokers have a 16-fold elevated lung cancer risk, which is further doubled in those who started smoking younger than 16 years of age.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
0c455f34583b03dd	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	How does smoking cessation affect lung cancer risk?	Smoking cessation lowers the risk of lung cancer. The relative risk remains high in the first 10 years after cessation and gradually declines to approximately 2-fold at 30 years after cessation.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
0b00a71f8d7bc4fc	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	Do modern cigarette formulations reduce lung cancer risk?	No. Modern cigarette formulations do not reduce the risk of developing lung cancer; they appear to increase the risk.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
6a282ac2e8e58c6e	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	What percentage of lung cancer cases occur in past smokers?	Almost 50% of lung cancer cases develop in past smokers, explained by the persistence of elevated risk for many years after smoking cessation.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
c17e93c8c34d46fb	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	How does cardiorespiratory fitness affect lung cancer risk in smokers?	Strong cardiorespiratory fitness may help reduce lung cancer risk in cigarette smokers and former smokers. Higher fitness levels are associated with lower incidence of lung cancer and better survival if lung cancer develops.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
a54300927fcbd2a7	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	What is second-hand smoke and its lung cancer risk?	Second-hand smoke is carcinogenic smoke inhaled passively by non-smokers. Urinary levels of carcinogens in non-smokers are 1-5% of those in active smokers. Approximately 25% of lung cancers in non-smokers are believed to be caused by second-hand smoke.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
40a200304098dd96	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	What other risk factors contribute to lung cancer development?	Age, male gender, occupational exposures, diet low in fresh fruit and vegetables and fiber, low physical activity, radon exposure, asbestos exposure, HIV infection, and exposure to beryllium, nickel, copper, chromium, and cadmium.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
b0436e97e016d49d	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	Are there lung cancers in never-smokers?	Yes. A minority of lung cancers develop in people who have never smoked. These are genetically distinct from smoking-related lung cancers and often have detectable genetic mutations amenable to targeted therapy. They have a better prognosis.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
63e57a09e853a736	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	What is the relative risk of lung cancer with family history?	A family history of lung cancer is associated with approximately a 2-fold increased risk of lung cancer development.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
9d3b5cfb1a813d06	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	What is radon and its association with lung cancer?	Radon is an inert gas produced from uranium decay, present in the environment. It is well-established as a risk factor in uranium miners. Approximately 2-3% of lung cancers annually are estimated to be caused by radon exposure. However, household radon exposure has not been clearly shown to cause lung cancer.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
d62481972bda4dc2	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	What is asbestos exposure and its lung cancer risk?	Asbestos exposure is strongly associated with lung cancer causation, increasing risk by up to 5 times. It is also associated with malignant pleural mesothelioma and pulmonary fibrosis.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
54f44a88b209044c	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	What is the synergistic risk of tobacco smoke and asbestos exposure?	Tobacco smoke and asbestos exposure act synergistically. The risk of developing lung cancer for persons who smoke tobacco and have asbestos exposure history approaches 80-90 times that of control populations.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
aa9ab4bbab3ec258	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	What is the relationship between HIV infection and lung cancer?	Persons with HIV infection have higher lung cancer risk than those without. In HIV-positive people, lung cancer is the most common and most fatal non-AIDS-associated malignancy. HIV infection appears to increase lung cancer risk independently by a factor of at least 2.5-fold.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
04efe77a58d70606	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	What histological type of lung cancer predominates in HIV-infected persons?	The majority of lung cancers in HIV-infected persons are adenocarcinomas (non-small cell carcinomas).	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
127b9ec231c2f26c	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	Did HAART change lung cancer incidence in HIV-infected persons?	In most studies, the incidence and risk of lung cancer in HIV-infected persons did not change significantly with the introduction of highly active antiretroviral therapy (HAART).	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
3727149ab25e4428	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	What is unique about lung cancer in HIV-infected patients compared to the general population?	Lung cancer in HIV-infected persons develops almost exclusively in smokers, but HIV-infected patients with lung cancer are significantly younger and present with advanced disease and shorter median survival compared to the general population.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
96bca0d270f53b42	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	What occupational exposures increase lung cancer risk?	Exposures to beryllium, nickel, copper, chromium, and cadmium increase lung cancer risk.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
8f217884c0f05ab0	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	What personal lung diseases increase lung cancer risk?	Personal history of lung disease that scars the lungs increases risk, including COPD, tuberculosis, and certain infections.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
8ec34195f5e1ccbc	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	What role do biomass fuels and cooking methods play in lung cancer risk?	Pollutants from cooking and heating with biomass fuel, burning coal indoors, burning wood indoors, cooking on wood stoves, and frying foods at high temperatures increase lung cancer risk.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
130d78765ba36089	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	What is the evidence for dietary protective factors in lung cancer?	Dietary fiber and vegetables have been suggested as protective. However, trials of beta-carotene, vitamin E, and retinol palmitate supplementation in at-risk persons actually increased lung cancer incidence.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
0de510e4dc2e31ce	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	What is the evidence for cannabis smoking and lung cancer risk?	Studies are showing that long-term cannabis smoking can increase the risk of lung cancer, but evidence is not yet as robust as for tobacco.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
c57e67d6593670bc	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	What are global trends in lung cancer incidence?	Lung cancer incidence has followed smoking trends with a lag time of several decades. Incidence is declining in developed countries (USA, Canada, UK) but expected to increase in developing countries with increased smoking.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
42a41b340da60ab5	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	What is the lung cancer trend in South Africa?	Lung cancer incidence is on an upward trend in South Africa. Notably, lung cancer incidence among women continues to increase despite very low smoking rates.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
124c2f9841f2326b	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	Why do Chinese women have higher lung cancer incidence than European women?	Despite very low smoking rates, Chinese women have higher lung cancer incidence than European women, with higher incidence of genetic mutations causing lung cancer.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
511d3ae0d2205e44	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	How is lung cancer classified histologically?	Lung cancer is divided into small cell lung cancer (SCLC) and non-small cell lung cancer (NSCLC). NSCLC accounts for 85-90% and includes squamous cell carcinoma, adenocarcinoma, and large cell carcinoma.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
9310f839ab691a19	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	How was small cell lung cancer traditionally staged?	Small cell lung cancer was traditionally staged as limited or extensive disease.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
cdaae5a997d1fab6	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	What are the characteristics of small cell lung cancer?	Small cell lung cancer: accounts for ~15% of all lung cancers; almost always in smokers; characterized by small round blue cells approximately twice the size of lymphocytes; usually centrally located; aggressive tumor that spreads quickly; often has distant metastases at diagnosis; more responsive to chemotherapy and radiotherapy but prognosis remains poor.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
bc05a076f5665f8e	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	What are the characteristics of non-small cell lung cancer?	NSCLC: accounts for 85% of lung cancers; can be centrally or peripherally located; often peripherally located; can be fast or slow growing; staged using TNM staging; has improved survival with newer treatments.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
d22da73b3553a2c6	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	What percentage of lung cancers is adenocarcinoma?	Adenocarcinoma accounts for 35-40% of non-small cell lung cancers. It is the most common type overall, most common in women, most common in non-smokers (though most patients are smokers), and usually peripheral.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
77d99f1091feaef5	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	What is the diagnostic implication of adenocarcinoma in non-smokers?	If a non-smoker is diagnosed with lung cancer, they almost certainly have adenocarcinoma.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
aa0e1da89ddece4d	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	What percentage of non-small cell lung cancers is squamous cell carcinoma?	Squamous cell carcinoma accounts for approximately 30% of non-small cell carcinomas.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
d3acd5cd572f6bbb	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	What are the characteristics of squamous cell carcinoma?	Squamous cell carcinoma: strongly associated with smoking; used to be the most common histological subtype but overtaken by adenocarcinoma; most common to cavitate; carries poor prognosis; usually central; difficult to resect because it grows into airways and close to mediastinal vessels.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
cf805e1cff8b3784	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	What are large cell carcinomas?	Large cell carcinomas are the least common type of non-small cell lung cancers. They are usually peripherally located and very large, typically 1-4 centimeters on diagnosis.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
7df784e91df2016e	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	How does lung cancer typically present early in disease?	Often lung cancer presents with no symptoms in early stages. Patients are asymptomatic until disease is advanced. In approximately 10% of cases, lung cancer is diagnosed incidentally on chest X-ray.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
cf22eebf5c4428c6	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	What is the stage distribution at initial lung cancer diagnosis?	At initial diagnosis: 20% have localized disease, 25% have regional metastases to local structures and lymph nodes, and 55% (majority) have distant metastases.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
b2c10b2269c64b43	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	What is the most common symptom of lung cancer?	Cough is the most common symptom. New onset or worsening cough in a smoker or former smoker should raise suspicion of lung cancer.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
a8f1ad7478a2f28b	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	What does hemoptysis indicate in lung cancer?	Hemoptysis (coughing up blood) is concerning for lung cancer.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
a83d2885d0f54afd	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	How does lung cancer cause dyspnea?	Dyspnea occurs due to: mass effect of tumor pressing on airways, inducing pleural effusion that causes lung collapse, and wheeze when tumor presses on airway lumen.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
e2f94efb715a483e	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	What causes chest pain in lung cancer?	Chest pain develops when tumor invades outside the lung parenchyma into the pleura, ribs, and chest wall, or when enlarged lymph nodes compress surrounding structures.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
6dcddf9e0219ef78	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	What causes hoarseness in lung cancer?	Hoarseness occurs when tumor compresses the recurrent laryngeal nerve around the trachea that innervates the vocal cords.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
028d9edd3c7f6ea0	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	What is superior vena cava syndrome?	Superior vena cava syndrome presents with headache, swelling of face, arms, and neck. It occurs when great vessels entering or exiting the chest are compressed by tumor, causing venous obstruction, blood vessel distension, and fluid backup in the region drained by the superior vena cava.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
627d4fd970cc3e61	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	What is a Pancoast tumor and what does it cause?	A Pancoast or superior sulcus tumor is located at the apex of the lung. It compresses the sympathetic plexus, causing Horner syndrome.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
70e38fd1a5d896c0	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	What are the components of Horner syndrome?	Horner syndrome is characterized by: meiosis (persistently constricted pupil), ptosis (drooping of eyelid), and anhidrosis (inability to sweat on the same side as tumor). It does not cause problems with vision.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
e8ac0cfb5dd9594e	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	What can result from progression of Pancoast tumors?	Progression can lead to brachial plexus involvement, causing arm and hand weakness.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
150da38b4cf2acf9	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	What are the most common sites of lung cancer metastases?	The most common sites for lung cancer metastases are bones, liver, brain, and adrenal glands.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
46a8dc19ae336680	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	How do bone metastases from lung cancer present?	Bone metastases present as localized bone pain or non-traumatic fractures due to bone weakness. Most commonly affected bones are spine, ribs, and pelvis.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
b7f634463509136e	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	How do liver metastases from lung cancer present?	Liver metastases present with jaundice, weakness, and weight loss.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
9d5ea8a4426af97b	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	How do brain metastases from lung cancer present?	Brain metastases present with a wide range of features including confusion, headache, nausea, vomiting, personality changes, and seizures.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
5d9cd5802701dd89	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	What are paraneoplastic syndromes?	Paraneoplastic syndromes are clinical syndromes arising not from direct tumor effect (mass, invasion, metastases) but from immune response to tumor or tumor production of substances like hormones or cytokines that cause electrolyte abnormalities or hormone syndromes. They occur in 10-20% of lung cancer patients.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
1e7f873b773b0f90	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	Which lung cancer type most commonly causes paraneoplastic syndromes?	Most paraneoplastic syndromes are caused by small cell lung cancer, though they may also occur in non-small cell lung cancer.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
cd7b7a4f951262d6	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	Which lung cancer type most commonly causes hypercalcemia?	Hypercalcemia is most commonly found in squamous cell carcinoma, though it can be caused by bony metastases or tumor secretion of parathyroid hormone-related peptide or calcitriol (usually from small cell lung cancer).	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
4e130b8139f70ed4	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	Which lung cancer types commonly cause clubbing and hypertrophic osteoarthropathy?	Clubbing and hypertrophic pulmonary osteoarthropathy are caused more frequently by adenocarcinomas.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
0e70e8c023bc4358	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	Which lung cancer type commonly causes thrombosis of hypercoagulability?	Thrombosis and hypercoagulability syndromes are caused more frequently by adenocarcinomas.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology
137061e4843dc588	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	What is SIADH in lung cancer?	SIADH (Syndrome of Inappropriate Antidiuretic Hormone) is more common in small cell lung cancer but can also occur in non-small cell lung cancer.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology

14ad6664bcc0f4fa	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	What are the clinical manifestations of hypercalcemia in lung cancer?	Clinical manifestations are often referred to as "stones, bones, groans, and psychiatric bones" referring to renal calculi, bone pain, abdominal pain, polyuria, and signs of altered mental status. d96665e1c34ac0b4	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung Cancer	What is Lambert-Eaton syndrome in lung cancer?	Lambert-Eaton syndrome is caused by antibodies against small cell lung cancer that cross-react with voltage-gated calcium channels on motor neurons, damaging them and leading to proximal muscle weakness and other symptoms.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology

A paraneoplastic syndrome seen in around 10% of patients with small cell lung cancer, presenting with hyponatremia symptoms and potentially cerebral edema.	subject:pulmonology

Caused by glucocorticoids from lung cancer or ectopic ACTH. Features include hypertension, hypoglycemia, moon facies, muscle weakness, purple striae, and buffalo hump.	subject:pulmonology

45148935c27eef47	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung-Cancer-pages-011-013	What aspects of history should be taken in suspected lung cancer?	Presenting complaint duration, alleviating/aggravating factors, associated symptoms, medical history, comorbidities, risk factors (asbestos, smoking), family history, and environmental exposures.	subject:pulmonology
d8389007ea6fcbd9	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung-Cancer-pages-011-013	What physical examination findings suggest lung cancer?	Weight loss, finger clubbing (especially in smokers), dullness to percussion from tumor or collapse, tracheal deviation, and pleural effusion signs.	subject:pulmonology
2957eb81d2a02894	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung-Cancer-pages-011-013	What symptoms and signs indicate possible lung cancer? 6f32cbf03e576fab	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung-Cancer-pages-011-013	What methods obtain tissue diagnosis in lung cancer?	Sputum cytology, pleural fluid cytology, bronchoscopy with biopsy, thoracoscopy, thoracotomy, mediastinoscopy, and percutaneous fine needle aspiration.	subject:pulmonology
