Extensive: bilateral, pleural effusion, or distant metastases. Limited 15-25% five-year survival; extensive 20% two-year survival.	subject:pulmonology
e7d1d76af3724a89	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung-Cancer-pages-011-013	What are three main treatment modalities for lung cancer?	Surgery, radiation therapy, and systemic therapies (chemotherapy, immunotherapy, targeted therapy). Choice depends on stage and patient fitness.	subject:pulmonology
ff8878b280492126	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung-Cancer-pages-011-013	What criteria determine surgical resection candidacy?	Tumor completely resectable in one lung, tumor small enough for lobe or lung resection, limited spread to ipsilateral lymph nodes, and patient fit for surgery.	subject:pulmonology
d96e515e0c0e5131	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung-Cancer-pages-011-013	How does radiotherapy work in lung cancer?	Direct DNA damage; normal cells repair faster than rapidly dividing cancer cells. Used after surgery, palliative stage 3B/4, endobronchially, and for brain/bone metastases.	subject:pulmonology
730988da394c5e48	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung-Cancer-pages-011-013	What are chemotherapy applications in lung cancer?	Shrinks tumor pre-surgery/radiation (neoadjuvant), kills residual cells post-surgery, primary treatment for unresectable tumors. Selected stage 1-3 NSCLC get chemotherapy after resection; stage 3B/4 get chemoradiation or chemotherapy alone.	subject:pulmonology
90d4d9095cc151fc	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung-Cancer-pages-011-013	What are targeted therapies in advanced NSCLC?	Drugs targeting specific mutations (EGFR, ALK inhibitors) to stop growth/spread. For stage 3/4 with nodal involvement. Prolong progression-free survival and improve response versus chemotherapy alone.	subject:pulmonology
8c9311b03b57c2ec	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung-Cancer-pages-011-013	How do immune checkpoint inhibitors work in lung cancer?	Target overexpressed inhibitory receptors/ligands on cancer cells that evade immune detection. Used for advanced metastatic NSCLC resistant to chemotherapy or combined with chemotherapy.	subject:pulmonology
aa7b7e2969b15546	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Lung-Cancer-pages-011-013	What factors influence lung cancer prognosis?	Stage (most important), metastases presence/location, targetable mutations, performance status, sex (females better with NSCLC), weight loss, pre-existing lung disease, elevated LDH/ALP, hyponatremia. Overall five-year survival ~20%.	subject:pulmonology
9bce0404a464c2d9	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Optimal Use Of Antibiotics - Antibiotic Stewardship	What is the definition of optimal use of antibiotics?	Cost-effective use of antimicrobial agents that maximizes clinical effect while minimizing drug-related toxicity and the development of antimicrobial resistance.	difficulty:medium subject:pulmonology

c30e241046d3e5da	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Optimal Use Of Antibiotics - Antibiotic Stewardship	What is the difference between time-dependent and concentration-dependent antibiotics?	Time-dependent antibiotics (e.g., penicillins, vancomycin, cephalosporins, lincosamides): effectiveness depends on how long drug levels stay above MIC. Concentration-dependent antibiotics (e.g., aminoglycosides, fluoroquinolones, macrolides): effectiveness depends on peak serum concentration relative to MIC.	antibiotics pharmacodynamics drug-classes subject:pulmonology
7e95254f6c1f6c71	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Optimal Use Of Antibiotics - Antibiotic Stewardship	How should time-dependent antibiotics be dosed?	Time-dependent antibiotics are given frequently (e.g., every 4-6 hours) to maintain drug levels above the MIC. The goal is to ensure adequate time-above-MIC coverage throughout the dosing interval.	dosing-strategy time-dependent antibiotics subject:pulmonology
2204f6f59c83f229	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Optimal Use Of Antibiotics - Antibiotic Stewardship	How should concentration-dependent antibiotics be dosed?	Concentration-dependent antibiotics are given as high doses once or twice daily to maximize peak concentration. This maximizes the concentration-dependent kill effect, achieving therapeutic success with less frequent dosing.	dosing-strategy concentration-dependent antibiotics subject:pulmonology
8dfde00cd74f732d	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Optimal Use Of Antibiotics - Antibiotic Stewardship	What are the key steps in antibiotic selection?	1. Make accurate clinical diagnosis and identify infectious vs non-infectious cause 2. Determine likely organisms based on site and clinical presentation 3. Collect appropriate samples before starting therapy 4. Base selection on pre-graduation education, scientific evidence, company information, clinical experience, local epidemiology, and local laboratory data 5. Always adapt national guidelines to local epidemiology and resistance patterns	antibiotic-selection clinical-practice subject:pulmonology
8fded48a1e097854	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Optimal Use Of Antibiotics - Antibiotic Stewardship	What are the reasons for antibiotic failure after initiation?	1. Incorrect diagnosis (not actually an infection) 2. Wrong type of antibiotic agent 3. Inadequate dose 4. Incorrect frequency of administration 5. Poor drug distribution to infection site 6. Inadequate host nutrition 7. Use of inappropriate combination therapy 8. Emergence of resistance 9. Other factors (abscess formation, foreign body, immune system issues)	treatment-failure antibiotic-management subject:pulmonology
52acfd4ad99df9ce	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Optimal Use Of Antibiotics - Antibiotic Stewardship	What are the adverse effects and dangers of antimicrobial agents?	1. Direct toxicity: hypersensitivity, encephalitis, rash, fever, blood disorders 2. Effects on normal flora: superinfection (Candida, C. difficile) 3. Masking of severe infection (e.g., abscess) 4. Drug tolerability issues 5. Selection and proliferation of drug-resistant organisms	adverse-effects drug-safety antibiotic-complications subject:pulmonology
a9dae23868d907ba	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Optimal Use Of Antibiotics - Antibiotic Stewardship	What are the indications for antibiotic prophylaxis?	Medical indications: recurrent fever, recurrent UTI. Surgical indications: clean surgery (especially if involving prosthetic devices), lower limb amputation, endocarditis prevention, cardiac surgery with underlying conditions, surgeries with prosthetic devices.	prophylaxis indications subject:pulmonology
b11fdd2d0cb8c6eb	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Optimal Use Of Antibiotics - Antibiotic Stewardship	What are the criteria for choosing a prophylactic antibiotic?	1. Risk of infection is high 2. Probable organism is identified 3. Agent is likely to be active against the organism 4. Narrow spectrum preferred (not broad-spectrum) 5. Agent should NOT be one used for treatment 6. Must be appropriate for the specific indication	prophylaxis antibiotic-selection subject:pulmonology
3efbb40ada91005e	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Optimal Use Of Antibiotics - Antibiotic Stewardship	What is antibiotic resistance and why is it a major problem?	Antibiotic resistance: organisms develop the ability to survive in the presence of antibiotics that previously killed them. It emerged after penicillin discovery in 1940 and now affects up to 100% of certain organisms in some areas. Resistance leads to high morbidity and mortality, is a major public health crisis, and requires continuous monitoring and appropriate use strategies.	resistance public-health stewardship subject:pulmonology
f76120925a42678a	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Optimal Use Of Antibiotics - Antibiotic Stewardship	What are the main resistance mechanisms in bacteria?	1. Enzyme production (beta-lactamase, AmpC, carbapenemase) 2. Altered target sites (penicillin-binding proteins) 3. Decreased permeability (gram-negative outer membrane) 4. Active efflux pumps 5. Biochemical modification of the antibiotic molecule	resistance-mechanisms microbiology subject:pulmonology
df77ffd692f7040e	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Optimal Use Of Antibiotics - Antibiotic Stewardship	What is the definition of antimicrobial stewardship?	Prudent use of antibiotics to achieve the best patient outcomes while minimizing antibiotic selective pressure, reducing drug resistance, and avoiding excessively adverse or undesirable dose effects.	stewardship definition antimicrobial-management subject:pulmonology
74ab02f256cae9ed	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Optimal Use Of Antibiotics - Antibiotic Stewardship	What are the core components of antimicrobial stewardship?	1. Confirm infection vs non-infection 2. Collect appropriate samples before treatment 3. Initiate empirical therapy after specimen collection 4. Monitor time to administration (should be rapid) 5. Re-evaluate at 48-72 hours (D2-D3) based on culture and sensitivity results 6. De-escalate, escalate, or tailor therapy according to results 7. Assess clinical stability and adjust accordingly	stewardship-components clinical-management subject:pulmonology
d40840fdeafeab0f	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Optimal Use Of Antibiotics - Antibiotic Stewardship	What additional factors are important in antimicrobial stewardship?	1. Appropriate dose and frequency 2. Correct route of administration 3. De-escalation from IV to oral when appropriate 4. Appropriate duration of therapy 5. Whether to use single agent vs combination therapy 6. Monitoring for adverse effects 7. Regular team-based audit and feedback	stewardship-optimization clinical-management subject:pulmonology
f1b01c4117c2b7e0	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Optimal Use Of Antibiotics - Antibiotic Stewardship	What is the proper duration of prophylactic antibiotics?	Prophylaxis should be given within 12 hours pre-operatively or 1 dose before surgery. Duration: typically one dose for most procedures. If dosing extends beyond 24 hours, it is considered therapeutic, not prophylactic (even if only 3-5 days). Prophylactic antibiotics must be kept short to prevent resistance emergence.	prophylaxis-duration surgical-prophylaxis subject:pulmonology
935f61b37d6f7757	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Optimal Use Of Antibiotics - Antibiotic Stewardship	What is the triangle approach to antibiotic optimization?	Three interconnected factors: (1) Drug: dose, pharmacokinetics/pharmacodynamics, concentration-dependent vs time-dependent properties, toxicity, infection site penetration. (2) Organism: susceptibility, virulence factors, resistance mechanisms, biofilm production. (3) Host: age, organ function (renal, hepatic), body weight, comorbidities, immune status, source of infection.	antibiotic-optimization pharmacotherapy subject:pulmonology
343ad5d60424063a	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Optimal Use Of Antibiotics - Antibiotic Stewardship	How does patient age affect antibiotic selection?	Neonates: many antibiotics are contraindicated or have altered metabolism. Common organisms in neonates (Group B Streptococcus, Listeria, gram-negative bacilli) differ from older patients. Drug penetration and metabolism vary significantly. Special dosing considerations (e.g., reduced renal/hepatic function) are critical.	age-considerations neonatal-therapy subject:pulmonology
b80ca1917a2c0262	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Optimal Use Of Antibiotics - Antibiotic Stewardship	What host factors must be considered when choosing antibiotics?	1. Age and metabolic capacity 2. Body weight 3. Renal function (affects clearance) 4. Hepatic function (affects metabolism) 5. Site of infection (drug penetration requirements) 6. Immune status 7. Comorbid diseases 8. Nutritional status 9. Previous source of infection history	host-factors patient-assessment subject:pulmonology
63174ccf2212a997	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Optimal Use Of Antibiotics - Antibiotic Stewardship	How should antibiotic therapy be adjusted based on culture and sensitivity results?	After 48-72 hours (D2-D3), review culture and sensitivity results. If organism is sensitive to initial choice: continue the same antibiotic. If resistance is found: step up to a more potent agent. Always tailor therapy based on susceptibility patterns rather than empirical choice.	de-escalation culture-results therapy-adjustment subject:pulmonology
0f31b6347a09d43c	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Optimal Use Of Antibiotics - Antibiotic Stewardship	When is it appropriate to switch from IV to oral antibiotics?	Switch from IV to oral when: (1) patient is clinically improving, (2) patient is tolerating oral intake, (3) appropriate oral agent with good bioavailability is available, (4) infection site allows for oral drug penetration.	iv-to-oral-switch route-optimization subject:pulmonology
8efd6cc50bb6e46c	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Optimal Use Of Antibiotics - Antibiotic Stewardship	What is the role of a team-based approach in antimicrobial stewardship?	Stewardship cannot be performed by one person alone. It requires a multidisciplinary team including: clinicians, microbiologists, pharmacists, nurses, and laboratory personnel. The team should: audit antibiotic usage, analyze data, provide feedback, establish guidelines, implement strategies, monitor compliance, and ensure adherence to stewardship principles.	stewardship-team multidisciplinary-approach subject:pulmonology

Antimicrobial cycling or rotation strategies 5. Combination therapy indications 6. Regular audit and feedback loops 7. Monitoring of resistance patterns 8. e28bc919ac38d76c	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Optimal Use Of Antibiotics - Antibiotic Stewardship	What are the differences in antibiotic coverage needs for gram-positive vs gram-negative organisms?	Gram-positive organisms: covered by penicillins, cephalosporins, vancomycin, macrolides. Gram-negative organisms: require different agents (e.g., fluoroquinolones, aminoglycosides, certain beta-lactams). Anaerobic coverage: requires specific agents. Chart shows coverage by drug class for each category.	gram-stain organism-coverage drug-selection subject:pulmonology
b675d8372e7d3822	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Optimal Use Of Antibiotics - Antibiotic Stewardship	How does antimicrobial stewardship relate to infection site?	Different infection sites require different considerations: UTI with complicated vs uncomplicated status affects antibiotic choice. CSF infections require agents with good CNS penetration. Localized infections (abscess) may require drainage in addition to antibiotics. The infection site also determines drug penetration requirements and local epidemiology.	infection-site disease-specific antibiotic-selection subject:pulmonology
da70fdd5bfd549fe	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Optimal Use Of Antibiotics - Antibiotic Stewardship	What is meant by narrowing antibiotic coverage and when should it be done?	Narrowing coverage means switching from broad-spectrum antibiotics to more specific agents once the causative organism and susceptibilities are known. This is done at 48-72 hours when culture results return. Narrowing reduces selective pressure for resistance and minimizes impact on normal flora.	de-escalation narrow-spectrum stewardship-principle subject:pulmonology
31ffbef79d6ea0ea	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Optimal Use Of Antibiotics - Antibiotic Stewardship	Why should national guidelines be adapted to local epidemiology?	National guidelines are based on epidemiological data that may not apply to every institution. Resistance patterns vary by region and facility. Using unadapted national guidelines may select for resistant organisms at your facility. Local surveillance data must guide modifications to guidelines to achieve optimal outcomes while managing local resistance patterns.	stewardship-principles local-adaptation guidelines subject:pulmonology
cc9dabc57515d3a8	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Optimal Use Of Antibiotics - Antibiotic Stewardship	What is the clinical evaluation pathway from presentation to appropriate therapy?	1. Patient presents with disease/symptoms 2. Ask: Is this really an infectious disease? 3. Make clinical diagnosis 4. Ask: Infectious or non-infectious problem? 5. Identify likely organisms for that site/diagnosis 6. Collect appropriate samples (blood, urine, CSF, etc) 7. Send specimens to laboratory 8. Start empirical therapy 9. Lab performs microscopy, gram stain, culture, organism ID, susceptibility testing 10. Report results at D2-D3 11. Adjust therapy based on results	clinical-pathway diagnostic-process therapy-initiation subject:pulmonology
dda65ff38486d0e5	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Optimal Use Of Antibiotics - Antibiotic Stewardship	How are antibiotic susceptibilities determined in the laboratory?	Laboratory performs disk diffusion tests (Kirby-Bauer): antibiotic discs are placed on bacterial culture plates. After incubation, inhibition zones are measured. Zones are compared to standard guidelines to classify organisms as sensitive, intermediate, or resistant. This determines which antibiotics are effective against the specific organism.	susceptibility-testing laboratory-methods microbiology subject:pulmonology
c6d27881a0539c2a	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Optimal Use Of Antibiotics - Antibiotic Stewardship	What does a green color indicate on an antibiotic susceptibility disc test?	Green color (or larger inhibition zone) indicates the organism is sensitive to that antibiotic (susceptibility typically greater than 80 percent). Orange/intermediate color indicates partial susceptibility (around 80 percent susceptibility). Red/no zone indicates resistance (the antibiotic is not effective). Full sensitivity is not guaranteed even with new agents.	susceptibility-interpretation laboratory-diagnostics subject:pulmonology
cbbf8b1bda19c34e	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Optimal Use Of Antibiotics - Antibiotic Stewardship	What are the factors affecting choice of antibiotic in uncomplicated vs complicated UTI?	Uncomplicated UTI: typically simple organisms (E. coli), shorter therapy, oral agents usually sufficient. Complicated UTI: may have polymicrobial infections, structural/functional urinary tract abnormalities, requires broader coverage and longer duration. Choice of agent and duration depends on complexity and patient factors.	uti-management disease-specific infection-classification subject:pulmonology
01fed9c4fe0a1a73	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Optimal Use Of Antibiotics - Antibiotic Stewardship	What is the relationship between antibiotic use and antibiotic resistance development?	Every antibiotic use creates selective pressure favoring resistant organisms. Resistance emerges within populations when antibiotics eliminate susceptible bacteria, leaving resistant strains to proliferate. Overuse and misuse accelerate this process. Appropriate stewardship minimizes selective pressure and slows resistance emergence.	resistance-development stewardship-rationale subject:pulmonology
812451e825018f5a	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Optimal Use Of Antibiotics - Antibiotic Stewardship	What are the components of combination antibiotic therapy?	Combination therapy should only be used with clear indications. Unnecessary combination kills all susceptible organisms and creates space for resistant organisms to emerge. Combination therapy is indicated for polymicrobial infections, enhanced coverage when organism is unknown, or specific synergistic effects. Single agent therapy should be used whenever possible.	combination-therapy stewardship antibiotic-selection subject:pulmonology
9b2f5cd605b39987	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Optimal Use Of Antibiotics - Antibiotic Stewardship	What is the bench-to-patient clinical pathway in antimicrobial stewardship?	Bench (Laboratory): microscopy, gram stain, culture, organism identification, susceptibility testing. Patient (Clinic): Initial diagnosis and empirical therapy selection, Re-evaluation at D2-D3 with lab results, therapy adjustment based on susceptibility, clinical monitoring for response and adverse effects.	clinical-pathway laboratory-integration stewardship subject:pulmonology
4906d61d61071d54	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Suppurative Lung Syndromes	What is bronchiectasis?	Bronchiectasis is the abnormal, permanent dilatation of medium and small bronchial trees. It may be focal or diffuse, resulting from transmural inflammation and destruction of the bronchial walls.	bronchiectasis definition lung-pathology subject:pulmonology
80839cacea0694c9	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Suppurative Lung Syndromes	What is the pathology of bronchiectasis?	Transmural inflammation and destruction predominantly affects the walls of medium-sized bronchi. This leads to: mucosal edema, replacement of cartilage/muscle/elastic tissue with fibrous tissue, mucus gland hyperplasia with impaired mucociliary clearance, recurrent bacterial infections, increased bronchial wall vascularity (neovascularization), and risk of hemoptysis.	bronchiectasis pathology mechanisms subject:pulmonology
0e88a7edd91b09c5	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Suppurative Lung Syndromes	What are the main mechanisms leading to bronchiectasis development?	Four main factors: 1) Repeated infective insults 2) Airway obstruction (foreign body or lymph node) 3) Reduced clearance of mucus/materials in airways 4) Defects in host immune defense system. These lead to bacterial toxins, immune response with mediators and oxygen free radicals, progressive destruction, airway colonization, and ongoing destructive changes.	bronchiectasis etiology mechanisms subject:pulmonology
202f841c5e0fb5b3	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Suppurative Lung Syndromes	What are the main etiological categories of bronchiectasis?	1) Airway obstructive causes (innate: bronchomegaly, tracheobranchomegaly; acquired: foreign body aspiration, lymphadenopathy) 2) Defects in host defense (immunoglobulin deficiencies - IgA, IgG; HIV; chronic malnutrition) 3) Impaired drainage (cystic fibrosis, Kartagener syndrome) 4) Secondary to connective tissue disease, alpha-1 antitrypsin deficiency, chronic childhood infections	bronchiectasis etiology causes subject:pulmonology
7abada6458f12b2b	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Suppurative Lung Syndromes	What is Kartagener syndrome and what is the classic triad?	Kartagener syndrome is a ciliary dysfunction disorder. The classic triad consists of: 1) Situs inversus totalis (complete mirror-image reversal of thoracic and abdominal organs) 2) Chronic sinusitis 3) Bronchiectasis.	bronchiectasis kartagener-syndrome etiology subject:pulmonology
267ca797c91ba133	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Suppurative Lung Syndromes	Which etiologies of bronchiectasis are associated with TB?	TB causes bronchiectasis through: 1) Necrotizing effect on lung parenchyma and airways 2) Airway obstruction by fibrosis or lymph node enlargement. Apical or posterior segment abscesses should raise suspicion for TB.	bronchiectasis tuberculosis etiology subject:pulmonology

4ef09a53ef75bfee	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Suppurative Lung Syndromes	What is cystic fibrosis and what is the genetic basis?	Cystic fibrosis is an autosomal recessive disease caused by a mutation in the cystic fibrosis transmembrane regulator (CFTR) gene on chromosome 7. The mutation affects chloride channels, resulting in viscid thick secretions that promote bacterial binding, proliferation, and recurrent infections.	cystic-fibrosis genetics pathophysiology subject:pulmonology

A lung abscess is an infection of the lung parenchyma consisting of one or more necrotic inflammatory cavities that contain fibrino-purulent exudates. It typically presents as a cavity with an air-fluid level on imaging.	lung-abscess definition subject:pulmonology

29cb577cdeb9306c	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Suppurative Lung Syndromes	What is the relationship between patient positioning and aspiration pneumonia location?	Patient positioning determines the location of aspirated material: 1) Supine (face up) → superior segment of lower lobe 2) Prone or lateral position → posterior segment (abdomen). This is because gravity and airway anatomy direct aspirated contents to different lung areas based on position.	lung-abscess aspiration anatomy subject:pulmonology

The presence of an air-fluid level is highly suggestive of lung abscess.	lung-abscess diagnosis imaging subject:pulmonology

Drugs are directed toward anaerobic coverage using: 1) Metronidazole 2) Clindamycin 3) Combinations of such agents 4) Carbapenems. Treatment should continue for appropriate duration until clinical improvement and imaging demonstrates resolution.	lung-abscess treatment antibiotics subject:pulmonology

If not responding well to oral antibiotic treatment, the abscess requires: 1) Postural drainage 2) Bronchoscopic drainage 3) Supportive care with analgesia. 1fb4f167c0a12156	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Suppurative Lung Syndromes	What is empyema?	Empyema is a purulent exudative pleural effusion secondary to pneumonia, lung abscess, or bronchiectasis. It represents bacterial invasion into the pleural space with accumulation of pus.	empyema definition subject:pulmonology
c6a41177afd416f9	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Suppurative Lung Syndromes	What are the risk factors for empyema?	1) Advanced age 2) Recurrent pneumonias 3) Alcohol or drug abuse history 4) Diabetes 5) Poorly controlled GERD (gastroesophageal reflux disease) 6) Poor oral hygiene 7) Immunosuppression 8) Previous pleural intervention	empyema risk-factors subject:pulmonology
b95714c605b43c21	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Suppurative Lung Syndromes	What are the clinical presentations of empyema?	Patients present as ill, toxic-looking individuals with: 1) Fever 2) Parrotic chest pain 3) Increased sputum production 4) History of bronchiectasis, previous lung abscess, recurrent pneumonias, or pulmonary TB	empyema clinical-presentation subject:pulmonology
9d3191f365bc4a2b	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Suppurative Lung Syndromes	What are the physical examination findings in empyema?	1) Medial spine shift 2) Tracheal deviation to the contralateral side (away from empyema) 3) Stony dullness to percussion 4) Reduced breath sounds	empyema physical-examination subject:pulmonology
f0a720e7ac475270	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Suppurative Lung Syndromes	What are the three stages of empyema and their typical durations?	1) Exudative stage (perineumonic effusion): ~2 weeks - normal pH, normal glucose, negative culture, reversible if treated early 2) Fibropurulent stage: 1-6 weeks - bacterial invasion, leukocytosis, low glucose, low pH, lactic acid accumulation 3) Organizing stage: ~2 weeks - fibrin deposit, formation of inelastic membrane, plural thickening, fibrosis	empyema pathophysiology stages subject:pulmonology
3e38082d38d24c77	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Suppurative Lung Syndromes	What characterizes the exudative stage of empyema?	The exudative stage (perineumonic effusion) involves: 1) Bacterial invasion into lung parenchyma causing pneumonia 2) Inflammation leads to reduced visceral pleural membrane permeability 3) Leakage of interstitial fluid into pleural space 4) High level of endogenous fibrinolytic enzymes 5) Laboratory findings: normal pH, normal glucose, negative culture. Early recognition and treatment prevents progression to fibropurulent stage.	empyema pathophysiology exudative-stage subject:pulmonology
3cf8383602eda4db	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Suppurative Lung Syndromes	What characterizes the fibropurulent stage of empyema?	The fibropurulent stage involves: 1) Bacterial transcendence into pleural membrane 2) Progressive leukocytosis and thrombocyte accumulation at pleural surfaces 3) Progressive tendency toward loculation 4) Bacterial and neutrophil phagocytic activity using up glucose, producing lactate 5) LDH rises in the effusion 6) Release of polymorphs and polynuclear cells 7) Laboratory findings: low pH, low glucose. This stage is more difficult to treat than exudative stage.	empyema pathophysiology fibropurulent-stage subject:pulmonology
f95d30d007a53dfa	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Suppurative Lung Syndromes	What characterizes the organizing stage of empyema?	The organizing stage involves: 1) Fibroblasts grow into pleural space forming inelastic membrane 2) PDGF (platelet-derived growth factor) and TGF-beta (transforming growth factor beta) lead to pleural fibrosis 3) Plural thickening develops 4) Thickening inhibits bacterial penetration, preventing antibiotic effectiveness 5) Microbiome biofilm formation reduces antibiotic effectiveness 6) Collagen deposition reduces fibrinolysis effectiveness 7) Surgery becomes essential and inevitable at this stage	empyema pathophysiology organizing-stage subject:pulmonology
d75b8a6be6c8a87c	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Suppurative Lung Syndromes	What imaging findings are seen in empyema?	Chest X-ray shows significant fluid accumulation or complete 'whiteout' of the affected lung with tracheal deviation toward the contralateral side.	empyema diagnosis imaging subject:pulmonology
772ce8c9c76d24ce	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Suppurative Lung Syndromes	What are Light's criteria and how are they applied to empyema?	Light's criteria identify empyema as an exudative effusion. Key findings in empyema: 1) Exudative fusion (elevated LDH, high protein) 2) Low glucose (characteristic finding) 3) Low pH (<7.2) - diagnostic of empyema 4) Predominant cell type: neutrophil-predominant (except in TB empyema which is lymphocyte-predominant)	empyema diagnosis lights-criteria subject:pulmonology

d4ed932ceafc1aa1	Basic	Corpus::Pulmonology::Suppurative Lung Syndromes	Why is intercostal chest drain the treatment of choice for empyema?	Intercostal drainage is the treatment modality of choice because pus accumulates in the pleural space. Drainage via normal pleural aspiration alone will not adequately drain the thick purulent fluid. Without proper drainage, the fluid will loculate, making treatment very difficult. If neglected, loculation occurs and becomes increasingly refractory to medical management.	empyema treatment drainage subject:pulmonology

The inverted pyramid consists of corticosteroids as the base, steroid-sparing agents as the next level, and treatment of refractory disease at the top. 3a99fe192d7e041a	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Gout	What is gout and what causes it?	Gout is a form of immunoarthritis caused by the deposition of monosodium urate (MSU) crystals in joints and surrounding tissues. MSU shedding in a joint leads to recurring episodes of acute inflammatory arthritis.	subject:rheumatology
73f9136c73e64a94	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Gout	What is hyperuricemia and what does it mean?	Hyperuricemia is a serum uric acid concentration above the saturation point (~6.8 mg/dL at body temperature). Clinical consequences include acute uric acid stones and nephropathies.	subject:rheumatology
a86643d6a4d83507	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Gout	What are the primary causes of hyperuricemia?	Primary causes (genetic predisposition) or secondary causes - overproduction of uric acid or under-excretion of uric acid. Under-excretion is the main cause of hyperuricemia.	subject:rheumatology
204b3594121d9ff5	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Gout	What is the male-to-female ratio and age of onset for gout?	Gout is the most common form of immunoarthritis in men. Male-to-female ratio is 2-4:1 in Caucasians and 8:1 in Asians. Peak age is 40-50 years. Very rare in males below age 30 and in women.	subject:rheumatology
8e509619c207018f	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Gout	Do all people with hyperuricemia develop gout?	No. Not everyone with hyperuricemia develops gout. Many people remain asymptomatic despite elevated uric acid levels.	subject:rheumatology
605e7578ee33e83f	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Gout	Where does uric acid come from in the body?	Uric acid comes from protein-based diet. Nucleic acids (adenine, guanine) are converted to hypoxanthine and xanthine, then to uric acid via the enzyme xanthine oxidase.	subject:rheumatology
55e35ac472cf24ae	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Gout	Why do humans accumulate uric acid compared to other primates?	In humans and higher primates, uricase (a hepatic enzyme that converts uric acid to the more soluble allantoin) is inactive due to a non-sense mutation. This prevents conversion of uric acid to the more soluble form, so uric acid accumulates.	subject:rheumatology
e61da8a04d4bfc86	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Gout	How is uric acid eliminated from the body?	Approximately 60-70% of daily uric acid is excreted by the kidneys. About 10% undergoes uricolysis in the gut. The amount of uric acid in the body is the natural result of production minus elimination.	subject:rheumatology
1edd5a59d6e8c037	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Gout	What conditions cause overproduction of uric acid?	High-purine foods, myeloproliferative disorders, psoriasis, and cytotoxic drugs cause overproduction of uric acid.	subject:rheumatology
78739f090ce6a028	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Gout	What conditions and drugs cause under-excretion of uric acid?	Alcohol, renal disease, hypertension, and drugs like diuretics, penicillamine, and thiazide diuretics cause under-excretion of uric acid.	subject:rheumatology
f35ade7f82a41477	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Gout	What is the relationship between uric acid and metabolic syndrome?	There is an association between uric acid and metabolic syndrome (obesity, hypertension, hypertriglyceridemia, cholesterol disease, and diabetes). Reduction in uric acid clearance is associated with these conditions.	subject:rheumatology
59f3e2c85a8bf6a7	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Gout	What are the consequences of asymptomatic hyperuricemia?	A patient with asymptomatic hyperuricemia may remain asymptomatic, develop acute gout attacks, develop tophi, chronic tophaceous gout, or uric acid neuropathy. Only a small percentage develop gout. Risk of kidney stones and kidney damage is low. Monitoring is important.	subject:rheumatology
90f3858e4afeba91	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Gout	What is the typical timing and presentation of an acute gout attack?	Acute gout onset is sudden with acute inflammation. Maximum risk of pain within 12-24 hours. Patients present with signs of acute inflammation that usually resolve in 5-10 days of treatment.	subject:rheumatology
6ae3a63f9c45e913	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Gout	Which joints are involved in gout attacks?	Gout can be monoarticular or polyarticular. About 50% of first attacks involve the first metatarsophalangeal joint. Lower limbs are clearly affected. In 8% of patients in the first attack, other joints like the elbow can be involved.	subject:rheumatology
495a65276973fb82	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Gout	Who typically develops polyarticular gout?	Polyarticular gout is often seen in older patients taking diuretics and in females.	subject:rheumatology
1c649f33249d2099	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Gout	Is uric acid level always elevated during an acute gout attack?	No. Uric acid level may be normal during an acute gout attack. Therefore, diagnosis relies on history and examination. A normal uric acid level does not exclude gout or acute gout.	subject:rheumatology
229b131515a47c7e	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Gout	What are the physical signs of acute gout?	The affected joint is red, warm, and tender. Acute inflammation is evident. The ankle bones or knee to foot can also be affected. The attack is striking with pain and swelling.	subject:rheumatology
f7b020ff12754aab	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Gout	What is chronic tophaceous gout?	Chronic tophaceous gout is widespread deposition of urate in joints, tendons, and cartilage. Tophi develop in about 10 years when hyperuricemia is undiagnosed, untreated, or inadequately managed. Can present in elderly women and may damage bones and joints.	subject:rheumatology
e8aa25531d62123c	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Gout	What do tophi look like and where can they be found?	Tophi contain white/chalky material. They can be seen in the ears, hands, and other joints. X-rays show destruction of bone. Material can discharge resembling chalky particles.	subject:rheumatology
5703f295edf387ab	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Gout	What is the relationship between gout and kidney stones?	Patients may develop nephropathies, including uric acid nephrolithiasis (kidney stones). Risk is related to the level of uric acid and urinary uric acid. Factors favoring stone production include high uric acid, dehydration, reduced urine volume, and reduced pH.	subject:rheumatology
7d927c6521640321	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Gout	What types of kidney disease are associated with gout?	Three types - (1) Uric acid nephrolithiasis (kidney stones), (2) Chronic uric acid nephropathy, (3) Acute uric acid nephropathy (tumor lysis syndrome) due to massive overproduction and chemotherapy.	subject:rheumatology
4d5375eb30b2cfe6	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Gout	What investigations should be done for suspected gout?	Should measure serum uric acid, serum creatinine, and renal function. Check for uric acid in urine. Examine synovial fluid (arthrocentesis) to confirm crystals. Screen for associated conditions and measure blood pressure.	subject:rheumatology
c2828abaf288f339	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Gout	What is the definitive diagnostic test for gout?	Definitive diagnosis is made by demonstration of monosodium urate crystals (needle-shaped) in synovial fluid obtained by joint aspiration. Synovial fluid appearance varies but is often clear or slightly turbid.	subject:rheumatology
4e1a36b69ee7630d	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Gout	What characteristics of synovial fluid suggest sepsis in gout?	The more turbid the synovial fluid, the more likely actual sepsis is present. Most of the time the fluid is clear or slightly cloudy.	subject:rheumatology
e4c82aec0ba071a4	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Gout	How should asymptomatic hyperuricemia be managed?	For asymptomatic hyperuricemia - monitor patients seriously without treatment. 7b28a12357f0f608	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Gout	What is the first-line acute management of gout?	First-line is NSAIDs, but must be used with caution. Avoid in active ulcers and renal insufficiency. 5d798a891c310d32	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Gout	What is the typical colchicine dosing regimen? 5c920cb3c9be54df	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Gout	Should urate-lowering therapy be started during an acute gout attack?	Urate-lowering therapy should not be initiated during acute attack due to chemotaxis concerns or prolonged attack. 23dbedaae325ba67	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Gout	What is allopurinol and how does it work?	Allopurinol is a xanthine oxidase inhibitor that acts on the last pathway converting xanthine to uric acid. 5c09f031cdc0e529	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Gout	How do uricosuric agents work?	Uricosuric agents increase uric acid excretion by the kidneys. They work by blocking uric acid reuptake in kidney tubules, increasing urinary excretion of uric acid.	subject:rheumatology

Opioids such as propoxyphene and codeine are for severe refractory cases. However, they should be used with caution as they have high propensity for abuse and dependency.	subject:rheumatology

Tricyclic antidepressants such as amitriptyline are used at low doses for nocturnal sedation. Antidepressants are needed because chronic pain is often associated with depression.	subject:rheumatology
5a9316de53d22e31	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Neck Pain  Back Pain	What are the goals of exercise in neck pain management?	The two principal aims are to restore normal range of movement and strengthen neck musculature. Exercise helps prevent acute exacerbations.	subject:rheumatology

d0d037e7c72281b0	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Neck Pain  Back Pain	What is the role of manipulation in neck pain?	Manipulative or chiropractic manipulation aims to correct vertebral subluxation, stretch tight joint capsules, and mobilization through oscillatory movements may have small benefit.	subject:rheumatology

83b61c902644b054	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Neck Pain  Back Pain	What is the natural history of cervical nerve root compression?	The majority of cases resolve in a few weeks to months. However, less than 10% have persisting moderate or severe disability.	subject:rheumatology

No long-term difference exists between cervical collar, physiotherapy, or surgery. The choice hinges upon minimizing harm.	subject:rheumatology

f883d8d405c0b1d3	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Neck Pain  Back Pain	What is the definition of low back pain?	Low back pain is pain localized below the line of the 12th rib and above the inferior gluteal folds, with or without leg pain.	subject:rheumatology
68043460c46bd68f	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Neck Pain  Back Pain	How is low back pain classified?	Low back pain may be specific (identifiable pathological cause) or non-specific (no known disease or pathogenic mechanism; anatomical source obscure).	subject:rheumatology
0ca6c82f3ed35221	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Neck Pain  Back Pain	What is the prevalence of low back pain?	Lifetime prevalence is 75-85%. Yearly prevalence in USA is 15-20% and in Europe is 25-45%. In UK, 5.5-7.5% of GP consultations are for low back pain.	subject:rheumatology
0794822941ebecb9	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Neck Pain  Back Pain	What is the natural history of work absence due to back pain?	Only 50% of patients off work for more than six months return to work. Almost nobody returns to work after two years of absence.	subject:rheumatology
51f40263f227158d	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Neck Pain  Back Pain	What are the occupational risk factors for back pain?	Increased association with heavy physical work, static work posture, and psychosocial factors. Highest risk in nurses and drivers.	subject:rheumatology
cd7457a3b41c4ab4	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Neck Pain  Back Pain	What is the recurrence rate of back pain?	Lifetime recurrence is up to 85%. In Sweden, one-year recurrence was 44%. In Canada, one-year recurrence was 20% and three-year recurrence was 36%.	subject:rheumatology
7983778f19409654	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Neck Pain  Back Pain	What is the impact of back pain on work loss?	Back pain is a major cause of sick leave. Average sick days in 1983-84 were 28.9 days, increasing to 105.8 days in 1993-94.	subject:rheumatology
ae1ad23ea1e909d3	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Neck Pain  Back Pain	What is the relationship between chronic back pain and psychiatric symptoms?	Chronic back pain is often associated with psychiatric symptoms (depression, substance abuse, anxiety, personality disorders) that often precede the onset of back pain.	subject:rheumatology
f25918328f36b31e	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Neck Pain  Back Pain	What are key epidemiological facts about back pain?	Back pain affects two-thirds of adults. It is second only to upper respiratory tract infection in frequency. Affects men and women equally. Most common between 30-50 years. It is an expensive cause of work-related disability.	subject:rheumatology
68299729a31a4a3f	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Neck Pain  Back Pain	What percentage of back pain is mechanical and what are the causes?	90% of low back pain is mechanical and due to muscular ligamentous injuries, age-related degeneration of intervertebral discs and facet joints, spinal stenosis, disc herniation, osteoporotic compression fractures, spondylosis, and spondylolysis.	subject:rheumatology
b742ae0da4a6b4f9	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Neck Pain  Back Pain	What is the natural history and spontaneous recovery rate of back pain?	50% are better after one week. More than 90% are better at 8 weeks. Only 7-10% of back pain persists beyond 6 months.	subject:rheumatology
9bfa38ded5614c69	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Neck Pain  Back Pain	What are the medical causes of low back pain?	Medical causes include spondyloarthropathies, spinal infections, osteoporosis, malignancy, and referred pain from pelvic structures, renal structures, aortic aneurysm, and pancreatitis.	subject:rheumatology
e545eaa6f221f1cc	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Neck Pain  Back Pain	What are the key clinical questions in back pain evaluation?	Is systemic disease causing the pain? 49774539347761f6	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Neck Pain  Back Pain	What is the general approach to back pain management?	Management involves watchful waiting. Patient education is important. Spontaneous recovery is the rule. Those who remain active despite pain have less future chronic pain. Patients should rest for 2 days or less. Reassess if pain worsens or neurological symptoms develop.	subject:rheumatology
1f0de826242bb7d0	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Neck Pain  Back Pain	What is the role of exercise in back pain?	Exercise has prevention power and helps prevent recurrences and treat chronic pain. However, exercise is not useful for the acute phase.	subject:rheumatology
c7e63981f49cb6db	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Neck Pain  Back Pain	Why should imaging studies be used cautiously in back pain?	Imaging studies can be misleading. Many abnormalities are common in pain-free individuals. In people under 60, there is low yield for unexpected x-ray findings.	subject:rheumatology
8576b03637bc110f	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Neck Pain  Back Pain	How common are abnormal disc findings in asymptomatic individuals?	In asymptomatic individuals under 60: spondylosis in 1 of 2,500, bulging discs in 1 of 3 patients, herniated discs in 1 of 5 patients. Over 60: herniated discs in 1 of 3, bulging discs in 80%, disc and apophysial joint degeneration in all, spinal stenosis in 1 of 5 cases.	subject:rheumatology

Red flags include fever, weight loss, pain worse when lying down and worse at night, morning stiffness, pain lasting more than 6 weeks, age over 50 with new onset pain, abnormal neurological signs, and point tenderness.	subject:rheumatology

Goal is to discriminate between benign causes and disorders requiring diagnostic studies. Diagnostic studies include x-rays, CT scans, and MRI. Useful lab tests include full blood count, ESR, calcium, alkaline phosphatase, and protein electrophoresis.	subject:rheumatology

Herniated disc, spinal stenosis, cauda equina syndrome, inflammatory spondyloarthropathy, spinal infection, vertebral fracture, malignancy, and referred visceral pain.	subject:rheumatology

3ec11afe41c9701c	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Neck Pain  Back Pain	How do osteoporotic compression fractures appear on x-ray?	X-rays show multiple wedge fractures with markedly reduced vertebral body density.	subject:rheumatology
422b0a16a8213f8d	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Neck Pain  Back Pain	What are the red flags for spinal malignancy?	Pain is worse at night, often associated with localized bone thinness. Blood count, ESR, and protein electrophoresis would be abnormal with elevated ESR. Abnormal x-ray findings should raise suspicion.	subject:rheumatology
bcddb37f67dad433	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Neck Pain  Back Pain	When is surgical referral indicated for sciatica? 82168bd4a2be7111	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Neck Pain  Back Pain	What are the indicators for surgical intervention in back pain?	Indicators include debility (inability to perform activities of daily living and functional impairment) and unbearable pain.	subject:rheumatology
3af212d5542926e8	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Neck Pain  Back Pain	What are the key clinical points about low back pain?	90% are due to mechanical causes and resolve spontaneously within 6 weeks to 6 months. Diagnostic workup is needed for red flags. ESR elevation warrants evaluation for malignancy or infection. Initial x-rays are beneficial in older patients. Rest is not recommended. Return to normal activities is most beneficial. Back exercises prevent recurrences and treat chronic pain. Surgery is appropriate for small proportion of patients.	subject:rheumatology

1e05f7fd35ae08bb	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Osteoarthritis	What is the primary characteristic of osteoarthritis pathology?	Focal softening and disintegration of articular cartilage accompanied by proliferation of new bone (osteophyte formation on joint margins).	subject:rheumatology
bee43461ce81318d	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Osteoarthritis	What are the three main x-ray findings in osteoarthritis?	1) Joint space narrowing, 2) Subchondral sclerosis, 3) Marginal osteophyte formation.	subject:rheumatology
4c4a31fdb1c9b334	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Osteoarthritis	What is the role of the collagen network in cartilage?	The collagen network maintains cartilage strength and integrity while preserving water content and hydration of the cartilage.	subject:rheumatology
c8650d6a86046d32	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Osteoarthritis	What happens when the collagen network is disrupted?	Cartilage integrity is compromised, leading to erosion, damage, dehydration, and microfractures in the subchondral bone.	subject:rheumatology
994da80700b2f6d8	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Osteoarthritis	What is the blood supply source for cartilage nutrients?	Cartilage is avascular (non-vascular) and receives nutrients from the vascular supply of the subchondral bone.	subject:rheumatology
9ae60fbb6c713f08	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Osteoarthritis	What stimuli trigger the pathogenic cascade in osteoarthritis?	Trauma, mechanical injury, or metabolic factors that stress the cartilage and affect chondrocytes.	subject:rheumatology
06f08bab030b5bde	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Osteoarthritis	What do damaged chondrocytes produce?	Enzymes that break down cartilage and collagen network, plus alkaline phosphatases that further damage cartilage integrity.	subject:rheumatology
c4bf8e63c3e4d92c	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Osteoarthritis	What is the primary function of cartilage in joints?	To act as a shock absorber, with synovial fluid lining the joint to prevent excessive stress and redistribute forces.	subject:rheumatology
1dfa6a036b608f28	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Osteoarthritis	What structures are involved in osteoarthritic joint damage?	Joint capsule (thickening), cartilage (fibrillation), synovium (hypertrophy), and subchondral bone (sclerosis, cyst formation).	subject:rheumatology
44609a457c4274b5	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Osteoarthritis	What causes synovial hypertrophy in osteoarthritis?	Inflammatory infiltrate within the synovium and fluid buildup, leading to stretching of the joint capsule.	subject:rheumatology

7c4ee465b838bf6f	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Osteoarthritis	What is radiographic osteoarthritis?	Incidental x-ray findings of osteoarthritis in asymptomatic patients showing features like joint space narrowing and osteophytes.	subject:rheumatology
07aff98ab116cb95	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Osteoarthritis	What percentage of adults in the US have radiographic hand OA?	32.5% of adults in the US have radiographic hand osteoarthritis.	subject:rheumatology

5773ab5556118a6f	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Osteoarthritis	How does pain in osteoarthritis differ from inflammatory arthritis pain?	OA pain is non-inflammatory, worse with movement and activity, and improves with rest (unlike inflammatory pain which improves with movement).	subject:rheumatology

4f6aaa51b61ad585	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Osteoarthritis	What is the relationship between obesity and osteoarthritis risk?	BMI greater than 30 increases osteoarthritis risk by 4 fold; losing 5 kg can reduce knee OA risk by up to 50% in those with BMI greater than 25.	subject:rheumatology

8281ed9098df0b29	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Osteoarthritis	What is the relationship between osteoporosis and osteoarthritis?	Strong inverse relationship: if a patient has osteoarthritis, they are less likely to have osteoporosis and vice versa.	subject:rheumatology

Rheumatoid arthritis is an autoimmune disorder characterized by chronic symmetrical inflammatory polyarthritis (inflammation of multiple joints) that may be associated with extraarticular manifestations.	subject:rheumatology
3f5ea220f6c2a952	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Rheumatoid Arthritis	What is the global prevalence of rheumatoid arthritis?	The global prevalence is around 1%, though it is lower in developing countries in Asia and Africa. 11329bbdc7007eeb	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Rheumatoid Arthritis	What is the typical age of onset for rheumatoid arthritis?	The peak age of onset is between 35 to 55 years.	subject:rheumatology
d9a76480fc493588	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Rheumatoid Arthritis	What is the female to male ratio in rheumatoid arthritis?	Rheumatoid arthritis is more common in women, with a female to male ratio of approximately 2-3 to 1.	subject:rheumatology
4d650df04c37c67d	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Rheumatoid Arthritis	Which joints are typically affected in rheumatoid arthritis?	RA characteristically affects joints symmetrically, initially beginning in smaller joints and commonly affecting the wrist, hands (metacarpophalangeal and proximal interphalangeal joints), shoulders, knees, and ankles.	subject:rheumatology
b1958fc494f0a887	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Rheumatoid Arthritis	What are the main genetic factors associated with RA?	The major genetic factors include HLA-DR4 (which shows the strongest association and is present in 70% of Caucasians with RA), HLA-DQ2, and other susceptibility genes. HLA-DR4 is found in about 25% of healthy Caucasian individuals.	subject:rheumatology
721d14d5c6d27630	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Rheumatoid Arthritis	What are the main environmental risk factors for developing RA?	Environmental risk factors include periodontal infections, smoking (associated with increased risk and greater disease severity), hormonal changes with increased prevalence in females and postpartum presentation, and dysbiosis of the gut microbiome.	subject:rheumatology
453d682a1939056b	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Rheumatoid Arthritis	How does smoking affect the risk of rheumatoid arthritis?	Smoking is associated with both increased risk of developing RA and greater disease severity.	subject:rheumatology
027a77b59bfef84a	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Rheumatoid Arthritis	What protective factors reduce the risk of RA?	Alcohol consumption and later age at menarche have protective effects, though moderate alcohol consumption is still recommended due to potential liver metabolism issues with RA medications.	subject:rheumatology
3d8db1a3712dd6fc	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Rheumatoid Arthritis	What is synovitis in rheumatoid arthritis?	Synovitis is inflammation of the synovial membrane, which becomes infiltrated with inflammatory cells including lymphocytes, plasma cells, dendritic cells, and macrophages. The synovial lining cells become hypertrophied and there is proliferation of fibroblast tissue with destruction of cartilage and bone erosion.	subject:rheumatology
e99d6a79b2e8f856	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Rheumatoid Arthritis	What are rheumatoid nodules?	Rheumatoid nodules are subcutaneous fibronecrotic or mobile masses found in subcutaneous tissue, typically on the extensor surface of the hands and elbows, though they can also be found on the lungs and heart.	subject:rheumatology
bc92d07e706b7791	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Rheumatoid Arthritis	What is the role of T cells in rheumatoid arthritis pathophysiology?	T helper cells (Th1 and Th17) become activated and produce pro-inflammatory cytokines (TNF-alpha, IL-1, IL-6, RANKL). These helper T cells also activate B cells, leading to production of autoantibodies such as rheumatoid factor and anti-CCP (anti-cyclic citrullinated peptide) antibodies.	subject:rheumatology
a77082e9b89b9b5f	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Rheumatoid Arthritis	What is the role of B cells in rheumatoid arthritis?	B cells, when activated by T helper cells, produce autoantibodies including rheumatoid factor and anti-cyclic citrullinated peptide (anti-CCP) antibodies, which form immune complexes that activate macrophages and the complement system, resulting in inflammation.	subject:rheumatology
691fe194e6c68e90	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Rheumatoid Arthritis	What cytokines are key in the pathophysiology of rheumatoid arthritis?	The key pro-inflammatory cytokines are TNF-alpha, interleukin-1 (IL-1), interleukin-6 (IL-6), and RANKL (receptor activator of nuclear factor-kappa B ligand). These mediate inflammation and bone destruction.	subject:rheumatology
d32b9546a2d499ac	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Rheumatoid Arthritis	What is the role of RANKL in bone destruction in RA?	RANKL (receptor activator of nuclear factor-kappa B ligand), produced by fibroblasts, initiates differentiation of osteoclasts from macrophages. Together with fibroblasts that produce matrix metalloproteinases (MMPs), osteoclasts mediate inflammation-dependent cartilage and bone destruction.	subject:rheumatology
95d8766762a6696f	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Rheumatoid Arthritis	What are matrix metalloproteinases (MMPs) and what is their role in RA?	MMPs are enzymes produced by synovial fibroblasts that are activated by pro-inflammatory cytokines. They are responsible for degradation of cartilage and bone in rheumatoid arthritis.	subject:rheumatology
b9b9c919c382326b	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Rheumatoid Arthritis	How long can the prodromal phase of RA last?	The process of RA development from initial genetic and environmental interactions to symptom onset can take up to 50 years before a person develops symptoms.	subject:rheumatology
e489844e79a5b399	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Rheumatoid Arthritis	What is the significance of synovitis lasting more than 6 weeks?	Synovitis or arthritis lasting more than 6 weeks is a criterion used to diagnose established rheumatoid arthritis.	subject:rheumatology

Approximately 30% of patients with early/undifferentiated arthritis may progress to established RA, while others may remit spontaneously or develop other connective tissue diseases.	subject:rheumatology
2ca685c41ace8189	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Rheumatoid Arthritis	What are the main symptoms of rheumatoid arthritis?	Main symptoms include joint pain, swelling, and morning stiffness (worse in the morning, significantly improved with movement/activity). c194b99b37386519	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Rheumatoid Arthritis	What clinical signs are found on examination of RA patients?	Clinical signs include swelling of joints, joint tenderness, muscle wasting (related to chronic inflammation), joint deformity, and limitation of range of movement.	subject:rheumatology
321a449e8e71db89	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Rheumatoid Arthritis	What is cervical subluxation in RA and what are its dangers?	Cervical subluxation (typically at C1-C2 joint) occurs when RA causes erosion of the transverse ligament. This can lead to spinal instability and compression of the spinal cord (myelopathy), which is now less common due to disease-modifying therapy.	subject:rheumatology
899f0cf7f0249d49	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Rheumatoid Arthritis	What symptoms suggest cervical myelopathy in RA?	Symptoms include neck pain radiating to the occiput, pain radiating to shoulders and hands, numbness, loss of hand grip strength, exaggerated reflexes (or absent reflexes), and weakness depending on the level of cord compression.	subject:rheumatology
5f39f60bd0b6a711	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Rheumatoid Arthritis	What are the shoulder manifestations of RA?	RA can cause rotator cuff tendinitis, adhesive capsulitis (frozen shoulder), and limitation of movements including abduction, external rotation, flexion, and extension.	subject:rheumatology
a0f273e2f37066fd	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Rheumatoid Arthritis	What causes lateral epicondylitis and medial epicondylitis in RA?	Lateral epicondylitis (tennis elbow) is caused by movement at the elbow during external rotation; medial epicondylitis (golfer's elbow) is caused by inward movement at the elbow.	subject:rheumatology

The piano key sign is a deformity of the distal radioulnar joint where the styloid process is prominent and can be compressed (like pressing a piano key), indicating erosion and instability of this joint.	subject:rheumatology
649767e377da5536	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Rheumatoid Arthritis	What hand deformities are characteristic of RA?	Characteristic deformities include swan neck deformity (hyperextension of PIP with flexion of DIP joint), boutonniere deformity (flexion of PIP with hyperextension of DIP), subluxation of MCP joints with shortening of fingers, muscle atrophy of dorsal muscles, and swelling of wrist and hand joints.	subject:rheumatology
5217e77129e0cc7b	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Rheumatoid Arthritis	What is swan neck deformity and what causes it?	Swan neck deformity shows hyperextension of the PIP (proximal interphalangeal) joint and flexion of the DIP (distal interphalangeal) joint, caused by relaxation of the PIP flexor and central slip tendon problems with lateral band migration.	subject:rheumatology
875714c84a2f5404	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Rheumatoid Arthritis	What is boutonniere deformity?	Boutonniere deformity consists of flexion at the PIP (proximal interphalangeal) joint and hyperextension at the DIP (distal interphalangeal) joint, typically caused by central slip disruption.	subject:rheumatology

The Phalen test involves flexing the wrist to vertical position for up to 60 seconds. A positive result (pain in the lateral fingers) suggests carpal tunnel syndrome, a complication of RA.	subject:rheumatology

e08fbc57eae28451	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Rheumatoid Arthritis	What is metatarsalgia in RA?	Metatarsalgia is pain in the metatarsal area, commonly caused by subluxation of MTP joints. Patients describe it as walking on pebbles.	subject:rheumatology

38e6bb50b5965aba	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::rheumatoid-arthritis-pages-011-018	How are rheumatoid arthritis classification criteria scored?	Classification criteria score based on: number of joints involved (higher score for more smaller joints), serology (positive RF/anti-CCP = higher points), and symptom duration (≥6 weeks = higher points). Seropositive RA (positive RF or anti-CCP) has higher risk of progression and development of extra-articular manifestations compared to seronegative RA. DAS28 (Disease Activity Score 28) is used to assess disease activity in RA by evaluating 28 specific joints: 10 on each hand (MCPs and PIPs), 2 wrists, 2 shoulders, 2 elbows, and 2 knees. Goals are to relieve pain and inflammation, prevent reversible joint damage, preserve/improve function, improve quality of life, and alter the natural history of disease. Physical therapy aims to relieve pain, control inflammation, preserve/improve function, and prevent deformities. 76d147e6bf12dd88	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Spondyloarthropathies	What is sacroiliitis and how is it related to SpA?	Sacroiliitis is inflammation of the sacroiliac joints, with or without spondylitis. It is a hallmark feature of SpA and can progress from early inflammation to erosions, sclerosis, and eventual fusion of the joints.	SpA sacroiliitis pathology subject:rheumatology
f0af18131f751922	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Spondyloarthropathies	What is the ASAS classification criteria for axial spondyloarthropathies?	Entry criteria: Back pain with onset less than 45 years, chronic (3+ months). Classification requires either: (1) Sacroiliitis on imaging plus 1+ SpA feature, OR (2) HLA B27 positive plus 2+ SpA features. SpA features include inflammatory back pain, arthritis, enthesitis, uveitis, dactylitis, psoriasis, Crohn's or colitis, good response to NSAIDs, family history, HLA B27 positivity, and elevated CRP.	SpA ASAS-criteria classification subject:rheumatology
2c9d4ac01d87bafe	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Spondyloarthropathies	What are the key characteristics of inflammatory back pain in SpA?	Inflammatory back pain: (1) Occurs in patients less than 40-45 years, (2) Insidious onset, (3) Chronic (3+ months), (4) Morning stiffness upon arising, (5) Pain improves during the day or with exercise, (6) Does not improve with rest, (7) Often associated with nocturnal pain and waking at night.	SpA inflammatory-back-pain clinical-features subject:rheumatology
5e0c9dbfb4bf167e	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Spondyloarthropathies	What is the significance of HLA B27 in spondyloarthropathies?	HLA B27 is a major genetic predisposition marker for SpA with greater than 90% association with ankylosing spondylitis, 85% with reactive arthritis, 50% with inflammatory bowel disease-related SpA, and 50% with psoriatic arthritis spondylitis. However, only 2% of HLA B27 positive individuals develop ankylosing spondylitis; prevalence rises to approximately 20% if both the patient and a first-degree relative are HLA B27 positive. In South Africa: Caucasians 8.56%, African population 4%, Colored population 3.3%, Indian population 2.2%. This ethnic variation affects the prevalence and clinical presentation of SpA in different populations.	SpA HLA-B27 epidemiology subject:rheumatology
532bea940ca1adf4	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Spondyloarthropathies	What is the role of HLA B27 testing in SpA diagnosis?	HLA B27 testing: (1) Assists in clinical diagnosis when clinical suspicion exists, (2) NOT a routine screening or confirmatory test, (3) NOT a screening test in the general population, (4) Useful in appropriate clinical settings with high specificity for certain diseases like ankylosing spondylitis, (5) Cannot distinguish between different types of SpA, (6) Prevalence is relatively low in general population. The criteria assess: (1) History of inflammatory back pain, (2) Limitation of motion of the lumbar spine (extension, flexion, and bending forward), (3) Chest expansion relative to standard values for age and sex (normal approximately 5cm), (4) Definite sacroiliitis on X-ray. Characteristic serology in SpA: (1) Negative rheumatoid factor, (2) Negative anti-nuclear antibodies, (3) No rheumatoid nodules, (4) May have elevated CRP, (5) HLA B27 positive in many cases. 199249d80f3e6814	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Spondyloarthropathies	What is the first-line drug therapy for SpA?	First-line therapy is non-steroidal anti-inflammatory drugs (NSAIDs), either intermittent or continuous. a2c4cdcbfa89b781	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Spondyloarthropathies	What are the disease-modifying therapies used in SpA?	Conventional DMARDs like sulfasalazine and methotrexate are mainly used for peripheral arthritis treatment but have limited efficacy for axial joint involvement. 0c40177cb8827866	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Spondyloarthropathies	What are the clinical features of psoriatic arthritis?	Psoriatic arthritis: (1) Inflammatory arthritis, (2) Involves distal interphalangeal (DIP) joints (distinguishes from rheumatoid arthritis), (3) Often asymmetrical, (4) Associated with dactylitis (sausage digits), (5) Nail involvement with nail-pitting and onycholysis, (6) No nodules, (7) Negative rheumatoid factor.	psoriatic-arthritis clinical-features subject:rheumatology
d2434d770451d228	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Spondyloarthropathies	What are the X-ray findings in psoriatic arthritis? f9b20e0d6a73094a	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Spondyloarthropathies	What are the patterns of joint involvement in psoriatic arthritis?	Five patterns: (1) Axial joint involvement, (2) Distal interphalangeal joint arthritis, (3) Oligo-articular (4 or fewer joints) asymmetrical arthritis, (4) Small joint symmetrical polyarticular pattern, (5) Arthritis mutilans (deforming arthritis).	psoriatic-arthritis patterns classification subject:rheumatology
3ad698a82ad53e87	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Spondyloarthropathies	What is the treatment approach for psoriatic arthritis?	Treatment: (1) NSAIDs for pain and inflammation, (2) Sulfasalazine and methotrexate for peripheral arthritis, (3) Anti-TNF therapies for dactylitis, enthesitis, and axial involvement, (4) Newer biologics: Interleukin-23 inhibitors (ustekinumab) and Interleukin-17 inhibitors (secukinumab).	psoriatic-arthritis treatment biologics subject:rheumatology
a0bacdd713f0a9a4	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Spondyloarthropathies	What is reactive arthritis and how does it differ from septic arthritis?	Reactive arthritis is a spondylarthropathy following a urogenital or gastrointestinal infection. Reactive arthritis is immunologically mediated inflammation of joints following distant infection without organism in the joint; Septic arthritis is direct infection within the joint itself.	reactive-arthritis definition pathophysiology subject:rheumatology

Common triggering organisms include: (1) Chlamydia, (2) Yersinia, (3) Salmonella. The classic triad consists of: (1) Ocular involvement (conjunctivitis), (2) Arthritis, (3) Urethritis. This triad is the hallmark presentation of reactive arthritis.	reactive-arthritis clinical-presentation triad subject:rheumatology
a99b27344d851166	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Spondyloarthropathies	What are the mucocutaneous manifestations of reactive arthritis?	Mucocutaneous manifestations include: (1) Keratoderma blennorrhagicum (papulosquamous rash affecting palms and soles), (2) Nail dystrophy, (3) Circinate balanitis (shallow ulcers on glans or shaft of penis), (4) Oral ulcers (typically painless on hard palate or tongue).	reactive-arthritis mucocutaneous-manifestations skin-features subject:rheumatology
1de4f47051b6714d	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Spondyloarthropathies	What is the treatment for chlamydia-induced reactive arthritis?	Chlamydia-induced reactive arthritis requires prolonged antibiotic treatment with agents such as tetracyclines, doxycycline, or erythromycin. This reduces post-chlamydia arthritis from 37% to 10%.	reactive-arthritis chlamydia treatment antibiotics subject:rheumatology
254b583e2ce7618b	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Spondyloarthropathies	What is enteropathic spondylarthropathy?	Enteropathic spondylarthropathy is arthritis accompanying inflammatory bowel diseases (Crohn's disease and ulcerative colitis). Arthritis occurs in 10-22% of IBD patients, with higher prevalence in Crohn's disease than ulcerative colitis.	enteropathic-arthritis IBD inflammatory-bowel-disease subject:rheumatology
14dfd7e47d3f9b8e	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Spondyloarthropathies	What is the temporal relationship between arthritis and gastrointestinal symptoms?	Arthritis may occur before gastrointestinal symptoms by a lengthy period, initially classified as undifferentiated spondylarthropathy until inflammatory bowel disease becomes apparent later. Arthritis typically presents as prosarticular (few joint involvement), asymmetric, involving one side of the body.	enteropathic-arthritis timing clinical-presentation subject:rheumatology
7a5454a99e3279f2	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Spondyloarthropathies	What is the relationship between enteropathic arthritis activity and bowel inflammation?	The activity of peripheral arthritis generally correlates well with the degree of active bowel inflammation, particularly in ulcerative colitis. If the patient has active bowel disease, they will also have swollen and tender joints.	enteropathic-arthritis correlation disease-activity subject:rheumatology

Non-articular complications include: (1) Skin manifestations: pyoderma gangrenosum, erythema nodosum, (2) Eye manifestations: anterior uveitis, (3) Associated features of inflammatory bowel disease.	enteropathic-arthritis extra-articular-complications systemic-features subject:rheumatology
40134ebc497ed2a7	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Spondyloarthropathies	What is the treatment approach for enteropathic spondylarthropathy?	Treatment: (1) NSAIDs for axial pain and lower back pain (use with caution in IBD), (2) Sulfasalazine for peripheral arthritis, (3) Steroids for peripheral arthritis, (4) Anti-TNF drugs (particularly infliximab) benefit both arthritis and inflammatory bowel disease.	enteropathic-arthritis treatment NSAIDs anti-TNF subject:rheumatology
64c67d9293c2d198	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Spondyloarthropathies	What are the spinal manifestations of enteropathic spondylarthropathy?	Spinal manifestations include syndesmophytes (bony bridges) that cross from one vertebral body to another, extending on either side of vertebrae, eventually progressing to involve more vertebral bodies and resulting in complete fusion and stiffening of the spine.	enteropathic-arthritis spinal-involvement imaging subject:rheumatology
bdf3a6a4d5294e41	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Spondyloarthropathies	What is the difference between mechanical and inflammatory back pain?	Mechanical back pain is differentiated from inflammatory back pain in SpA by: (1) Age of onset (inflammatory less than 40-45 years), (2) Insidious vs acute onset, (3) Chronic duration (3+ months), (4) Morning stiffness pattern and improvement with activity, (5) Nocturnal pain, (6) No improvement with rest.	back-pain differentiation clinical-assessment subject:rheumatology
16961d054d30503f	Basic	Corpus::Rheumatology::Spondyloarthropathies	What are the vertebral erosive changes seen in SpA?	Early erosive changes at the corners of vertebral bodies are associated with SpA, with formation of syndesmophytes at the edge of vertebral bodies, progressing to bridge adjacent vertebrae and eventually resulting in bamboo spine with complete vertebral fusion.	SpA vertebral-pathology imaging subject:rheumatology
